b'Times Insider'
b'By Tyler Kepner'
b'Times Insider explains who we are and what we do, and delivers behind-the-scenes insights into how our journalism comes together.'
b'This is my 20th year as a baseball writer for The New York Times, and to commemorate the occasion, I built a house.'
b'Not literally, of course. I\xe2\x80\x99m the least handy person you\xe2\x80\x99ll ever meet. But as a writer I sometimes think in analogies, and in this case I\xe2\x80\x99m a carpenter. I build hundreds of tables and bookshelves and cabinets each year, taking pride in crafting something stable and sturdy. But I\xe2\x80\x99ve always wanted to apply those skills to something grander. I\xe2\x80\x99ve always wanted to build a house.'
b'As The Times\xe2\x80\x99s national baseball writer, I\xe2\x80\x99ll generate hundreds of articles each year in the 800- to 1,200-word range. A few might reach 2,000. But what about something truly long-lasting: 112,000 words, sold in stores, shelved in libraries, with my name on the spine? What if I built ... a book?'
b'Now I\xe2\x80\x99ve written one, called \xe2\x80\x9cK: A History of Baseball in Ten Pitches,\xe2\x80\x9d released by Doubleday on April 2. It\xe2\x80\x99s the culmination of a three-year scavenger hunt across the baseball landscape, locating more than 300 interview subjects to ask about the pitches that make up the game\xe2\x80\x99s DNA.'
b'The book devotes a chapter apiece to the fastball, the curveball, the sinker, the slider, the cutter, the changeup, the splitter, the screwball, the knuckleball and the spitball. To write it, I had to find a different voice than the one in the newspaper, one of assurance and authority. To be comfortable doing that, I relied on the tools I use to write daily columns and features: talking to as many people as possible, and always learning to try something new.'
b'My mission was to find every pitcher who threw any pitch especially well, plus catchers, hitters, coaches, umpires, scouts and so on \xe2\x80\x94 and, because the book would cover the scope of baseball history, to make sure to get voices of the past, too.'
b'I spent several days engrossed in ancient news clippings at the Hall of Fame library in Cooperstown, N.Y. I leaned on players I covered in my dozen years as a beat writer (at The Times and on the West Coast), reconnecting with pitchers I covered: Mariano Rivera, Andy Pettitte, Roger Clemens, Mike Myers, Al Leiter, Jamie Moyer, Chuck Finley \xe2\x80\x94 and especially Mike Mussina, the new Hall of Famer, who invited me to lunch in his hometown, Montoursville, Pa. We talked about pitching for hours.'
b'In all, I interviewed 22 Hall of Fame pitchers, plus many standouts who made a huge impact in the game but do not have a plaque in Cooperstown: guys like Carl Erskine, Roger Craig, Elroy Face, Wilbur Wood, J. R. Richard, Kent Tekulve, Ron Guidry, Dave Duncan, Mario Soto, Brad Lidge and many more.'
b'I also tracked down pitchers whose feats I witnessed in my formative days as a fan near Philadelphia: the Hall of Famer Steve Carlton, my first baseball hero; Steve Rogers, the longtime Expo who beat Carlton in the first game I ever saw, in 1981; and Scott McGregor, who clinched the 1983 World Series for the Orioles as I watched, forlornly, from the front row at old Veterans Stadium.'
b'Carlton was famous for never talking to the news media, but he was delightful in our 40-minute chat. He greeted my call with a playful brushback pitch: \xe2\x80\x9cSo you\xe2\x80\x99re writing a book \xe2\x80\x94 don\xe2\x80\x99t you know people don\xe2\x80\x99t read anymore?\xe2\x80\x9d But I hung in there and learned all about the pitch that might have been the best slider in the history of the game.'
b'Rogers, whom I found at an All-Star Game in Cincinnati, was a riot; he shared the advice of an early pitching coach, Calvin Coolidge Julius Caesar Tuskahoma McLish \xe2\x80\x94 Cal, for short \xe2\x80\x94 who told young pitchers, \xe2\x80\x9cAll right, son, just get out in front and get whippy!\xe2\x80\x9d Quite a contrast to today\xe2\x80\x99s coaches, who calculate every revolution of the ball with precise technological backing.'
b'McGregor, whose name I cursed as a vanquished 8-year-old Phillies fan, was also a treat to interview, reflecting not just on his moment of glory but also on his final day in the majors, in 1988, when he knew he was finished. The Twins were lashing his lifeless pitches, and the Orioles\xe2\x80\x99 shortstop, Cal Ripken Jr., asked McGregor on the mound if he was still throwing his changeup.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re all the same speed anymore,\xe2\x80\x9d McGregor told Ripken. \xe2\x80\x9cJust back up a little bit. You might be a little safer.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'You\xe2\x80\x99ll find that story on Page 207. I like to pick up the book and flip to a random page like that, to remember the moments of discovery behind every paragraph. For me, it\xe2\x80\x99s like roaming the hallways of the house I finally built, every insight a support beam holding it up.'
b'I hope you\xe2\x80\x99ll drop by and enjoy your stay.'
b'Follow the @ReaderCenter on Twitter for more coverage highlighting your perspectives and experiences and for insight into how we work.'

b'2019 M.L.B. Season Preview'
b'With better development and technology at every level of the game, teams can draw from a player pool that is bigger \xe2\x80\x94 and cheaper \xe2\x80\x94 than ever. '
b'CreditCreditDale Crosby Close'
b'Supported by'
b'By Tyler Kepner'
b' '
b'BRADENTON, Fla. \xe2\x80\x94 The first player drafted this decade was Bryce Harper, who is now starting a 13-year, $330 million contract with the Philadelphia Phillies. The third player drafted was Manny Machado, who is now starting a 10-year, $300 million contract with the San Diego Padres.'
b'Tucked within that $630 million sandwich, with the second overall pick in 2010, was Jameson Taillon, a starter for the Pittsburgh Pirates whose career has been sidetracked by a torn elbow ligament, a sports hernia and testicular cancer.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI always made sure when I was hurt that my investments were going well and they were in a good place,\xe2\x80\x9d Taillon, 27, said, smiling last month at the Pirates\xe2\x80\x99 training complex here. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can still believe in yourself, but when an injury happens, there\xe2\x80\x99s not much you can do. You never know what\xe2\x80\x99s on the horizon.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'When Taillon was drafted, he could not have known the landscape that would greet him in his first full, healthy season in the majors, which came last year. In 2010, according to Fangraphs, there were 22 pitchers who worked at least 50 innings and averaged 95.2 miles per hour on their fastball. Last season, according to Fangraphs, there were 67, including Taillon.'
b'[A.L. Preview: How Hard Will It Be for the Red Sox to Repeat? Ask the Astros]'
b'The supply of prospects with big-league stuff \xe2\x80\x94 and the savvy to go with it \xe2\x80\x94 has skyrocketed. This is part of the reason that Major League Baseball\xe2\x80\x99s player population has continued to balloon: According to the Elias Sports Bureau, a record 1,379 players appeared in a major league game last season, the sixth year in a row with an increase.'
b'In 2010, Elias said, there were 1,249 overall players \xe2\x80\x94 meaning that teams have added the equivalent of five 25-man rosters, and then some, to the playing ranks since this decade began.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI threw 95 and I was one of the hardest throwers in all of minor-league baseball,\xe2\x80\x9d Taillon said. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, you see guys across every level that throw hard. And a lot\xe2\x80\x99s written about young guys throwing weighted balls and only focusing on throwing hard, but some of these guys are polished. They\xe2\x80\x99re throwing three or four pitches for strikes at 22, 23 years old.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re investing so much money in player development, you\xe2\x80\x99re seeing kids coming to big-league camp that aren\xe2\x80\x99t intimidated.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Standout players like Taillon \xe2\x80\x94 who posted a 3.20 earned run average in 32 starts last season \xe2\x80\x94 can marvel at the trend while still expecting to someday sign a lucrative contract. But while Harper and Machado scored big in free agency, many more veterans will open this season with much lower salaries than players of their caliber once commanded. '
b'That has contributed to a growing sense among players that a system that once reliably rewarded their years of toil no longer works for them. The flurry of contract extensions late in spring training only underscored the point: Take what\xe2\x80\x99s in front of you, because the open market can be a cold, lonely place.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIf you look at the guys that are having to sign these nonroster deals, or guys that still don\xe2\x80\x99t have jobs, far and away most of them comprise that middle class of baseball players,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sean Doolittle, the All-Star closer for the Washington Nationals.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re not the young guys anymore, and they\xe2\x80\x99re not the superstars. They\xe2\x80\x99re very serviceable big leaguers, they\xe2\x80\x99re past arbitration, they\xe2\x80\x99re veterans, and the market for those guys is evaporating. Teams are no longer looking at that eight-year veteran who they might have to spend several million on as someone that can help their team. They\xe2\x80\x99ll take the cheaper labor \xe2\x80\x94 the guy that has options, gives them roster flexibility, and costs a lot less.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'[N.L. Preview: A Deep Talent Pool Should Keep the Dodgers Afloat]'
b'Players need six years of major league service to become free agents, and between two and three years to be eligible for salary arbitration. It could be that the sophistication of player development \xe2\x80\x94 in the United States amateur ranks, the international market and major league farm systems \xe2\x80\x94 is producing a deeper pool of acceptable choices for major league rosters.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I first started scouting in 2007, veteran scouts would still talk about high school kids getting homesick, guys that had never left their hometown, and that\xe2\x80\x99s just not the case anymore,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mike Elias, the Baltimore Orioles\xe2\x80\x99 general manager. \xe2\x80\x9cThese guys, when they enter professional baseball, they\xe2\x80\x99ve already hit with wood, they\xe2\x80\x99ve been on the travel circuit, they know each other from the summers. They\xe2\x80\x99re ready.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The rise in overall players can be traced almost entirely to pitchers. Last season included only six more position players than in 2010, but 124 more pitchers \xe2\x80\x94 up to a record 751. Starters work fewer innings, in part, because teams have better options to replace them as they get deep into games.'
b'Many of those options can be shuttled back and forth between the majors and the minors while making close to the minimum salary of $555,000. They may also offer a higher ceiling of production \xe2\x80\x94 though a lower floor \xe2\x80\x94 than a midlevel veteran who costs much more and cannot be sent to the minors. For many teams, the choice is easy.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cOne of the things teams have realized is the value in giving that opportunity to a player that you\xe2\x80\x99ve signed, scouted and developed relative to the potential marginal upgrade in someone that has four or five years of major league service,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ross Atkins, the Toronto Blue Jays\xe2\x80\x99 general manager. \xe2\x80\x9cDetermining where that difference is, in a more thorough way, is occurring because of manpower, really.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'That realization has transformed franchises. Since Atkins and team president Mark Shapiro took over the Blue Jays\xe2\x80\x99 baseball operations in late 2015, Toronto has added significantly to its front office in an effort to better cultivate its own talent. The Blue Jays have added at least six positions for research and development in addition to creating a high-performance department that emphasizes strength and conditioning, mental health and nutrition throughout their farm system.'
b'Long gone are the days when minor leaguers subsisted on peanut butter and jelly sandwiches with threadbare coaching staffs to guide them. Teams employ more coaches than ever in the minors and the majors, and arm them with increasingly sophisticated tools to develop players.'
b'Jeff Luhnow, the general manager of the Houston Astros, noted that the first aim of the analytics revolution \xe2\x80\x94 think of the \xe2\x80\x9cMoneyball\xe2\x80\x9d-era Oakland Athletics, in the early 2000s \xe2\x80\x94 was using statistics to better predict future performance. Now, he said, teams emphasize technology to better identify tools in amateurs and attack the deficiencies of their own prospects.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s much more actionable feedback today than there ever has been, so you don\xe2\x80\x99t let a player go down a path for very long if it\xe2\x80\x99s not a path that\xe2\x80\x99s going to work for him, long term, in the big leagues,\xe2\x80\x9d Luhnow said.'
b'He mentioned three companies many teams now use \xe2\x80\x94 Rapsodo, TrackMan and Blast Motion \xe2\x80\x94 that give coaches instant feedback on players\xe2\x80\x99 movements. Objective, easy-to-visualize data appeals to players from a tech-savvy generation.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cBefore, you relied on the scout or the coach to say, \xe2\x80\x98Well, you need to make this adjustment, and just trust me on it because I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen it before, and what you\xe2\x80\x99re doing is not going to work at the next level, so you need to change something,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Luhnow said. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, you can actually show them video, show their numbers, and say, \xe2\x80\x98This is what you\xe2\x80\x99re doing, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll show you why that doesn\xe2\x80\x99t work at the next level and show you what does work, and let\xe2\x80\x99s work on changing you, or modifying what you\xe2\x80\x99re doing.\xe2\x80\x99'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThat, by definition, will lead to a higher success rate in development. Let\xe2\x80\x99s say 5 percent of players get developed into big leaguers and it goes up to 7 percent. That\xe2\x80\x99s a massive difference, because we haven\xe2\x80\x99t increased our capacity, but what we\xe2\x80\x99ve done is increase our throughput. So the success rate is good for players, but it probably results in an abundance of an oversupply. Obviously, it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t affect a Harper or Machado, but there are just more options.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Other teams have noticed the Astros\xe2\x80\x99 success. Two of Luhnow\xe2\x80\x99s assistants have become general managers elsewhere \xe2\x80\x94 Baltimore\xe2\x80\x99s Elias and the Milwaukee Brewers\xe2\x80\x99 David Stearns \xe2\x80\x94 and several former Astros coaches have received promotions with other teams, including Alex Cora, who managed the Boston Red Sox to the World Series title last fall.'
b'The same thing has happened to the Los Angeles Dodgers, who have won two National League pennants in a row with a knack for progressive methods. Their president of baseball operations, Andrew Friedman, said he got 39 requests last fall from other teams wanting to interview Dodgers personnel.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe past two off-seasons, I\xe2\x80\x99ve spent the first four to six weeks like an H.R. director,\xe2\x80\x9d Friedman said.'
b'When the Dodgers lost their hitting coach, Turner Ward, to the Cincinnati Reds, they replaced him with Robert Van Scoyoc, 32, a private hitting instructor who never played professionally but who has helped otherwise ordinary hitters, like J.D. Martinez and Chris Taylor, develop into stars. Unlocking hitting secrets, Friedman said, remains a largely untapped field.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cSince I\xe2\x80\x99ve been sitting in advance meetings and been closer to that prep, nothing has changed on the offensive side in 12, 13 years,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cwhereas on the run-prevention side, everything has changed.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'While runs per game have fluctuated in this decade, strikeouts \xe2\x80\x94 the ultimate way to fool or overpower a hitter \xe2\x80\x94 have increased every season since 2006. This month, the league and the union finally gave into the trend and legislated a change.'
b'With no end in sight to the procession of polished, power arms, they agreed to expand the size of the active roster next season to 26 \xe2\x80\x94 virtually assuring that the record of 1,379 players in a season will not last long.'
b'Tyler Kepner has been national baseball writer since 2010. He joined The Times in 2000 and covered the Mets for two seasons, then covered the Yankees from 2002 to 2009.  @TylerKepner '

b'By The Associated Press'
b'MONTERREY, Mexico \xe2\x80\x94 The St. Louis Cardinals, Cincinnati Reds and all of Major League Baseball will have one big fan rooting for them when they come together this weekend at Estadio de B\xc3\xa9isbol de Monterrey \xe2\x80\x94 Mexican President Andres Manuel L\xc3\xb3pez Obrador.'
b'L\xc3\xb3pez Obrador, who took the oath of office last December, is a devoted baseball fan and is trying hard to help the sport succeed in a country where soccer is still king.'
b'The Cardinals and Reds meet for games on Saturday and Sunday, and the Houston Astros and Los Angeles Angels will play another two-game series at the same ballpark in early May.'
b'But L\xc3\xb3pez Obrador wants baseball to be more than just a couple of big league sets every year.'
b'"It\'s well known that baseball is my favorite sport, since I was a kid in my town there was nothing more than baseball," said the 65-year-old L\xc3\xb3pez Obrador, who has several videos on YouTube where he shows off a good swing. "I can still play, I can still hit above .300, I played center field when I was younger and had a good arm and covered a lot of ground."'
b'MLB began holding regular season games in Mexico in 1996 when the San Diego Padres and Mets played three times. The 1999 season opened with a matchup in Monterrey between the Padres and Colorado. Last May, the Padres and Los Angeles Dodgers played a three-game series at the stadium \xe2\x80\x94 Walker Buehler and the Dodgers bullpen combined for a no-hitter.'
b'All of those series were a success with sellouts in Monterrey.'
b"L\xc3\xb3pez Obrador put some action behind his passion and recently created Promobeis, a government office run by \xc3\x89dgar Gonz\xc3\xa1lez, a former player who spent a couple of seasons as a Padres second baseman and was Mexico's manager at the World Baseball Classic."
b'Among other things, Promobeis has a goal to have between 60 and 80 Mexican players in the majors. This season, only eight local players started the season on big league rosters, but Gonz\xc3\xa1lez \xe2\x80\x94 brother of former All-Star Adrian Gonz\xc3\xa1lez \xe2\x80\x94 believes that he can he can have success because there are over 160 Mexicans in the minors.'
b'"I love the challenge, it\'s something that has never been done before and can be a success. I think it\'s easier to have more Mexican baseball players in the big leagues than players in the NFL. Besides, we believe that we can help to take kids away from the bad habits," Gonz\xc3\xa1lez told The Associated Press.'
b'"We believe that having more players in the majors will help us to grow the sport\'s popularity. Right now we have a little bit over 160 players in the minors, in the majors right now there are 10 because some of them are constantly been called up or sent down. It\'s a good number and I believe we can grow in the future and have more players signing with MLB clubs like the Puerto Ricans or the Dominicans," Gonz\xc3\xa1lez said.'
b'To put the program in motion, Gonz\xc3\xa1lez and Promobeis have a $17.5 million budget for 2019. The money will be invested in the opening of 10 baseball academies that are going to be administered by the government.'
b'"It\'s going to be great if they support baseball like the president wants. You need to start early in the sport to be successful," said former big league slugger Vinny Castilla, considered one of the all-time great Mexican players. "Baseball is our president*s great passion and that\'s big news because for all of us that love the sport, he is going to support the sport as much as he can."'
b"The idea of having more Mexican players in the big leagues as a way to boost the sport's popularity in the country is not new. MLB Commissioner Rob Manfred has spoken about it on several occasions as a big factor for the country to be considered like a candidate for an expansion franchise in the future."
b"Besides L\xc3\xb3pez Obrador's ideas, another door to the majors opened for the Mexicans last March. That's when MLB announced an agreement with the Liga Mexicana de B\xc3\xa9isbol that allows all players who are under contract to an LMB team to be able to sign with any MLB club as long the player is at least 25 years old or has six or more years of professional baseball experience abroad."
b'"Mexico is an important market for our game, as demonstrated by our series in Monterrey. We look forward to the opportunities to develop more major league players and baseball fans in the country," Manfred said.'
b"Even though soccer is considered to be Mexico's national pastime, it was not always like that. Football and baseball took turns last century being the nation's favorites. The NFL has had great success in playing in Mexico, and Fernando Valenzuela helped the country's baseball profile when he ascended with the Dodgers in the 1980s."
b'The Liga Mexicana de B\xc3\xa9isbol, established in 1925, had several American greats on its rosters such as Satchel Paige, "Cool Papa" Bell and Josh Gibson, along with the likes of star pitcher Sal Maglie.'
b"But eventually Mexican teams were unable to compete with U.S. salaries and the sport's popularity faded as soccer grew in the '60s and early '70s, after Mexico hosted the 1970 World Cup."
b'The success of the government plan to help baseball rise in popularity in Mexico remains to be seen, meanwhile the country has regular season series guaranteed until 2021 that can also help the sport to become more mainstream.'
b'"It\'s clear that soccer is the strongest, but we want to compete with them and we want to be able to say that baseball is growing and also that the kids know that they have a chance there," Gonz\xc3\xa1lez said.'
b'"While we try to get more Mexicans in the majors, it\'s great to have this games every year, it\'s a great way for kids to look at those athletes and know the discipline required to make it, it becomes aspirational and that can definitively help us."'

b'For some players, the sense of beginnings and hope is tempered by the thought: Is this my last one?'
b'By Doug Glanville'
b'Mr. Glanville is a former professional baseball player.'
b'Players from all over the world are now deep into the daily work that defines major league spring training camps in Arizona and Florida. Most, undoubtedly, are still filled with that unexplainable feeling that captures baseball\xe2\x80\x99s spring fever. It is in the air, in the humidity and, over time, in your bones. Your own internal barometer is telling you it\xe2\x80\x99s time to play ball.'
b'But the knowledge that baseball season will start again is not free of trepidation. Pros, especially veterans, are aware that no matter how they may push science, they will inevitably be defeated by the battle of attrition that erodes skill sets and, ultimately, their value to a team.'
b'This does not happen immediately, for the early years are filled with optimism and confidence \xe2\x80\x94 we all are one pitch, one bloop single away from making the team. '
b'But at every spring training, players are quietly wondering if this is it, the last time they will be putting on the uniform as a professional. Beyond the shortfall of talent that may prematurely push them out of the game while still young, a few in this category are genuinely exhausted from the grind of the road, the off-season surgeries, the missed birthday parties, the flowers they had to send in their place to the funeral of a friend. But many have been sliding into home for a long time, the last 90 feet telling them that it is the only destination \xe2\x80\x94 a final career resting place where they both score and expire.'
b'My own slide into home started when I was running to first base, in Texas in 2003. I felt a pop, my strides became irregular, and I was helped off the field in my first major league trip to the injured list as a 32-year-old veteran outfielder. Speed was my game, so this was a bigger deal than when, for instance, the Seattle Mariners\xe2\x80\x99 star hitter Edgar Martinez (now a newly minted Hall of Famer) popped a hamstring. In my case, my resulting torn hamstring tendon compromised all aspects of my game as a base-stealing, contact-hitting defender.'
b'I would come back after surgery, close to new. But to perform as if I was new required hours of pre-game rituals, bearable when I was still a starter, less so when my job was to pinch-hit off a nasty closer or spot-start against an ace.'
b'By the season after surgery, I knew I would not be able to match my National League-leading 678 at-bats in 1998. My body could not take it, even with the training and pre-game routine.'
b'It was the new normal. The days of Wiffle ball in front of my parents\xe2\x80\x99 house, when no stretching was required, had faded to memory. Using the telephone pole and the lamppost as foul territory markers (while denoting the electrical wire above the sidewalk as \xe2\x80\x9cinterference\xe2\x80\x9d) felt further away because only my mind and soul could reach that place, not my body. I had to stretch, and often, just to step into the batter\xe2\x80\x99s box.'
b'When I came into the Yankees\xe2\x80\x99 spring training in 2005, I had already started smelling the roses. Getting autographs from opponents, taking pictures with teammates, collecting archives and old newspaper clippings were now as much a part of my routine as getting ultrasounds on my hamstring. I had to start thinking about the children I didn\xe2\x80\x99t have, the memories that would someday be jarred to life by a memento. It was the first time I understood what it meant to simultaneously be present and watch a personal future dim, while collecting what had been.'
b'Shawon Dunston, my Chicago Cubs teammate and mentor, once said to me: "Never give the uniform back. Let them rip it off your body." He was noting the unavoidable endpoint to playing this game as a professional. Yet to hand the uniform in creates a sense of control that you rarely experience in your career. In retrospect, I found that the uniform does not get metaphorically ripped off your body, but slowly falls off, year by year, stich by stitch, unraveling to reveal the indiscriminate work of time.'
b'Ballplayers know the phone call asking for their services will stop ringing one day, but the birds of spring will never stop calling. The ghosts of our career in this love affair will stay with us as glimpses. The walk-off home run, the first hit, the tears in our eyes when playing after the Sept. 11 attacks.'
b'Right now, the players are focused on the work at hand, and until that first cut, they all know they have the same opportunity as every player in camp: to make the team, make history, and for those at the end of their careers, buy time.'
b'Yet at best, it is a mortgage, divinely owned and borrowed. It is a spring contract where the fine print is our friend, reminding us that the only way to extend time in this game is to deed it to the next generation.'
b'Doug Glanville (@dougglanville), a former professional baseball player, is the author of \xe2\x80\x9cThe Game From Where I Stand,\xe2\x80\x9d a lecturer at Yale and a contributing opinion writer.'

b'daily crossword column'
b'Alan Arbesfeld gets us over the midweek hump.'
b'By Deb Amlen'
b'WEDNESDAY PUZZLE \xe2\x80\x94 When we talk about \xe2\x80\x9cwheelhouses,\xe2\x80\x9d we are describing the length, breadth and depth of one\xe2\x80\x99s knowledge. Something you know is in your wheelhouse. Something you don\xe2\x80\x99t really know might be guessed at, but you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t really say that it is in your wheelhouse. Things you absolutely don\xe2\x80\x99t know are in your outhouse.'
b'Ha ha! Just kidding, sort of. I can take a reasonable guess at some sports clues, but usually they are not only outside my wheelhouse and my outhouse, but they actually reside down the road in a neighboring town.'

b'Keeping Score'
b'Just four years after Major League Baseball\xe2\x80\x99s integration, Newcombe won 20 games for the Dodgers. That was the start of an extraordinary club. '
b'Don Newcombe was the first black player to win 20 games. He did so three times in his career.CreditCreditAssociated Press'
b'Supported by'
b'By Benjamin Hoffman'
b'When Dave Stewart reported to big league camp for the Los Angeles Dodgers in the mid-1970s, he did so as one of baseball\xe2\x80\x99s rarities: a black starting pitcher. '
b'Fortunately for Stewart, Don Newcombe was in camp to walk the teenager through those early days, setting Stewart up for future stardom.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cHis influence on me is beyond words,\xe2\x80\x9d Stewart said. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t even tell you how huge it was for me to meet him at the time I did in my career.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Being around Newcombe, who died on Tuesday at 92, gave Stewart unfiltered access to what Mudcat Grant, another top black pitcher, would one day describe as a Black Ace. Grant, who wrote a book on the subject, had simple criteria for that distinction: an American- or Canadian-born black player who won 20 games in a season. That\xe2\x80\x99s it.'
b'Newcombe did it first, winning 20 for the Brooklyn Dodgers in 1951, just four seasons after his eventual teammate Jackie Robinson broke baseball\xe2\x80\x99s color barrier. In the 67 seasons since, 195 pitchers besides Newcombe have recorded a 20-win season, and only 14 of them were black \xe2\x80\x94 a select list that includes Stewart, who won at least 20 games in four consecutive seasons, from 1987 to 1990.'
b'Making wins the sole criterion for being a Black Ace may seem antiquated, but Stewart said the 20-win mark represented something beyond the technical definition of the statistic. To him, 20 wins showed that the player was a workhorse, shouldering a burden for his teammates by taking them deep into games.'
b'Vida Blue, a three-time 20-game winner, said that the standard worked because it set a high bar of success among an already-select group of people.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cTo become a starting pitcher in itself was unique,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s no different than being a black quarterback.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Out of Grant\xe2\x80\x99s book emerged a support group of sorts. There were initially 12 Black Aces \xe2\x80\x94 Newcombe, Sam Jones, Bob Gibson, Grant, Ferguson Jenkins, Earl Wilson, Al Downing, Blue, J.R. Richard, Mike Norris, Dwight Gooden and Stewart \xe2\x80\x94 and Grant organized events for some of them to attend, reached out to young players and pushed for recognition for the Black Aces\xe2\x80\x99 accomplishments.'
b'As Grant\xe2\x80\x99s health faltered in recent years, the group drifted apart even as it added three new members: Dontrelle Willis, C.\xe2\x80\x89C. Sabathia and David Price. '
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThis group deserves to be more than just a footnote in baseball history,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bob Kendrick, the president of the Negro Leagues Baseball Museum. \xe2\x80\x9cIf it takes the passing of Don Newcombe to help us reflect and remember, then that\xe2\x80\x99s another gift that he has given us.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Why haven\xe2\x80\x99t there been more Black Aces in recent years? There are two popular explanations: Winning 20 games is remarkably difficult in modern baseball, and there simply aren\xe2\x80\x99t that many black players on major league rosters. '
b'Last year, there was an uptick in the latter, with the number of black players from the United States and Canada on opening-day active rosters rising to 8.4 percent, according to Major League Baseball, but that was well below the sport\xe2\x80\x99s high-water mark of 19 percent, in 1986. Among that 8.4 percent, black starting pitchers were a minority within another minority, a group that includes Sabathia, Price, Chris Archer and Tyson Ross.'
b'Blue, who reached the majors in 1969, thought the explanation could be as simple as athletes who choose baseball over football or basketball wanting to play a position that had more action. But according to Kendrick, the scarcity of black pitchers goes all the way back to the early days of integration, and that, at least initially, it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t the players making the decision.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cDuring that era of the Negro Leagues, the pitching position, the catching position and the shortstop position were all seen as cerebral positions,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou had this underlying belief that black athletes weren\xe2\x80\x99t smart enough to do it.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Newcombe emphatically proved any doubters wrong. He established himself as a front-line starter in his first three seasons, with a 56-28 record and 3.39 earned run average. After missing two seasons because of military service, and then struggling in his first year back, he soared to new heights in 1955, helping to lead the Dodgers to their first World Series title. '
b'In 1956, a season that ended with a loss to the Yankees in the World Series, Newcombe not only won 27 games and the National League\xe2\x80\x99s Most Valuable Player Award, but he also was named the first winner of the newly established Cy Young Award, which that season was given to the best pitcher in the majors. '
b'Newcombe, by his own frequent admissions, struggled with alcohol in his career, which most likely contributed to a steep decline after the 1956 season. His major league career ended abruptly in 1960, and his playing days came to an unusual end in 1962 with a brief sojourn to Japan, where he played mostly as an outfielder. But Newcombe quit drinking and was back working with the Dodgers by 1970, positioning him for his eventual role mentoring Stewart and dozens of other players, including the Dodgers\xe2\x80\x99 current closer, Kenley Jansen.'
b'It was Newcombe\xe2\x80\x99s innate ability to relate his experiences to young players that Kendrick said would leave a \xe2\x80\x9cgaping void\xe2\x80\x9d after the star player\xe2\x80\x99s death. '
b'\xe2\x80\x9cEvery time we lose one of these guys that window of opportunity closes just a little more,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAt some point in time there won\xe2\x80\x99t be any Negro league players left to attest to what that league was all about.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'But from one Black Ace to another, Newcombe taught Stewart the value of being there when new black starters come along. And Stewart said he was proud that both Sabathia and Willis have acknowledged the influence he had on their careers. '
b'\xe2\x80\x9cFor those guys, I was present when they needed me,\xe2\x80\x9d Stewart said.'
b'Newcombe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have had it any other way.'
b'Benjamin Hoffman is a senior staff editor and regular contributor to the Keeping Score column in sports. He joined The Times in 2005.  @BenHoffmanNYT \xe2\x80\xa2 Facebook '

b'By David Waldstein and Michael Tackett'
b'President Trump, reversing an agreement negotiated by the Obama administration, abruptly ended a deal between Major League Baseball and the Cuban Baseball Federation that had eased the path for players to compete in the United States without defecting from their country.'
b'The Trump administration said that the deal constituted a violation of trade laws because the Cuban federation was part of the government in Havana \xe2\x80\x94 a departure from the previous administration\xe2\x80\x99s stance.'
b'In a letter to M.L.B.\xe2\x80\x99s outside counsel, which was obtained by The New York Times, Nikole Thomas, the acting assistant director for licensing at the Office of Foreign Assets Control, outlined the U.S. government\xe2\x80\x99s opposition to the deal, which stipulated that the Cuban federation would receive 25 percent of a player\xe2\x80\x99s signing bonus for a minor league player and between 15 and 25 percent for a major league player.'
b'The letter said that the O.F.A.C. had \xe2\x80\x9cdetermined that M.L.B.\xe2\x80\x99s payments to the Cuban Baseball Federation are not authorized\xe2\x80\x9d because \xe2\x80\x9ca payment to the Cuban Baseball Federation is a payment to the Cuban government.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'News of the administration\xe2\x80\x99s rejection of M.L.B.\xe2\x80\x99s plan was first reported by The Wall Street Journal and ESPN.'
b'Cuban sports entities, including its baseball federation and Olympic committee, have argued they are independent of the central government, but the Trump administration rejected that assessment.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe U.S. does not support actions that would institutionalize a system by which a Cuban government entity garnishes the wages of hard-working athletes who simply seek to live and compete in a free society,\xe2\x80\x9d said Garrett Marquis, a spokesman for the National Security Council. \xe2\x80\x9cThe administration looks forward to working with M.L.B. to identify ways for Cuban players to have the individual freedom to benefit from their talents, and not as property of the Cuban state.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'In a statement, M.L.B. said: \xe2\x80\x9cWe stand by the goal of the agreement, which is to end the human trafficking of baseball players from Cuba.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'One M.L.B. official, who requested anonymity because they were not authorized to speak publicly on the matter, said the league had requested a face-to-face meeting with U.S. government agencies but were not granted one. M.L.B. was prepared to base its argument on the fact that the Obama administration had given it a license in 2016 to do business with the Cuban baseball federation, and that a Trump-era national security presidential memorandum published on June 16, 2017 promised the agreement would be grandfathered in. The memorandum stated that any new regulations \xe2\x80\x9cwill not affect existing contracts and licenses.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Several U.S. business entities, including travel and telecommunications companies, do business in Cuba. But M.L.B. officials worried that, as a high-profile organization, its deal would be challenged by hard-liners in the government.'
b'John Bolton, the national security adviser, seemed to foreshadow the move on Sunday when he tweeted: \xe2\x80\x9cCuba wants to use baseball players as economic pawns \xe2\x80\x94 selling their rights to Major League Baseball. America\xe2\x80\x99s national pastime should not enable the Cuban regime\xe2\x80\x99s support for Maduro in Venezuela.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Cuba has produced some of the best players currently in the major leagues, including Aroldis Chapman of the Yankees, Yoenis Cespedes of the Mets and Jose Abreu of the Chicago White Sox. They and most other Cuban players in M.L.B. left their country illegally, in some cases smuggled out by disreputable agents and human traffickers on speedboats and rafts over dangerous waters.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s bad in the sense that they can\xe2\x80\x99t come anymore in the formal, legal way that was planned,\xe2\x80\x9d Chapman said on Monday.'
b'For some players, the dangers continued after they arrived because they were at the mercy of the smugglers.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cTo this date, I am still harassed,\xe2\x80\x9d Abreu, who has played for the Chicago White Sox since 2014, said in a statement issued at the time the deal was announced in December.'
b'M.L.B. and its players union sought to eliminate that dangerous process by creating a posting system similar to the one used with players from Japan and South Korea. Under the plan, a major league team seeking to sign a Cuban player would pay a posting fee to the Cuban federation, which would in turn allow the player to leave the country and return home at will. Just days ago, the Cuban federation released the names of 34 players it said were eligible to sign with M.L.B.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe objective of the agreement, which is the product of years of negotiations with the F.C.B., is to end the dangerous trafficking of Cuban baseball players who desire to play professional baseball in the United States,\xe2\x80\x9d M.L.B.\xe2\x80\x99s lawyers said in their petition to the Treasury Department in January.'
b'M.L.B. and the Cuban baseball federation commenced talks after the United States and Cuba began a thaw in relations under the Obama administration in 2014. Soon after that, baseball\xe2\x80\x99s commissioner\xe2\x80\x99s office secured a license from the Treasury Department to legally begin negotiations with the Cubans.'
b'After announcing its agreement in December, M.L.B. quietly sought to convince administration officials \xe2\x80\x94 who immediately criticized the plan publicly \xe2\x80\x94 that it fit within the guidelines for an exemption to the trade laws. At the same time, M.L.B. officials were also resigned to the political opposition to the deal and the possibility of returning to the old method of signing Cuban players.'

b'By Reuters'
b'WASHINGTON \xe2\x80\x94 The Trump administration on Monday scrapped a historic agreement between Major League Baseball and the Cuban Baseball Federation that would have allowed Cuban players to sign with U.S. teams without needing to defect.'
b"The existing deal will not be allowed to go forward because it was based on an erroneous ruling by the former Obama administration that the Cuban Baseball Federation was not part of Cuba's Communist government, a senior U.S. official said."
b'The move essentially overturns an agreement reached between MLB and the Cuban federation in December after three years of negotiation under which Cuban players would have had a safe, legal path to playing professionally in the United States. In the past, some Cuban stars have undertaken risky escapes, including being smuggled off the island in speedboats.'
b"The U.S. decision was the latest step by President Donald Trump to roll back the rapprochement with Havana, America's old Cold War foe, that was spearheaded by his predecessor, Barack Obama. Trump has especially stepped up pressure on Cuba recently over its support for Venezuela's socialist president, Nicolas Maduro."
b'"The agreement with #MLB seeks to stop the trafficking of human beings, encourage cooperation and raise the level of baseball," the Cuban Baseball Federation said on Twitter. "Any contrary idea is false news. Attacks with political motivation against the agreement achieved harm the athletes, their families and the fans."'
b'The senior Trump administration official suggested that the agreement would have subjected the players to "human trafficking" by the Cuban government, making them "pawns of the Cuba dictatorship."'
b'Carlos Tabares, a member of Cuba\'s 2004 Olympic gold medal-winning squad who until last year played for a Havana team, said the Trump administration was seeking "to destroy us, even through baseball." The MLB deal, he said, would have allowed Cuban players "to enter legally rather than falling into the hands of traffickers and having to risk their lives."'
b'The U.S. official, speaking on condition of anonymity, said the administration would be willing to work with MLB to seek an arrangement consistent with U.S. law.'
b'"We are unsure of the next steps," an MLB source said.'
b'The announcement came just days after the Cuban federation released its first list of 34 players authorized to sign contracts directly with major league teams.'
b"'A PAYMENT TO THE CUBAN GOVERNMENT'"
b"The U.S. Treasury Department on Monday reversed the Obama-era ruling that Cuba's league was not part of the Cuban government, which had laid the groundwork for the baseball deal."
b"The Trump administration considers the Cuban Baseball Federation to be part of the island's National Sports Institute, which, according to the U.S. official, is a government entity that U.S. law says makes such business dealings illegal."
b'"A payment to the Cuban Baseball Federation is a payment to the Cuban government," Treasury said in a letter to MLB.'
b'Cuba maintains, however, that its baseball federation is not part of the state. It says the federation falls under the Cuban Olympic Committee, which in turn reports to the International Olympic Committee, not the Cuban government.'
b'MLB teams would have paid the federation a release fee for each Cuban player signed, providing a windfall for Cuban baseball, which has suffered from dwindling budgets and the defection of its best players.'
b'In the past, many Cuban players seeking riches in the big leagues have made dangerous journeys via human traffickers to defect. Others abandoned the Cuban national team while playing abroad.'
b'Cuban MLB players who had defected include Yasiel Puig of the Cincinnati Reds, Yoenis Cespedes of the New York Mets and Jose Dariel Abreu of the Chicago White Sox - all of whom have signed multi-year, multimillion-dollar contracts.'
b"Puig defected from Cuba on a speedboat at age 21 and soon found himself entangled with Mexico's notorious Zetas crime organization, which threatened to chop off his arm if it failed to receive the promised $250,000 fee for his passage."
b'When Puig finally reached U.S. shores in 2012, he was rewarded with a seven-year, $42 million contract with the Los Angeles Dodgers.'
b'Under the deal agreed by MLB and the Cuban federation in December, MLB teams were going to pay the federation a fee ranging from 15 percent to 25 percent of the value of player contracts.'
b'That money, which was not to be deducted from the player\xe2\x80\x99s salary but be paid on top of it, would have been used to help Cuba further develop its baseball program.'
b'Cuban players older than age 25 and with six years of service in the Cuban league would have been free to sign with MLB teams. Younger players would have needed Cuban baseball\xe2\x80\x99s permission to leave.'

b'WEST PALM BEACH, Fla. \xe2\x80\x94 I walked into the spring training locker room of the Houston Astros with a question for the assembled players. What is going on with the slow pace of free-agent contracts this winter?'
b'Alex Bregman, the Astros\xe2\x80\x99 star third baseman, had just finished taking his morning hacks on the field and is likely to be a future occupant of baseball\xe2\x80\x99s penthouse. He could earn a munificent contract of the sort handed out to Bryce Harper ($330 million) and Manny Machado ($300 million) these past two weeks. But he sees many dozens of ballplayers who have fallen short of stardom without contracts, and that angers him.'
b'Worse, he sees teams content with mediocrity: Fewer clubs are competing to sign the stars. \xe2\x80\x9cA lot of teams seem fine with losing and getting TV money and making no attempt to sign players,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is bad for the game.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'I wandered over to Josh Reddick\xe2\x80\x99s locker. Lithe and a free spirit, he\xe2\x80\x99s a good right fielder and a careful observer of the game. He suggested that perhaps baseball players should follow the lead of N.B.A. players and speak out.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cA lot of guys are pissed off,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are a lot of guys who should have jobs who are just hanging there. If it takes another bad strike to change this, then that\xe2\x80\x99s what we need to do.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'We have arrived at a hinge point in sports. From angry baseball players talking strike to quarterback Colin Kaepernick to running back Le\xe2\x80\x99Veon Bell to the N.B.A. players LeBron James, Stephen Curry and Chris Paul, professional athletes are splendidly outspoken. They opine on President Trump and popular culture and their sport\xe2\x80\x99s economics, and it is indisputably stirring to see young men and women looking beyond the horizon of wins and losses.'
b'Yet politicization plays out in radically different ways and not invariably to the benefit of athletes. Baseball grooves on and is constrained by its traditions. It has a powerful union, the strongest in pro sports, and that coexists with the sense that individuality is suspect and no player is as big as the sport itself.'
b'So baseball players sail toward a possible confrontation with the owners without leaders who possess the transcendent cultural cachet and business power of, say, a LeBron James.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt feels like baseball lives in the past, and that undercuts player power,\xe2\x80\x9d noted Adrian Burgos, a history professor at the University of Illinois and editor of La Vida Baseball, which studies the Latino influence on baseball. \xe2\x80\x9cWhereas the N.B.A. imagines itself as the future, and it has to create a world in which players have more power.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The N.B.A., players in many sports will tell you, stands as the zeitgeist prototype, the most free-spirited of the leagues and with the youngest fan base \xe2\x80\x94 the average age of an N.B.A. viewer is 37, compared with 55 in baseball. Its stars have become hybrids: players, power brokers and globe-spanning businessmen. So James, who signed with the Los Angeles Lakers with an eye toward building an entertainment empire, spent much of February trying to force the New Orleans Pelicans to trade its star center, Anthony Davis, to the Lakers.'
b'James\xe2\x80\x99s heist failed amid complaints he had stepped out of his player\xe2\x80\x99s lane and tampered with another team\xe2\x80\x99s star. Suffice to say James did not appear chastened and Davis could try to force a trade this summer. James has a television show, \xe2\x80\x9cThe Shop,\xe2\x80\x9d on HBO, and guess who was one of his scheduled guests on the Season 2 premiere on Friday night?'
b'Yes, the same A. Davis.'
b'The N.B.A. life is nothing if not an intersectional experience.'
b'Baseball and football are more tightly bound by their cultures and history. The N.F.L. long ago went all in on Death Star dominance, sated on money and nose wrinkled in distaste for dissent. The owners cut a presumably very large check to Kaepernick, who almost certainly was blackballed for taking a dignified knee during the national anthem. There is no assurance he will again run out onto an N.F.L. field.'
b'Player solidarity in football is a barely flickering lamp. When Bell refused to report to the Steelers\xe2\x80\x99 training camp last fall, The Pittsburgh Post-Gazette reported that teammates removed his name plate and plundered his locker of shoes. Bell decided to sit out the 2018 season rather than accept a constraining franchise-tag deal.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe N.F.L. has capitalized in post-9/11 patriotism and the sense that its shield is bigger than players, and that infects the sport,\xe2\x80\x9d David Leonard, a professor who teaches about the intersection of race, culture and sport at Washington State, Pullman, said.'
b'The N.B.A. is the antithesis of this. Player and even coaching personas \xe2\x80\x94 see Steve Kerr and Gregg Popovich\xe2\x80\x99s scathing takes on Trump \xe2\x80\x94 have become central to its marketing appeal, a Twitter, WhatsApp, Instagrammed world of hooping and opining. Adam Silver, the commissioner of the N.B.A., may not groove on athletes\xe2\x80\x99 desire to bend teams to their will, but that is woven into the league\xe2\x80\x99s DNA.'
b'Bill Russell, the 85-year-old basketball legend who took a photo of himself wearing a T-shirt with the words, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m with Kap,\xe2\x80\x9d and tweeted it out, serves as a reminder, too, that its athletes have been outspoken for generations.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cN.B.A. players are taking a role in their own marketing and their own futures,\xe2\x80\x9d Leonard said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou see a concerted effort not only to wield power but to create power.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'This has not escaped the notice of baseball players. In their world, too much individuality, wearing a hat backward or tossing a bat like a baton after a home run, can draw a roll of eyes.'
b'Reddick, the Astros\xe2\x80\x99 right fielder, has watched N.B.A. players with admiration. \xe2\x80\x9cBasketball players are very outspoken about their opinions,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBaseball has always been about giving the generic clich\xc3\xa9 answer that keeps you guessing. Basketball players are much more outspoken and go into it with a lot of depth.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Analytics border on Holy Writ in baseball front offices and for many sportswriters, and this too acts to strangely diminish stars even in their moment of glory. So we\xe2\x80\x99re told that Harper, an intense and seemingly transcendent young star, is less than he appears because his WAR rating last year (wins above replacement and a rather subjective statistic) was low. There is the implicit suggestion that players and fans would do well to yield to numeracy triumphalism.'
b'That insistence acts as oil poured on the bonfire of player discontent. Stars to the side, more than 70 major leaguers remain without contracts, and rank-and-file sorts have signed minor league contracts in hopes they might clamber onto a major league roster with a good spring. The players note \xe2\x80\x94 how to say this sweetly? \xe2\x80\x94 the coincidence that team computer programs seem to kick out contract offers that occupy the same narrow bandwidth.'
b'In the 1980s, such coincidence went by the name of collusion; today, it\xe2\x80\x99s apparently just artificial intelligence doing what it does.'
b'Zack Britton, a fine relief pitcher, signed a three-year, $39 million contract this winter with the Yankees, and counted himself lucky. \xe2\x80\x9cThese clubs are all controlled by Major League Baseball,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd they are submitting almost the same offers, which is kind of weird, right?\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Reddick has walked this modern baseball world. He played four years for the Oakland Athletics and loved his manager and the bleacher bums with whom he partied and the Bay Area. When he reached free agency and wanted his compensation, well: Hasta la vista, baby.'
b'The Athletics pull off this tightwad act with exceedingly bright management, but it is an exception.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cUnless you\xe2\x80\x99re a die-hard fan and you\xe2\x80\x99ve got a date or you just want to get hammered on $9 beers, it\xe2\x80\x99s hard to see why you\xe2\x80\x99d keep going to the games of some teams,\xe2\x80\x9d Reddick said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a bummer because you are supposed to want to win games.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The union-management agreement expires in two years. In this age of the outspoken athlete, the center may no longer hold. \xe2\x80\x9cIn 2021,\xe2\x80\x9d Reddick predicted, \xe2\x80\x9ca lot of stuff could hit the fan.\xe2\x80\x9d'

b'It\xe2\x80\x99s called the yips, and it\xe2\x80\x99s a sudden inability to play. I had to find my way out of it. '
b'By Naomi Gordon-Loebl'
b'I quit playing baseball when I was 11 years old. That was the cutoff for girls; after that point, we had to switch to softball, and I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t having it.'
b'My father tried to reason with me.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cEverybody plays softball eventually,\xe2\x80\x9d he told me. \xe2\x80\x9cAll adults make the switch.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'But I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t an adult. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to pitch underhand. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to play with a ball the size of a grapefruit. Most of all, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want the most important thing in my life taken away from me because I was a girl.'
b'Up to that point, baseball had defined me. I had one of those T-shirts \xe2\x80\x94 \xe2\x80\x9cBaseball is life. The rest is just details.\xe2\x80\x9d And in my case, it was pretty much true. If anyone asked me what I was going to be when I grew up, I told them: the first woman in the Major League.'
b'I shelved my glove, took up basketball and didn\xe2\x80\x99t walk back onto a baseball diamond for 17 years.'
b'Until one spring two years ago, when I joined a softball team.'
b'Tryouts were on a cold Saturday morning, and as I walked over to the playground, I wondered what I was getting myself into. I had known about this league for years, but joining had never occurred to me. '
b'Something, though, made me more open. I missed sports: the current that runs through you when you catch the meaty part of the ball with your bat; the way everyone cheers when the shortstop catches a line drive to win the game. I wanted to be in the park at dusk, that perfect time of day in summer when the sun finally stops being punishing and all of the fields look orange in the light. And I wanted the camaraderie of a team.'
b'Still: Dreams of friendship and sunsets over baseball diamonds were one thing. Throwing a softball for the first time with three dozen strangers in finger-stiffening temperatures was entirely another.'
b'A middle-aged woman with dimples pinned the number 22 to the back of my sweatshirt and sent me off to play catch. The ball was big, but it flew easily out of my hand and into my partner\xe2\x80\x99s glove. It felt good to throw \xe2\x80\x94 familiar, even. We took turns hitting, then fielded grounders at every base. Each time, I scooped up the ball and hurled it at first. Each time, my throw went straight into the woman\xe2\x80\x99s mitt.'
b'The first month was good. Our team played once a week. After most games we\xe2\x80\x99d head to a bar, where we\xe2\x80\x99d drink a few beers and rehash the ump\xe2\x80\x99s bad calls. Softball was turning out exactly as I had hoped it would.'
b'And then, sometime in June, I started throwing wild.'
b'I\xe2\x80\x99m not sure how it happened. All I know is that I suddenly found myself incapable of throwing the ball. '
b'The loss of skill was so complete that it was as if I had never been able to throw a ball in my life. Except that I remembered, so clearly that it was agonizing, a time when throwing was the most natural action in my body\xe2\x80\x99s repertoire. The motion was so deeply etched in my ligaments that it seemed like the process happened entirely outside my brain.'
b'The disappearance of something I had always been good at; the fear that it would never come back; the feeling that something valuable had been taken from me without explanation \xe2\x80\x94 it was like losing baseball all over again.'
b'It turns out that the sudden inability to complete the most basic motion in sports has a name: the yips.'
b'As I soon discovered on Wikipedia, the yips is the \xe2\x80\x9closs of fine motor skills in athletes.\xe2\x80\x9d It occurs \xe2\x80\x9cwithout apparent explanation.\xe2\x80\x9d And, Wikipedia helpfully informed me, it \xe2\x80\x9chas no known treatment.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Great.'
b'I read about high-profile cases: Chuck Knoblauch, the second baseman who once hit a sportscaster\xe2\x80\x99s mother in the face with a wild ball. Mackey Sasser, the catcher who couldn\xe2\x80\x99t throw back to the pitcher without double-taking.'
b'And then there was Steve Blass, the great pitcher who developed the yips and was sent down to the minors. I dug up Roger Angell\xe2\x80\x99s 1975 profile of him from The New Yorker and read every word, hoping to uncover some secret that would change my own luck. There was, of course, nothing. Mr. Blass retired early and became a jewelry salesman.At first my team chalked it up to nerves.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cNaomi!\xe2\x80\x9d my coach shouted after I fielded a grounder and then threw it 10 feet to her right at practice. \xe2\x80\x9cJust relax!\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'I tried. But the problem with trying to relax is that the more you try, the less relaxed you feel. I tried to take my time. I tried to be natural. I tried to focus on the mechanics; I tried not to focus on the mechanics at all. I tried to think about being loose; I tried to think about throwing hard. I tried really, really hard not to think.'
b'Sometimes it would work for a while. I\xe2\x80\x99d land a few good tosses, and hopefulness would creep in. But inevitably the anxiety would catch up to me, and soon enough a ball would sail past my partner, sending her jogging into the distance to retrieve it.'
b'After a particularly rough game, my coach pulled me aside. I had been desperate to tell someone on the team what was happening to me: that I was under some kind of curse, that my baseball skills had apparently gone into a coma, that this wasn\xe2\x80\x99t the real me. I thought this was my chance.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cYou know, I\xe2\x80\x99ve been having this weird problem \xe2\x80\x94 maybe it\xe2\x80\x99s anxiety. This thing happens where I can\xe2\x80\x99t throw the ball.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Her face went blank. '
b'\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t worry, bud, we all get nervous,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cJust do your best.\xe2\x80\x9d She patted me on the shoulder and headed off.'
b'The realization came with a thud: To my coach, I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t suffering from some kind of performance anxiety. I just sucked.So again, I quit. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to further damage my relationship to something that had once been a source of joy. I stopped playing baseball when I was 11, but I had always carried it with me as something that I was good at, something that no one could take away. Now it seemed that the opposite was true. '
b'One weekend after I left the team, my partner and I made plans to go away with her family. Her father had been a semipro pitcher in his youth.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cYou should bring your glove,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI bet my dad would love to play with you.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'A few days later, she spoke to her father on the phone. '
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI told him about your problem,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cHe said the same thing happened to him once. And he said what you need to do is ...\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'I readied myself for advice I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t use: \xe2\x80\x9cSet your feet before you throw.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cJust relax!\xe2\x80\x9d '
b'But that wasn\xe2\x80\x99t what was coming.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cHe said you need to find a wall with no one around, and take a ball there and throw it against the wall for a long time, over and over, until it comes back to you. All alone.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'I thought about the hours I spent as a kid throwing a ball against a wall: old tennis balls against my parents\xe2\x80\x99 stoop; blue Sky Bounce balls against my elementary school building at recess. When we went to my aunts\xe2\x80\x99 and uncles\xe2\x80\x99 houses in the suburbs, we threw baseballs against the garage door until they left marks and we got in trouble. It was the kind of throwing I longed for now, the kind of throwing you don\xe2\x80\x99t have to think about.'
b'I didn\xe2\x80\x99t bring my glove on vacation. But I did stop into a sporting goods store a few weeks ago. I asked the salesman if there were any softballs soft enough to throw against a wall, and he pointed me to a box of neon-yellow ones.'
b'I flung one against the floor. It leapt up at me, satisfying, easily making its way back into my hand.'
b'I bought it. It\xe2\x80\x99s been sitting on my dresser for a while now.'
b'There\xe2\x80\x99s a playground down the street from my parents\xe2\x80\x99 house. We used to play catch there, and basketball, and sometimes we\xe2\x80\x99d skateboard. I haven\xe2\x80\x99t been in years. But I remember a big, wide handball wall, too, one that seemed to be rarely used.'
b'Maybe I\xe2\x80\x99ll try it.'
b'Naomi Gordon-Loebl is a writer and fellow at the Type Media Center.'

b'It is a term that goes back more than 100 years.'
b'The first reference in the The New York Times to \xe2\x80\x9cdisabled list\xe2\x80\x9d in a baseball context dates to 1887, when Dick Buckley of the Syracuse Stars was said to be on such a list.'
b'By 1915 it had become more codified, and The Times reported: \xe2\x80\x9cHere-after the league will keep a \xe2\x80\x98disabled\xe2\x80\x99 list, which provides that an injured player can be kept out of the game at least ten days, and an extra player substituted for him.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Now Major League Baseball will no longer use the phrase \xe2\x80\x9cdisabled list\xe2\x80\x9d for the roster of injured players. In the future, it will be known as the \xe2\x80\x9cinjured list.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The change, first reported by ESPN, came after a push by advocates for disabled people, who objected to the term.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe disability community identifies with the term \xe2\x80\x98disabled,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d said Jay Ruderman, president of the Ruderman Family Foundation, which encourages greater inclusion of people with disabilities in society. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen it\xe2\x80\x99s used incorrectly, when someone is injured, not disabled, that\xe2\x80\x99s offensive.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cPeople with disabilities do not consider themselves injured,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, noting that disabled people like the former Yankee Jim Abbott, a pitcher with one hand, had played baseball successfully. \xe2\x80\x9cSomeone who tears an A.C.L. is not permanently disabled.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Link20, a group of young activists sponsored by the Ruderman foundation, sent a letter to M.L.B. in November raising the issue. \xe2\x80\x9cUsing the term \xe2\x80\x98disabled list\xe2\x80\x99 for players who are injured reinforces the belief that people with disabilities are injured and therefore are not able to participate or compete in any sports,\xe2\x80\x9d it said.'
b'Ruderman said there had been little opposition from M.L.B. \xe2\x80\x9cThey said, \xe2\x80\x98Hey, you\xe2\x80\x99re right, this needs to be changed.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cBaseball is hugely influential on American culture,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x99 he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is really refreshing that a major sport really got the issue very quickly. It\xe2\x80\x99s a huge win for the disability community. Things like this really help break down stigma.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The key connection for the group was Billy Bean, a vice president at Major League Baseball who works on issues of inclusion. Bean said in a statement that the change was \xe2\x80\x9can example of our determination to get better off the field in the same way we try to improve on the field.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'"He has some power in the organization,\xe2\x80\x9d Ruderman said. \xe2\x80\x9cUsually the person dealing with inclusion is powerless in the organization.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Some fans on Twitter lamented the change, complaining that they could no longer use the well-known abbreviation \xe2\x80\x9cD.L.\xe2\x80\x9d But a good number welcomed the switch.'

b'By Richard Goldstein'
b'The Brooklyn Dodgers traded for the Chicago Cubs\xe2\x80\x99 All-Star Randy Jackson in December 1955, hoping he would become their regular third baseman.'
b'Injuries limited his playing time during the next two seasons, but on Sept. 28, 1957, when he delivered a three-run homer against the Phillies in Philadelphia, Jackson became a footnote in the history of a storied franchise.'
b'Jackson, who died on Wednesday morning at 93 at his home in Athens, Ga., was the last Brooklyn Dodger to hit a home run.'
b'The Dodgers were playing their next-to-last game when Jackson\xe2\x80\x99s drive landed in the upper left-field stands at Connie Mack Stadium off a pitch from the right-hander Don Cardwell, propelling Brooklyn to an 8-4 victory in a series that followed the Dodgers\xe2\x80\x99 final home games at Ebbets Field.'
b'Jackson was an unlikely candidate for his melancholy achievement: He had hit only one other home run that season, and he played in just 48 games while hobbled by a leg injury. And that home run seemed nothing special to him when the third-place Dodgers closed out their history the next day with a 2-1 loss to the Phillies, the last Brooklyn pitch delivered by an unproven left-hander named Sandy Koufax.'
b'Earlier in that summer of \xe2\x80\x9957, when the Dodgers were on the brink of moving to Los Angeles and the New York Giants seemed headed to San Francisco, the Brooklyn-born comedian Phil Foster took up a lost cause with his rendition of \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s Keep the Dodgers in Brooklyn\xe2\x80\x9d on Coral Records.'
b'The song, foreshadowing the borough of Brooklyn\xe2\x80\x99s fate if its heroes fled to the West Coast, included a brief tribute to Jackson and the outfielder Gino Cimoli, though their Dodger careers were modest:'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cBrooklyn would be like a pair of socks that\xe2\x80\x99s holey/ Without Jackson and Cimoli.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Jackson, in fact, had his best major league seasons playing for the Cubs from 1950 to 1955. He was a two-time All-Star with Chicago, hit 59 home runs over his final three seasons there and played alongside the future Hall of Fame shortstop Ernie Banks.'
b'The Dodgers obtained Jackson in a multiplayer deal, expecting him to take over at third base from an aging Jackie Robinson. He arrived with a reputation for lacking fire, but as he told The Miami News at his first Dodger spring training camp, \xe2\x80\x9cMy voice doesn\xe2\x80\x99t carry more than six feet before it drops off, so what\xe2\x80\x99s the use?\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'He did have his moments before that parting home run.'
b'In June 1956, Jackson combined with Duke Snider and Gil Hodges in a barrage of three consecutive ninth-inning home runs to lift the Dodgers to a victory over the Phillies at Ebbets Field. Jackson appeared in his only World Series that fall, going 0 for 3 as a pinch-hitter when the Dodgers lost to the Yankees in seven games.'
b'Robinson retired after the 1956 season. Pee Wee Reese, the Dodgers\xe2\x80\x99 longtime shortstop, shared third base with Jackson, Charlie Neal and Don Zimmer in 1957, the Dodgers\xe2\x80\x99 last year in Brooklyn.'
b'Ransom Joseph Jackson Jr. was born on Feb. 10, 1926, in Little Rock, Ark., the son of Ransom Sr. and Ann Polk Coolidge Jackson. His father, a former Princeton University baseball captain, prospered in the 1920s running a cotton brokerage business with two partners and offices in New York, London and Paris. After the business failed in the stock market crash of 1929, he sold life insurance. The Yankee catcher Bill Dickey was a family friend.'
b'Randy won three Southwest Conference batting titles and played halfback in two Cotton Bowl games, for Texas Christian in 1945 and the University of Texas in 1946, while enrolled in World War II-era naval officer training programs on their campuses.'
b'He was signed by the Cubs in 1947 and made his debut for them in May 1950. Along the way he acquired the name Handsome Ransom. (\xe2\x80\x9cThe nickname has less to do with looks and more to do with a sportswriter looking for something to rhyme with Ransom,\xe2\x80\x9d he wrote in a 2016 memoir, \xe2\x80\x9cHandsome Ransom Jackson: Accidental Big Leaguer,\xe2\x80\x9d written with Gaylon H. White.)'
b'He remained with the Dodgers when they moved to Los Angeles in 1958, was sent to Cleveland late that season, then finished his career as a Cub again in 1959. A right-handed batter, he hit 103 home runs and had a career batting average of .261. During the off-seasons he worked in his family\xe2\x80\x99s laundry business and sold life insurance.'
b'Jackson\xe2\x80\x99s first marriage, to Ruth Fowler, ended in divorce. He is survived by his second wife, Terry (Yeargan) Jackson; two sons, Randy and Chuck, and a daughter, Ann Bolton, from his first marriage; two daughters, Ginny Bunn and Meredith Jackson-Parton, and a son, Ransom, from his second marriage; 13 grandchildren; and eight great-grandchildren. His son Randy said the cause of death was pneumonia.'
b'Jackson, who owned an insurance agency in Athens after his playing days, still received autograph requests in his late years, though he said some of the letter writers thought they were reaching the musician Randy Jackson, a longtime judge on \xe2\x80\x9cAmerican Idol.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'That final Brooklyn Dodger home run had never registered with Jackson until he got a phone call from one of his sons in the late 1990s.'
b'As Jackson related it to The Athens Banner-Herald: \xe2\x80\x9cHe told me that \xe2\x80\x98Good Morning America\xe2\x80\x99 had a trivia question: Who was the last Brooklyn Dodger to hit a home run? It was me. I did not know that myself till then.\xe2\x80\x9d'
