b'TAMPA, Fla. \xe2\x80\x94 James Paxton is a starting pitcher for the Yankees, not a statistics innovator. But during the 2018 season with the Seattle Mariners, before his off-season trade to New York, he found a novel way to measure his efficiency and aggressiveness on the mound.'
b'Paxton, 30, asked the Mariners\xe2\x80\x99 analytics department to give him a printout of a statistic he was tracking himself: the percentage of at-bats that, after three pitches, were either in a 1-2 count or already completed. He named the statistic A3P\xe2\x80\x94 After 3 Pitches \xe2\x80\x94 and monitors it often, rather than relying on more conventional statistics such as earned run average or wins and losses.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t control some of those things,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI try to focus on the things I can control. And I can control if I\xe2\x80\x99m attacking the hitter.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Baseball is saturated with statistics. Want to know which hitters do the best against low fastballs over 95 miles per hour from left-handed relief pitchers? Want to know which outfielders cover the most ground or what percent of the time a similar play resulted in an out? Those can be found in a matter of clicks online.'
b'As the ways to quantify what happens on the field continually evolve, so has the debate over which measurements are best. In modern baseball circles, many traditional statistics no longer hold the same power.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think batting averages are a thing now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luke Voit, 28, a Yankees first baseman.'
b'In the Yankees\xe2\x80\x99 clubhouse, there is a wide range of ways in which players evaluate themselves through statistics, some relying on the conventional measures with others leaning toward the contemporary. A casual poll of players provided a window into their thinking and which skills they value \xe2\x80\x94 or don\xe2\x80\x99t.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t look at anything,\xe2\x80\x9d said C. C. Sabathia, 38, who is entering the 19th and final season of a distinguished career that includes a Cy Young Award and six All-Star selections.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand WHIP,\xe2\x80\x9d he added about an oft-used statistic that adds walks and hits before dividing that total by innings pitched. \xe2\x80\x9cAll I know is E.R.A., wins and losses. I\xe2\x80\x99m old school in that sense.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Luis Severino, 25, the Yankees\xe2\x80\x99 injured ace, may have grown up in a different era of baseball, but he feels the same way. His go-to statistics for self-evaluation are E.R.A. and innings pitched, two categories in which he has led the Yankees\xe2\x80\x99 rotation the past two seasons.'
b'His fellow starter J. A. Happ, 36, said he follows win-loss records and E.R.A. because they are ubiquitous. But over the years, he has found himself gravitating toward WHIP after a conversation with R. A. Dickey, a former Cy Young Award winner, when they were both Toronto Blue Jays.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt started to make sense that generally speaking \xe2\x80\x94 although numbers can prove anything \xe2\x80\x94 that, if you\xe2\x80\x99re doing a good job of keeping the walks and hits down, then you\xe2\x80\x99re doing pretty good over all,\xe2\x80\x9d Happ said.'
b'During a recent spring training meeting, Yankees coaches stressed the need to be aggressive. Paxton takes this to heart: He is among the best in baseball at throwing strikes. In addition to his A3P statistic, Paxton checks on the percentage of first pitches in each plate appearance that were strikes. His goal is to reach at least 70 percent; he was at 66 percent last season, according to Baseball-Reference.com.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cBy thinking about this often, and if I can do it consistently, I\xe2\x80\x99m putting myself in a good spot to have success,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.'
b'As for offensive statistics, outfielder Aaron Judge, the Yankees\xe2\x80\x99 best all-around hitter, regularly checks two statistics during the season to see if he is doing his job as one of the team\xe2\x80\x99s main sluggers: runs and R.B.I. And he will examine how well he did with runners on base or in scoring position.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the name of the game: scoring more runs than the other team,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.'
b'But the R.B.I. statistic has been de-emphasized by modern baseball because it is as much a product of a team\xe2\x80\x99s lineup as the individual hitter\xe2\x80\x99s skill. Put Judge, 26, in the worst lineup in baseball last season and he would have a harder time cracking the cherished 100-R.B.I. plateau.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt still shows value to me,\xe2\x80\x9d Judge said. \xe2\x80\x9cFor me, I always look at the guys that are leading in R.B.I. on their team, and usually those teams are doing better.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Another measure Judge cited seemed counterintuitive. At 6 foot 7, Judge is one of the tallest position players in baseball history. But he prides himself on being faster than you would think; 374 players were slower than him last season, according to Major League Baseball\xe2\x80\x99s Statcast data. Judge keeps tabs on how often he is scoring from second base on a single or advancing from first to third as a base runner.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIf you look all over the game, you\xe2\x80\x99ll see a lot of guys just go first to second,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a lot more chances to go first to third than people think. You just got to be aggressive.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Judge\xe2\x80\x99s fellow slugger, Giancarlo Stanton, prefers to examine which of his hitting tendencies opposing pitchers might exploit. When scrutinizing his performance with runners in scoring position, he said, he examines his mental approach rather than a popular statistic like on-base-plus-slugging percentage (O.P.S.).'
b'Stanton, 29, does check his chase rate, a phrase for the percentage of times a batter swings at pitches outside the strike zone. During Stanton\xe2\x80\x99s first season with the Yankees in 2018 after a trade from the Miami Marlins, his chase rate jumped more than five percentage points to nearly 33 percent from the previous season, according to FanGraphs.com. That may have contributed to his decline in overall power production.'
b'Several players said they did not focus on any preferred statistics.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIf you look only at R.B.I., then maybe you don\xe2\x80\x99t hit much in terms of average \xe2\x80\x94 or the reverse,\xe2\x80\x9d said catcher Gary Sanchez, 26, who is hoping to bounce back from the worst all-around season of his career and return to his 2017 All-Star form. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all important. You try to do it all.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Others said they avoided statistics entirely. Relief pitcher Dellin Betances, 30, said he knew exactly how he was performing without the numbers to break it down. Troy Tulowitzki, 34, the Yankees\xe2\x80\x99 primary shortstop while Didi Gregorius recovers from elbow surgery, cited an unheard-of statistic: \xe2\x80\x9cI look at a winning player.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Tulowitzki pointed to the Yankees\xe2\x80\x99 new infielder D. J. LeMahieu, 30, a former teammate from their days with the Colorado Rockies, as one. LeMahieu, a career .298 hitter who once won a batting title, loosely keeps tabs on the quality of his at-bats and his strikeouts.'
b'Some players, like relief pitchers Chad Green, 27, and Adam Ottavino, 33, have adapted well to the data revolution in baseball.'
b'Even closer Yankees closer Aroldis Chapman, 31, an advanced statistics neophyte, said he has learned since his half season with the Chicago Cubs in 2016 that one applies well to him: his outings usually turn out better when he gets the first batter out. He has tracked it himself since.'
b'Ottavino, though, is on his own level. During the season, he checks two statistics he believes reflect how he is throwing: first-strike and walk percentages. At the end of the season, he dives deeply into how he threw the ball, using advanced statistics like xwOBA (expected weight on-base average), which in layman\xe2\x80\x99s terms, essentially measures the quality of the contact made against him.'
b'He labeled other statistics, like E.R.A. or the scoring percentage of inherited base runners, as faulty.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re trying to strip away all the luck factors so you can be as real with yourself as possible,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you\xe2\x80\x99re not doing that, you\xe2\x80\x99re going to ride the roller coaster really bad, and you\xe2\x80\x99re lying to yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Even if some of Ottavino\xe2\x80\x99s teammates are not quite there yet in terms of the game\xe2\x80\x99s advanced numbers, the Yankees\xe2\x80\x99 robust analytics department is there to help them all break down the multitude of statistics in the game. The players just have to ask.'

b'Supported by'
b'By Kevin Draper'
b'The best way to understand Mike Trout, the outfielder for baseball\xe2\x80\x99s Los Angeles Angels, who on Tuesday reportedly agreed to the richest contract in North American sports, is as the perfect reflection of his sport.'
b'Little known to many Americans, including some sports fans, Trout, 27, is the best player in a game that has frequently been mocked as a fading form of entertainment with primarily regional audiences.'
b'Baseball is often dismissed as far less enticing than the N.F.L., which is believed to bring in more than $14 billion annually even with its health and safety issues. Baseball also is called less dynamic than the N.B.A., even though it had about $2 billion less in revenue than M.L.B. last year.'
b'So when Trout\xe2\x80\x99s 12-year, $430 million contract was revealed, it prompted an obvious question: How could a sport that is struggling to attract a new generation of fans, and that is facing a potentially bruising labor fight with its players in two years, afford yet another nine-figure contract?'
b'News of Trout\xe2\x80\x99s deal came about three weeks after outfielder Bryce Harper accepted a 13-year, $330 million contract  with the Phillies and a month after infielder Manny Machado got a 10-year, $300 million deal from the San Diego Padres.'
b'A comparison of all the Major League Baseball players who signed contracts of at least $100 million.'
b'The answer has more to do with the mechanics of the collective bargaining agreement between players and owners than with anything that might constitute a referendum on the game\xe2\x80\x99s popularity. There are several dynamics at work in the sport, where huge wealth still flows to the best of the best, while there is less money left over for the so-called middle class, despite continuing growth in baseball\xe2\x80\x99s revenues.'
b'Owners, it seems, have figured out how to keep costs down by using younger players, who are cheaper than veterans, thus freeing up cash for the occasional megadeal. Players like Trout, Harper and Machado benefit, while others are forced to accept less.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIn the past, teams were valuing players differently,\xe2\x80\x9d said James Paxton, a starting pitcher for the Yankees. \xe2\x80\x9cFor a long time, that system was fine. Now that the teams have changed the way they\xe2\x80\x99re valuing players, especially older players, it\xe2\x80\x99s kind of broken the system and it is forcing us to take a look at it.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'In a study that has not yet been published, J.C. Bradbury, an economics professor at Kennesaw State University, calculated that baseball revenue rose at an average rate of 6.6 percent from 2003 to 2015, while player salaries rose at an average rate of just 4.4 percent.'
b'Major League Baseball revenues reached $10.3 billion last year. The sources include evermore money from guaranteed sources, like multibillion-dollar media deals for the entire sport and other lucrative media contracts, many at the local level. Less important have been ticket sales, which generally fluctuate with wins and losses.'
b'With the shift in valuation of players, the average salary went down in 2018 for the first time in 14 years (to a little more than $4 million), according to the players association. It was just the fourth time in the last 50 years that the average has gone down.'
b'Dan Halem, the deputy commissioner of M.L.B., said there was no evidence of teams not trying to win, and he painted a very different picture of baseball\xe2\x80\x99s economics.'
b'Salaries have gone up on average 5.3 percent each year since 2012, keeping pace with baseball\xe2\x80\x99s revenue growth during that time, he said, and while the pay may have been slightly down or flat in 2018, one year does not make a trend. He also said that over the last decade the players had received a consistent percentage of the game\xe2\x80\x99s revenue, from 52 to 57 percent.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIn a market-based system, depending upon the year and how our clubs are valuing players,\xe2\x80\x9d said Halem, \xe2\x80\x9ccertain players do better than others.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Yet words like \xe2\x80\x9ccollusion\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cstrike\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x94 once the third rails of the sport, especially after the ruinous strike of 1994-1995 \xe2\x80\x94 are now regularly uttered by players and agents \xe2\x80\x94 despite the huge contracts.'
b'Focusing on giant deals misses the bigger picture, according to Jeff Borris, a longtime baseball agent who has represented stars like Barry Bonds, and is now the general counsel of the Ballengee Group, a sports representation agency.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cContracts like Trout\xe2\x80\x99s and Harper\xe2\x80\x99s are aberrations,\xe2\x80\x9d Borris said. \xe2\x80\x9cThe focus should be more on things like why isn\xe2\x80\x99t Dallas Keuchel signed or the contract that Gio Gonzalez just signed. Those are the areas that are of concern.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Keuchel, a two-time All-Star starting pitcher for the Houston Astros, remained unsigned one week before opening day, while Gonzalez, a solid, durable starting pitcher, has signed a minor-league deal with the Yankees.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI think the owners are manipulating the system to their advantage,\xe2\x80\x9d Borris said.'
b'For decades, baseball players have been represented by what is widely considered to be the strongest union in all of professional sports. As a result, baseball has no limits on salaries, though its luxury tax, which penalizes a team after its payroll rises above $206 million, can function as a de facto cap.'
b'The problem now, at least as articulated by players, is that while teams reward superstars, they no longer feel compelled to spend to win if they do not believe they already have the pieces in place to compete for a championship.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWhy is there not an interest in players that can help teams win, particularly as we get to the last two weeks of spring training?\xe2\x80\x9d Tony Clark, the executive director of the players association, said before the Trout deal was reached.'
b'Despite baseball\xe2\x80\x99s strong union, certain provisions that kept salaries rising for decades turned out to be norms, not hard and fast rules.'
b'Baseball players do not become free agents until they accrue six years of service time. They are generally paid at the league minimum for their first three seasons, while in the next three they can receive higher salaries through negotiation or arbitration. This leads to odd situations like Trout\xe2\x80\x99s being voted the American League most valuable player in his third full season while earning just $1 million.'
b'This used to be acceptable to players because they made up for it on the back end of their careers. There are the infamous albatross contracts given to veterans, like the $125 million extension signed by Ryan Howard in 2010, but most of the time it was veteran players being paid a fair wage, or being slightly overpaid, well into their 30s, and sometimes 40s.'
b'It\xe2\x80\x99s not a coincidence that Trout, Harper and Machado all got their megadeals at the age of 27 or younger. But as the circumstances of older players like Gonzalez and Keuchel demonstrate, teams have been working on the assumption that a veteran\xe2\x80\x99s abilities are likely to decline soon after his salary peaks and that it makes sense to use younger players, who can fill out a roster at lower costs and risks \xe2\x80\x94 even if the chance to make the playoffs is also reduced.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think that we thought that teams all of a sudden were going to just stop competing to the degree that they are now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Adam Ottavino, a Yankees pitcher. \xe2\x80\x9cA lot of things changed really fast.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Email Kevin Draper at kevin.draper@nytimes.com or follow him on Twitter: @kevinmdraper.'
b'Tyler Kepner and James Wagner contributed reporting.'

b'Sports of The Times'
b'By Michael Powell'
b'So the mighty Frank Robinson is dead at 83.'
b'As a young kid, I was struck by the man, tall, strong, so coolly menacing at the plate, and the statistics and awards that underlined all that the eye took in: those 586 pre-steroidal home runs and the only man who won the Most Valuable Player Award in both leagues.'
b'And there were all those times he was knocked down and dusted himself off and hit a home run on the next pitch. He practically sneered as he trotted around the bases.'
b'As Sports Illustrated wrote in the 1960s, pitchers figured \xe2\x80\x9cthe only way to deal with Robinson is to hit him before he hits you.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'He was also the first black manager in baseball. He was given a crappy team, of course, an old Buick station wagon of a Cleveland Indians squad. You thought white owners would give the first black manager the keys to a Mercedes? He played designated hitter on that team and at age 39 hit nine home runs with a .508 slugging percentage in 118 at-bats.'
b'On opening day of that year, 1975, Robinson the player hit a home run for Robinson the manager.'
b'And as with all things African-American and Major League Baseball, Robinson looms as a figure out of the Mesozoic Age. Less than 8 percent of major league players are African-American, according to the Institute for Diversity and Ethics in Sport, down from a high of more than 18 percent. More damning still, there is but one manager who could be classified as African-American. Dave Roberts of the Los Angeles Dodgers is of African-American and Japanese descent (There are four Latino managers).'
b'There are 25 white managers.'
b'This is a deeply perplexing shortfall, and as it persists year after year after year like the winter and summer solstices, one can safely assume that something more than dumb chance accounts for this problem. We might look to the fact that those doing the hiring, owners and team presidents and general managers, are overwhelmingly white.'
b'Baseball men, and the vast majority are male, tend to twin talk of a dearth of minority managers with talk about trends toward analytics, as if that explains something. As Ivy League guys rule many front offices, it is said, they naturally seek managerial candidates fluent in O.B.P., WHIP, FIP, and so on and on, the arcana and language of analytics.'
b'This is silliness.'
b'Last fall, the Boston Red Sox played the Dodgers in the World Series. The manager of the Red Sox, Alex Cora, was Latino and there was the aforementioned Roberts in the other dugout. They had bested a passel of white-managed teams on the way to that affair, not one of whom possessed an advanced mathematics degree from Caltech.'
b'I put the questions of baseball analytics to Joey Cora, brother of Alex and baseball coach for decades. He had interviewed several times for manager without making the final cut.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cEveryone in baseball understands the importance of statistics and analysis; we\xe2\x80\x99re saturated with that,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s no secret anymore. You also need to know baseball and how to lead men and how to combine all of that.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWhy wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t we understand that?\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'That was a good question last October and a good question still.'
b'Robinson managed in the pre-analytics era, and few who knew him doubted his necessities when it came to understanding the facets of that game. As the Cincinnati Reds scout George Powles told Sports Illustrated, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what his I.Q. is, but his B.Q., his baseball quotient, has always been genius.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'His will was indomitable. In 1965, the Reds traded him to the Baltimore Orioles, his general manager calling him \xe2\x80\x9can old 30.\xe2\x80\x9d The next year, his first in the American League, he won the Triple Crown, hitting .316 with 49 home runs and 122 R.B.I. That May, he faced Luis Tiant, a brilliant fireball pitcher with the Indians and a man who didn\xe2\x80\x99t mind brushing back a hitter.'
b'Robinson hit a Tiant pitch 541 feet, making him the first and only man ever to hit a home run entirely out of Memorial Stadium in Baltimore. The Orioles planted a flag labeled \xe2\x80\x9cHERE\xe2\x80\x9d on the spot where the ball, still in the stratosphere, left the stadium.'
b'Baseball men tend to go on and on about their plans to attract great African-American athletes back to baseball. They might note that their lamentations coexist with a large number of retired black baseball players who never got a chance to manage baseball teams.'
b'Bump your head against a ceiling long enough, and maybe you decide there are better things to do than develop a bruise. Perhaps best to start hiring black managers and planting a flag that says HERE.'

b'By Matt Stevens'
b'Larry Baer, the chief executive of the San Francisco Giants, has been suspended without pay until July over a video that shows him involved in a public altercation with his wife, the commissioner of Major League Baseball announced on Tuesday.'
b'Mr. Baer has been on a leave of absence from the team since March 4, time away that the league said would now be converted to an unpaid suspension. During the approximately four-month suspension, which goes through July 1, Mr. Baer \xe2\x80\x9cshall have no involvement in the operations of the Giants.\xe2\x80\x9d The team will be put under the control of someone selected by the team\xe2\x80\x99s ownership group, a statement from Major League Baseball said.'
b'In the meantime, Mr. Baer must be evaluated by an expert to determine an appropriate treatment and counseling plan, the statement said.'
b'The baseball commissioner, Rob Manfred, said league investigators had reviewed video footage of the March 1 episode, in which Mr. Baer was seen lunging for a cellphone in his wife\xe2\x80\x99s hand and forcefully grabbing her. They struggled, and she fell to the ground.'
b'In a statement on Tuesday, Mr. Manfred said that he had met with Mr. Baer and that he had concluded that Mr. Baer\xe2\x80\x99s conduct was \xe2\x80\x9cunacceptable under M.L.B. policies and warrants discipline.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIn determining the appropriate level of discipline, I find that Mr. Baer should be held to to a higher standard because as a leader he is expected to be a role model for others in his organization and community,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Manfred said. \xe2\x80\x9cBased on my conversation with Mr. Baer, it is clear he regrets what transpired and takes responsibility for his conduct.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The board of the San Francisco Giants, speaking on behalf of the team\xe2\x80\x99s ownership, said in a statement that it believed the discipline imposed by the commissioner was appropriate. The team said that its acting chief executive, Rob Dean, would serve as the interim \xe2\x80\x9ccontrol person\xe2\x80\x9d until it designates someone permanently.'
b'Mr. Baer, who in addition to leading the Giants\xe2\x80\x99 executive office is one of the team\xe2\x80\x99s principal owners, will continue to serve as the team\xe2\x80\x99s chief executive and president after he returns from his suspension, the team added.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI respect and accept the commissioner\xe2\x80\x99s decision,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Baer said in his own statement. \xe2\x80\x9cI made a serious mistake that I sincerely regret and I am truly sorry for my actions.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The punishment came as the sports world waits to see how the N.F.L.\xe2\x80\x99s commissioner will reprimand the owner of the New England Patriots, Robert K. Kraft, who has been accused of soliciting prostitution at a massage parlor in Jupiter, Fla. Mr. Kraft pleaded not guilty to two first-degree misdemeanor charges late last month.'
b'The situation involving Mr. Baer and the allegations against Mr. Kraft have drawn attention to how team owners and executives are punished after being accused of misconduct, compared with players, for whom fines and suspensions may carry a greater impact.'
b'After video of the encounter, which was first posted by TMZ Sports, surfaced publicly this month, Mr. Baer and his wife, Pam, initially issued statements denying that a physical altercation had taken place. The video appeared to show them arguing in a public plaza. Ms. Baer sat in a chair, and her husband stood next to her. And then Mr. Baer was seen lunging toward her, grabbing her and setting off the struggle, during which she fell to the ground and screamed, \xe2\x80\x9cOh my God!\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The police and Major League Baseball quickly pledged to investigate what had taken place and Mr. Baer apologized. Three days after the confrontation, the Giants announced that Mr. Baer would take a leave of absence from his job as chief executive.'
b'In mid-March, a coalition of women\xe2\x80\x99s groups posted an open letter online that called on Mr. Manfred to commit to \xe2\x80\x9cfirm and appropriate action in disciplining\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Baer. The mayor of San Francisco, London N. Breed, endorsed the call for a serious penalty.'
b'After the suspension was announced, the coalition, #DoWhatsRightMLB, said it appreciated Mr. Manfred\xe2\x80\x99s reprimand.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cM.L.B. is showing why it is regarded as a leader in advancing cultural change in professional sports by not tolerating violence against women,\xe2\x80\x9d the coalition said in a statement.'
b'Ms. Breed agreed, saying in a statement that the league had \xe2\x80\x9csent a clear message that violence against women is unacceptable in any form.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Last week, the San Francisco District Attorney\xe2\x80\x99s Office determined that the evidence in the case did not support filing criminal charges against Mr. Baer, a spokesman said Tuesday.'
b'Mr. Baer joined the Giants as the club\xe2\x80\x99s marketing director in 1980, left the team to attend Harvard Business School and then served in positions at Westinghouse Broadcasting and CBS, according to a biography.'
b'He returned to the team as a limited partner of the ownership group, and was named chief operating officer in May 1996, president in October 2008 and chief executive in January 2012.'

b'By Reuters'
b"WASHINGTON \xe2\x80\x94 The U.S. Federal Aviation Administration said on Friday it was investigating reports that a drone flew over Boston's Fenway Park during a baseball game, and the drone's manufacturer said the user apparently overrode a system designed to prevent flights over prohibited areas such as sporting events."
b"The Boston Red Sox said on Friday that a drone flew over the playing field during the late innings of Thursday's evening game against the Toronto Blue Jays. FAA regulations prohibit flying drones around stadiums during major sporting events."
b'Footage aired on TV showed the drone hovering over the playing field. The club said it "reported the incident to the Boston Police Department for investigation."'
b'The drone appeared to be a DJI Phantom, the manufacturer said in a statement Friday.'
b'"We are trying to learn more about what happened, and stand ready to work with Boston Police and other security agencies to investigate this incident," the U.S. unit of Shenzhen, China-based SZ DJI Technology Co Ltd said in a statement Friday.'
b'"Whoever flew this drone over the stadium apparently overrode our geofencing system and deliberately violated the FAA temporary flight restriction in place over the game."'
b'The company added the "incident shows why the federal government must mandate a remote identification system for airborne drones as soon as possible."'
b'U.S. regulators are grappling with how to address drones in unauthorized areas and integrate them into U.S. airspace.'
b'FBI deputy assistant director Scott Brunner told a Senate committee last year the agency was "concerned that criminals and terrorists will exploit (drones) in ways that pose a serious threat to the safety of the American people."'
b'Threats could include surveillance, chemical, biological or radiological attacks or attacks "on large open air venues" like concerts and sporting events and attacks against government facilities, he said.'
b'In January, the FAA said 43 flights into New Jersey\xe2\x80\x99s Newark Liberty International Airport were required to hold after a reported drone sighting at a nearby airport, while nine flights were diverted.'
b'Major U.S. airports are assessing the threat of drones and have been holding meetings to address the issue.'
b'The U.S. Congress last year gave the Department of Justice and Department of Homeland Security new powers to disable or destroy threatening drones after officials raised concerns about the use of drones as potential weapons.'
b' (Reporting by David Shepardson; editing by Chizu Nomiyama and David Gregorio)'

b'Supported by'
b'Keeping Score'
b'By Benjamin Hoffman'
b'As Bryce Harper and Manny Machado, both 26, reached free agency this off-season, there was a drumbeat of declarations about how rare it was for baseball players so good and so young to hit the open market. Many expected the confluence to shatter previous records for free agent contracts.'
b'Yet without nearly as much fanfare, Nolan Arenado, the star third baseman for the Colorado Rockies, appears close to a deal for the highest annual salary for a position player in baseball, even though he has not become a free agent.'
b'Multiple news outlets on Tuesday reported that Arenado and the Rockies were on the verge of an eight-year contract extension that would pay him more per season than the $30 million annual average that Machado will receive in his 10-year deal with the San Diego Padres. And depending on the numbers in a deal for Harper, who is still on the market, Arenado could top him, as well.'
b'But even if Arenado breaks the record for a position player \xe2\x80\x94 with, according to the reports, an average annual value of $32.5 million \xe2\x80\x94 Harper\xe2\x80\x99s and Machado\xe2\x80\x99s representatives should view that extension as further evidence of their clients\xe2\x80\x99 extraordinary status.'
b'In a sport flush with cash from savvy off-field business moves, teams typically lock up young players before they hit free agency, as the Rockies just did, so the chance to acquire Machado or Harper for cash alone has truly been extraordinary.'
b'A comparison of all the Major League Baseball players who signed contracts of at least $100 million.'
b'However, if Harper wants to end up with the top contract of this off-season, he should hope to avoid any other comparisons between his performance and Arenado\xe2\x80\x99s.'
b'Arenado, who will turn 28 in April, is 18 months older than Harper and 15 months older than Machado. And he has established himself as the face of the Rockies\xe2\x80\x99 franchise, while his production has been nothing less than sensational.'
b'Aided somewhat by Denver\xe2\x80\x99s thin air, Arenado hit .297 with a .358 on-base percentage and .573 slugging percentage from 2015 through 2018. He hit 158 home runs \xe2\x80\x94 leading the National League in three of those four seasons \xe2\x80\x94 and even after adjusting for his home park, he has generated 25.3 wins above replacement, placing him fourth in the majors behind Mike Trout, Mookie Betts and Jose Altuve in that span.'
b'A perennial candidate for the National League\xe2\x80\x99s Most Valuable Player Award, Arenado has been having the type of career many predicted for Harper.'
b'Since high school, Harper had been viewed as baseball\xe2\x80\x99s chosen one. He was selected with the first overall pick in the 2010 draft, and his LeBron James-like hype was seemingly justified in 2015, when he produced 10 wins above replacement for the 22nd time in N.L. history \xe2\x80\x94 and the first by someone other than Barry Bonds or Sammy Sosa since 1975.'
b'But in the three years since then, as Harper prepared for a free agency payday that at one point had people speculating about a $500 million deal, he has a combined WAR of just 7.5.'
b'Arenado, who was selected with the 59th pick of the 2009 draft and was known more for his glove than for his bat over his first few seasons, has produced 19.4 WAR in the same three-year period.'
b'They have been fairly similar offensively. Arenado\xe2\x80\x99s production, when adjusted for his home park, has been 31 percent better than the league average, while Harper\xe2\x80\x99s as an outfielder has been 33 percent better. But Arenado has been more durable, and the gulf between their defensive value is extreme: Arenado has produced 5.4 defensive WAR and has won a Gold Glove in each of those three seasons, while Harper, at a far less vital position, has generated an ugly minus-4.2 defensive WAR.'
b'If Arenado had made it to free agency next season, there is no telling what type of offer he might have received. He would have been nearly 29 and would have the taint of Coors Field prompting questions about whether his success could be replicated elsewhere. But he also would be on the (very) short list of baseball\xe2\x80\x99s best all-around players, a claim that neither Harper nor Machado could truly make.'
b'Harper, though, should benefit greatly from Arenado\xe2\x80\x99s being off the market for eight years. Any teams that had designs on signing Colorado\xe2\x80\x99s franchise player next year will be forced to look elsewhere.'
b'And Harper, alone at the top of the market, is not only the best remaining free agent but also, accounting for his age and his ceiling, the best that will be along for the next several seasons. He will undoubtedly be paid on a similar level as Arenado, and the only open question will be if he can get back to playing like him, too.'

b'Keeping Score'
b'With 3,089 hits in the majors and 1,278 in Japan, some believe Suzuki surpassed Pete Rose. It is more complicated than that.'
b'By Benjamin Hoffman'
b'How many hits does Ichiro Suzuki have? It seems like a simple question, but the answer, like everything in Suzuki\xe2\x80\x99s world, is not simple.'
b'Some numbers are clear. After starting this season 0 for 5, Suzuki announced his retirement on Thursday, leaving him with 3,089 hits across parts of 19 major league seasons. In Japan, where he began his career, he had 1,278 hits in nine seasons.'
b'Those totals have him ranked 23rd in the majors \xe2\x80\x94 21 hits behind Dave Winfield and seven ahead of Albert Pujols \xe2\x80\x94 and 95th in Japan. But it has become common to simply combine the totals, crediting him with 4,367 career hits, thus pushing him past Pete Rose as baseball\xe2\x80\x99s hit king.'
b'That is where things get murky. It\xe2\x80\x99s widely understood that Nippon Professional Baseball, where Suzuki starred for the Orix BlueWave, can produce top-tier major league talent, but the league over all is not on the same level as M.L.B. So to simply add on those hits, free of adjustment or context, could be seen as akin to including a player\xe2\x80\x99s minor league statistics.'
b'Once you do that \xe2\x80\x94 allowing, of course, for the fact that there is no way to compare the quality of various minor and international leagues across eras with any degree of certainty \xe2\x80\x94 Suzuki falls behind Rose, just edging Ty Cobb for second place.'
b'With Suzuki falling short, either way, it is easier to view his hit totals separately, without trying to find a way to combine them. Rose remains the hit king, especially from the perspective of people in the United States. Similarly, Barry Bonds is considered the home run champion with 762, despite Sadaharu Oh hitting 868 in Japan during a 22-season career that ended in 1980.'
b'Still, that does not mean Suzuki was a lesser hitter than Rose. A .311 hitter in the majors and a .353 hitter in Japan, Suzuki is perhaps the greatest spray hitter in history regardless of league. He hit for very little power \xe2\x80\x94 despite being known for prodigious blasts in batting practice \xe2\x80\x94 and drew relatively few walks. Yet despite spending nine years of his pro career in a different league, he will retire with the sixth-most singles in major league history, having outdistanced masters of the art like Rod Carew and Tony Gwynn.'
b'In addition to his line drives, Suzuki was a top-notch defender and an above-average base stealer, giving him a skill set that sticks out among his peers. By similarity score \xe2\x80\x94 a statistic developed by the statistician Bill James to sort out which players are statistically similar, with adjustments made for position \xe2\x80\x94 Carew\xe2\x80\x99s career is the closest one to Suzuki\xe2\x80\x99s, at 849.8 points out of a possible 1,000. But the list of the 10 players most similar to Suzuki includes Max Carey, Doc Cramer and Harry Hooper. In all, six of those 10 made their debut before 1930. The only players of the last 30 years to show up on the list are Kenny Lofton and Gwynn.'
b'Off the field, Suzuki, through a combination of his personality and his insistence that his interpreters provide literal translations from his Japanese to English, has long been known for delivering spectacular quotations. He did not disappoint when asked by Sports Illustrated in 2002 to describe his career and his instant fame upon joining the Mariners in 2001.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m unique,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m a very rare kind of player.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'His distinctive achievements should be further secured in a few years, when he will almost certainly become the first player to be inducted into the halls of fame in both Japan and the United States. '
b'He does not need to be baseball\xe2\x80\x99s hit king to do that. He most likely did not need his last several seasons in the majors to have secured a spot in both halls. And even ignoring his time in Japan entirely, he retires as a 10-time All-Star, a 10-time Gold Glove winner, a two-time batting champion and a one-time winner of the Most Valuable Player Award. He owns the record for hits in a single season, and the legend of his powerful throwing arm should carry on just as long as his reputation for batting exploits.'
b'He may not have surpassed Rose in hits, but to make it into the hall of fame in two countries seems to perfectly encapsulate a player who had the unique distinction of being a superstar in two leagues \xe2\x80\x94 a feat capped perfectly by his final games as a professional coming in Japan.'
b'Adding up what he did between the leagues in an accurate manner may be difficult, but declaring it to have been rare \xe2\x80\x94 to use Suzuki\xe2\x80\x99s word \xe2\x80\x94 is remarkably easy.'
b'Benjamin Hoffman is a senior staff editor and regular contributor to the Keeping Score column in sports. He joined The Times in 2005.  @BenHoffmanNYT \xe2\x80\xa2 Facebook '

b'On Baseball'
b'By Tyler Kepner'
b'WEST PALM BEACH, Fla. \xe2\x80\x94 Rob Manfred reported to spring training ready to play defense.'
b'Manfred, the commissioner of Major League Baseball, is tired of hearing complaints from players and agents about the changing economic landscape of his sport. In a news conference on Sunday, he suggested that players get over it, and that most of the unsigned free agents are not very good, anyway.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThere are 11 players that had a WAR above 1.0 that are unsigned,\xe2\x80\x9d Manfred said, referring to wins above replacement figures used by MLB.com. \xe2\x80\x9cI believe that, just like last year, that market is going to clear at some point in the next few weeks. Those players are going to get signed.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cDo I wish, if I had my way, that Scott Boras or Dan Lozano \xe2\x80\x94 whatever agent \xe2\x80\x94 would find a way to make a deal with some club sooner rather than later? Yes, I do. But we negotiated a system that allows a market to operate.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Boras represents outfielder Bryce Harper. Lozano represents infielder Manny Machado. Both players are 26 years old, with 10 All-Star selections between them. They were still unsigned on Sunday, with dozens of others, nine of whom have a WAR above 1: Jose Bautista, Clay Buchholz, Carlos Gonzalez, Gio Gonzalez, Marwin Gonzalez, Dallas Keuchel, Craig Kimbrel, Jose Iglesias and Denard Span.'
b'Teams have stopped spending like they used to, and only one free agent has signed for more than $68 million this winter \xe2\x80\x94 starter Patrick Corbin, who got a six-year, $140 million deal from the Washington Nationals. Gone are the days that a player in his 30s could get a 10-year, $240 million contract, like the one Robinson Cano got from the Seattle Mariners before the 2014 season.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m surprised,\xe2\x80\x9d Cano, who was traded to the Mets in December, said on Sunday. \xe2\x80\x9cI would say every baseball player is shocked.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'They shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be, Manfred insisted. Instead, he suggested, players should recognize that they are now valued differently by a data-driven industry less prone to the emotional decisions that once drove salaries ever higher.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI think it\xe2\x80\x99s important to remember that the Major League Baseball Players Association has always wanted a market-based system, and markets change, particularly when the institution around those markets change,\xe2\x80\x9d Manfred said. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve had a lot of change in the game. People think about players differently. They analyze players differently. They negotiate differently.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'While the Boston Red Sox had the game\xe2\x80\x99s highest payroll last season and won the World Series, Manfred cited the Tampa Bay Rays, who won 90 games with a low budget. That did not translate to big crowds \xe2\x80\x94 the Rays finished last in the American League in attendance \xe2\x80\x94 but Manfred said it proved that spending does not equate to winning.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe process of putting together a competitive team looks a little different,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cFans have to get used to that different process and have a little faith in the people that are running their clubs.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Players have been frustrated by teams choosing to build with young, cheap labor when proven veterans are available \xe2\x80\x94 for more money, of course. But the calculus that players used for decades no longer applies. That, said Nationals reliever Sean Doolittle, is a bigger issue than any perceived flaws in the collective bargaining agreement.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cOur biggest mistake, I think, was operating under the assumption that the norms that were always in place were going to continue to be respected,\xe2\x80\x9d Doolittle said on Friday. \xe2\x80\x9cLike, there was always this kind of unspoken agreement, or model, set up where for your first six years, most guys are underpaid because they don\xe2\x80\x99t really have that much leverage. You\xe2\x80\x99re locked into your minimum salary your first three years, and then when you go through the arbitration process for the next three years, there\xe2\x80\x99s no market \xe2\x80\x94 it\xe2\x80\x99s just you and one buyer, so there\xe2\x80\x99s only so much you can do to move your value. But then you make it up on the back end in free agency.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Most players have stopped short of claiming outright collusion, the illegal tactic owners used to suppress salaries in the 1980s. But many are at least suspicious of the way groupthink has taken over the industry. Adam Wainwright, a highly respected veteran starter for the St. Louis Cardinals, sounded an alarm in an interview last week with InsideSTL.com.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cUnless something changes, there\xe2\x80\x99s going to be a strike, 100 percent,\xe2\x80\x9d Wainwright said. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think anybody\xe2\x80\x99s hiding that. I\xe2\x80\x99m just worried people are going to walk out midseason.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Baseball had a prolonged midseason strike in 1981 and a devastating strike in 1994 that canceled the World Series for the first time in 90 years. That strike bled into the next season, when owners turned spring training into a farce by fielding teams of replacement players.'
b'The current collective bargaining agreement runs through 2021, and Manfred dismissed concerns about a strike this season.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt reflects a fundamental misunderstanding of how you conduct good labor relations to have people running around three years before an agreement expires, before there has been one word of negotiation, arguing that there is going to be a strike,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI actually have a degree in labor relations. We never learned that tactic. It really is not productive in terms of our business, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think it\xe2\x80\x99s good for our fans, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think it\xe2\x80\x99s good for our players. And I know it\xe2\x80\x99s not going to change the outcome of the negotiations, ultimately.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'For now, Manfred would like to negotiate on-field changes like a 20-second pitch clock, which will be used in spring training games and will most likely be adopted for the regular season. Other changes \xe2\x80\x94 such as a three-batter minimum for relief pitchers and the adoption of the designated hitter in the National League \xe2\x80\x94 will not be in place this season.'
b'Manfred would rather fans debate those topics \xe2\x80\x94 or anything else, really \xe2\x80\x94 as long as they don\xe2\x80\x99t dwell on those unsigned free agents.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI do believe that we have the greatest game in the world,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cMaybe we\xe2\x80\x99re going to make some little changes to make it even better. And I do think once we do get out there and start playing, that positive glow around the game will re-emerge.\xe2\x80\x9d'

b'An exhibition at the Museum of the City of New York \xe2\x80\x94 including photographs that have never been published before \xe2\x80\x94 shows the pioneering player with his teammates and his family.'
b'By Sopan Deb'
b'In 1947 the New York Post sportswriter Jimmy Cannon wrote that Jackie Robinson \xe2\x80\x9cis the loneliest man I have ever seen in sports.\xe2\x80\x9d Robinson had recently made his debut with the Brooklyn Dodgers \xe2\x80\x94 as a 28-year-old who had led the charge to racially integrate Major League Baseball. The isolation was real. He endured racist taunts and death threats, in addition to being shunned by some of his teammates.'
b'Yet a new photography exhibition at the Museum of the City of New York \xe2\x80\x94 \xe2\x80\x9cIn the Dugout With Jackie Robinson: An Intimate Portrait of a Baseball Legend\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x94 gives a broader view of Robinson\xe2\x80\x99s life on and off the field. In about 30 photographs, all taken in either 1949 or 1953 and mostly unpublished, he is seen surrounded by teammates and family members. The images were originally shot for Look magazine by the staff photographers Kenneth Eide and Frank Bauman. '
b'The exhibition also has rare memorabilia, like a baseball signed by the 1952 Dodgers and a glove that belonged to Robinson. A home video shows Robinson hitting baseballs to his son, Jackie Jr., and some other neighborhood children in Connecticut.'
b'[On Jackie Robinson\xe2\x80\x99s 100th Birthday, 100 Photos of an Icon]'
b'[Claire Smith: Jackie Robinson Showed Me How to Fight On, Not Fight Back]'
b'[George Vecsey: How Jackie Robinson Changed the Game]'
b'Robinson wasn\xe2\x80\x99t the first black player in the history of Major League Baseball. That distinction belongs to Moses Fleetwood Walker, a catcher for the Toledo Blue Stockings in the American Association in 1884. He only played one season because of an injury and soon after, black players were banned. But Robinson\xe2\x80\x99s legacy is unmatched: He not only inspired generations of Americans to pursue baseball but also helped black football and basketball players gain acceptance in their leagues while devoting himself to the civil rights movement before his death at the age of 53 in 1972.'
b'Robinson\xe2\x80\x99s special relationship with Look magazine is apparent in the show. He wrote three articles in 1955, including one called \xe2\x80\x9cNow I Know Why They Boo Me,\xe2\x80\x9d which can be read at the exhibition. Robinson also announced his retirement through Look, instead of at a news conference, an unusual move at the time. The magazine donated its New York archives to the museum in the 1950s, and for years the negatives and contact prints were buried in its basement. Over the last year, Sean Corcoran and Susan Gail Johnson, the exhibition\xe2\x80\x99s curators, combed through hundreds of negatives before finally picking the ones that would be on view for this small yet powerful show. '
b'Robinson is seen at home with his wife, Rachel Robinson, and his son, Jackie Jr. Many of these stills are from 1949, two years after he had entered the major leagues. Another photograph shows Robinson typing, an indication of how much Robinson wanted to be in charge of his own story. The Robinsons married in 1946, just before spring training in Florida, and that same year, Rachel gave birth to Jackie Robinson Jr. He later died in a car crash in 1971 at 24. '
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt was important to show him as a human being more than just this towering figure in baseball history,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Corcoran said. \xe2\x80\x9cTo ground him as a human being with a personal story.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The exhibition also shows Robinson late in his career, in 1953, seemingly having positive interactions with his teammates, as well as some with Robinson shirtless, which would not have been published at the time. In one of the shirtless photos, Robinson is seen talking to a white teammate in the clubhouse. Museum officials believe he is Carl Furillo, an all-star right fielder.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cHis guard is down. This is a completely intimate moment,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Corcoran said in an interview. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t know what they\xe2\x80\x99re saying, but they\xe2\x80\x99re having some sort of serious but open discussion with each other.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'These photos show Robinson perhaps a bit more at ease than he was as a rookie, partially because at this point, he had shown himself to be one of the best players in the league, which resulted in him being more accepted in the locker room.'
b'Robinson was an exceptional player. But with that came resentment from opposing teams. Opposing pitchers often threw at his body. (He was hit 72 times in his career.) In his second full year, he led the National League in hit-by-pitches with seven. In seven of his 10 seasons, he was among the top five players hit by opposing pitchers.'
b'Robinson was also a speedster. He led the National League twice in stolen bases \xe2\x80\x94 and actually stole home 19 times, which today would be unheard-of.'
b'In the Dugout With Jackie Robinson'
b'Through Sept. 15 at the Museum of the City of New York, 1220 Fifth Avenue, Manhattan; 212-534-1672, mcny.org.'
b"Sopan Deb is a culture reporter, writing about the intersection of politics and culture, among other topics. He covered Donald J. Trump's presidential campaign for CBS News, and his work has appeared on NBC, Al Jazeera America and elsewhere.  @sopandeb "

b'On Baseball'
b'By Tyler Kepner'
b'Spring training camps open next week, and while Major League Baseball would never say it, the industry badly needs a better season than last year.'
b'There were highlights, to be sure \xe2\x80\x94 there always are \xe2\x80\x94 but the problems were obvious to all: fewer fans in the seats and more hopeless teams than ever. The malaise has continued deep into the off-season, with more than 100 free agents still unsigned, including the marquee stars Bryce Harper and Manny Machado.'
b'On Wednesday, at least, it was fun to have some on-field news to debate. The commissioner\xe2\x80\x99s office and the players\xe2\x80\x99 association traded proposals last month that could lead to several significant rule changes. The designated hitter might be coming to the National League, although probably not until 2020. Pitchers might be required to face at least three batters. Rosters might be expanded to 26 in the regular season but reduced to a maximum of 28 in September.'
b'Those possibilities and more \xe2\x80\x94 reported by The Athletic and ESPN \xe2\x80\x94 were confirmed on Wednesday by multiple officials with the league and the union who were not authorized to speak publicly about continuing negotiations. Whatever comes from the proposals, it is nice to see the sides talking, undercutting the perception that a skeptical union was simply freezing out Commissioner Rob Manfred.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe players are always looking to find ways to streamline the game to make it more competitive and to put a better product for fans on the field,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chris Iannetta, the veteran catcher for the Colorado Rockies, in a telephone interview. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat we\xe2\x80\x99re against is making wholesale changes we don\xe2\x80\x99t feel are going to be meaningful.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'There is incentive for the players to engage Manfred, because the collective bargaining agreement, which runs through 2021, gives him the right to unilaterally impose some ideas he proposed last year, including a 20-second pitch clock. But Iannetta, a member of the union\xe2\x80\x99s executive subcommittee, said the players\xe2\x80\x99 primary objective was to create a sport in which more teams actively try to win.'
b'Naturally, when teams shrug at the free-agent market, it manifests itself in salaries below players\xe2\x80\x99 expectations. But the widespread rebuilding phenomenon \xe2\x80\x94 or tanking, as some call it \xe2\x80\x94 resulted in eight teams with at least 95 losses last season, the most in history. It was no coincidence that attendance also dropped by more than three million fans, falling below 70 million for the first time since 2003.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe numbers are very telling, and I think it\xe2\x80\x99s derived from the competitiveness of the individual teams,\xe2\x80\x9d Iannetta said. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s teams that can become much more competitive just from tapping into the talent pool that\xe2\x80\x99s available on the free-agent market right now, and not being willing to do that should be alarming to everybody.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s easy to say, \xe2\x80\x98We\xe2\x80\x99re going to be the Cubs or the Astros,\xe2\x80\x99 but that\xe2\x80\x99s a very difficult thing to do \xe2\x80\x94 and keep in mind the Cubs and Astros used veteran talent to groom those players, and when it was time to win, they significantly added veteran players.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Both sides offer examples of the wisdom or folly of spending big on free agents. The Boston Red Sox stormed past the luxury-tax threshold \xe2\x80\x94 which most teams see as a de facto salary cap \xe2\x80\x94 and signed designated hitter J.D. Martinez to a five-year, $110 million contract last February. Martinez hit .330 with 43 homers and led the Red Sox to the World Series title.'
b'But what about the Baltimore Orioles? They signed a free-agent starter, Alex Cobb, to a four-year, $57 million contract last spring, only to watch him go 5-15 with a 4.90 E.R.A. The team set a franchise record for losses, with 115. Cobb was signed as a rotation stabilizer, not a star, but the move signaled the Orioles\xe2\x80\x99 intentions to marginally improve. It turned out to be another cautionary tale.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cTeams used to pay big money for mediocrity, for the average player,\xe2\x80\x9d said Steve Phillips, the former Mets general manager and a host on MLB Network Radio. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd now they\xe2\x80\x99re not willing to do that, because you can find close to average with a young guy who\xe2\x80\x99s not going to cost you money.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Iannetta, in the second season of a two-year, $8.5 million deal, sits squarely in baseball\xe2\x80\x99s middle class. He said the union cares equally about the welfare of all members \xe2\x80\x94 from stars to rookies \xe2\x80\x94 and said players were frustrated by a system that squeezes out qualified veterans while also keeping elite prospects (think Vladimir Guerrero Jr. of the Toronto Blue Jays) in the minors to manipulate their service time and keep their price low.'
b'As it stands, the worst teams get the best draft picks and the most money to spend on amateur talent. The Astros and the Cubs showed that weathering years of struggle can help lead to sustained success \xe2\x80\x94 but, of course, not all rebuilding teams will win big.'
b'In their proposal to the owners, the players have advocated a system that would incentivize winning: lower draft position for perennial losers, better draft position for low-revenue teams that win, and so on.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cOne thing that\xe2\x80\x99s going come up in the next few years is: \xe2\x80\x98Oh, this is all about dollars and cents; this is all about players being greedy and wanting more,\xe2\x80\x99 and that\xe2\x80\x99s not the case,\xe2\x80\x9d Iannetta said. \xe2\x80\x9cWe play a game our whole lives and we work our butts off, and we want to compete against the best \xe2\x80\x94 and when the best isn\xe2\x80\x99t out there on the field, it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t feel right.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cA lot of my colleagues looking for jobs are better than players on rosters right now, and that\xe2\x80\x99s not fair,\xe2\x80\x9d he added. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s not putting the best product on the field. There\xe2\x80\x99s talent right now that\xe2\x80\x99s not being utilized, for whatever reason. And it\xe2\x80\x99s not fair to the fans that are spending their money, either.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Phillips, who was Mets\xe2\x80\x99 general manager from 1997 to 2003, said impulsive owners once pushed salaries higher and higher by basing free-agent decisions largely on emotion. Now, he said, more and more owners defer to general managers who rely increasingly on analytics. Players and their agents, perhaps, must recognize and adapt to this new world.'
b'But something seems off when an industry that boasts record revenues suddenly gets smart, all at the same time. The owners would seem to be far too canny to collude against players as they did in the 1980s. But whether it\xe2\x80\x99s groupthink or a flawed system, something needs to change to revive the sport.'
b'After a troubling regular season and another frigid off-season, the players \xe2\x80\x94 those with jobs, anyway \xe2\x80\x94 will soon return to work. They\xe2\x80\x99ll have a lot on their minds.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a critical point,\xe2\x80\x9d Iannetta said. \xe2\x80\x9cWe need to really be cognizant of putting the right ideas first and the right values first \xe2\x80\x94 and that\xe2\x80\x99s maintaining the competitive nature and the free-market system and going from there.\xe2\x80\x9d'

b'By Richard Goldstein'
b'Even into her 90s she called him Daddy. She loved to tell of the 14-room apartment on Manhattan\xe2\x80\x99s Upper West Side where he delighted in trimming the Christmas tree with icicles and how, when she began dating, he made sure she returned home by midnight.'
b'In her later years, she traveled around the baseball map, most notably to the house he had figuratively built, telling of his life beyond the ballpark.'
b'Julia Ruth Stevens, the adopted daughter of Babe Ruth, died on Saturday in an assisted living facility in Henderson, Nev., her son, Tom, said. A Boston Red Sox fan in her later years but always having a warm spot for the Yankees, she was 102.'
b'She was born in Athens, Ga., on July 7, 1916, to Claire Hodgson, a teenage beauty, and her husband, Frank, who was many years his wife\xe2\x80\x99s senior. They separated when Julia was a baby, and Ms. Hodgson went with her to New York and found work as an illustrator\xe2\x80\x99s model.'
b'Ms. Hodgson met Ruth in 1923, when he had become a prodigious home run hitter, three years after being sold to the Yankees by the Red Sox, for whom he had been a star pitcher.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI have this vague image of this huge man, who was really nice, coming to visit Mother, and he\xe2\x80\x99d pick me up and set me on his lap and ask me little questions about how I was,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Stevens told The Arizona Republic in 2001. \xe2\x80\x9cIf Mother and I went out with him, people just crowded around.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Ruth already had a wife, but they were estranged. He had married Helen Woodford when he was a Red Sox rookie, and they had a daughter, Dorothy. The Ruths separated in 1925, and Helen moved to suburban Boston. She died in a house fire in January 1929 while Dorothy was at boarding school.'
b'Ruth married Claire Hodgson on opening day of the 1929 baseball season. He adopted Julia, and Claire adopted Dorothy in 1930, and they all lived together, with Claire\xe2\x80\x99s extended family, in an apartment on West 88th Street.'
b'Mrs. Stevens remembered her childhood as a happy time. The Babe took her bowling, and as a teenager she would go with her parents to the Vanderbilt Hotel, which had an orchestra and a dance floor. \xe2\x80\x9cHe taught me how to dance, and I would really much prefer dancing with Daddy than with any of my dates,\xe2\x80\x9d she once recalled.'
b'When Ruth visited Japan with a major league all-star team in 1934, Julia and Claire came along. Dorothy stayed home, and years later she maintained that her stepmother had given Julia favored treatment.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI was excess baggage,\xe2\x80\x9d Dorothy Ruth Pirone said in her 1988 memoir, \xe2\x80\x9cMy Dad, the Babe,\xe2\x80\x9d written with Chris Martens. \xe2\x80\x9cRaising me was a burdensome job, like a stack of unexpected paperwork dropped in her lap.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'And she offered a revelation. She said that in 1980, the former Juanita Jennings, a longtime friend of the Ruth family, told her two weeks before her death that she was her biological mother, having had an affair with Ruth, who then persuaded her to give the baby to Helen.'
b'Claire Hodgson Ruth died in 1976 and Mrs. Pirone died in 1989. Mrs. Stevens ultimately became the spokeswoman for the Ruth family.'
b'She was at Yankee Stadium in May 1998 for the unveiling of a postage stamp portraying Ruth admiring one of his home run drives. That August, she threw out the first pitch at a Red Sox game in Fenway Park at ceremonies marking the 50th anniversary of Ruth\xe2\x80\x99s death.'
b'She was at Fenway Park in October 1999 to toss the first pitch before the decisive Game 5 of the American League Championship Series. Having lived for many years in Conway, N.H., she had become a Red Sox fan.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI went to see the Red Sox beat the Yankees tonight,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.'
b'As for the so-called Curse of the Bambino, arising from Ruth\xe2\x80\x99s sale to the Yankees, she said at the time: \xe2\x80\x9cMostly I think it\xe2\x80\x99s a myth. But it is a coincidence definitely that there\xe2\x80\x99s been many, many, many years since the Red Sox managed to win a World Series.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The Red Sox lost to the Yankees in that 1999 postseason, but they ended an 86-year drought in 2004 by beating the Yankees in the American League Championship Series and winning the World Series, defeating the St. Louis Cardinals.'
b'Mrs. Stevens made occasional appearances at major league ballparks until she was 100, although she had become legally blind from macular degeneration.'
b'When the Yankees played their last game at the old Stadium, the House That Ruth Built, in September 2008, she threw out the first pitch. And she threw out the first ball at a Red Sox game at Fenway Park on July 10, 2016, to mark her 100th birthday three days earlier.'
b'In July 2014, the 100th anniversary of Ruth\xe2\x80\x99s major league debut as a pitcher for the Red Sox, Mrs. Stevens told The Los Angeles Times, \xe2\x80\x9cI miss him even to this day.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cBut his spirit seems to hover over the baseball field,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.'
b'Interviewed by Golf.com in March 2015, Mrs. Stevens remembered Ruth\xe2\x80\x99s passion for golf \xe2\x80\x94 how it gave him pleasure, especially in the 1930s, when he had become discouraged by the failure of his quest to become a baseball manager.'
b'She told how he would often awaken her at dawn, cook fried bologna and egg sandwiches for both of them, and then head for a day of golf at St. Albans Country Club in Queens.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cBaseball broke his heart, but golf kept him going,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.'
b'Mrs. Stevens\xe2\x80\x99s first husband, Richard Flanders, who died in 1949, operated a New Hampshire ski lodge. She later operated a general store in Eaton Center, N.H. Her marriage to her second husband, Grant Meloon, ended in divorce. She operated a New Hampshire poultry farm with her third husband, Brent Stevens, to whom she was married for 49 years, and later worked in retail managerial jobs and occasionally received small amounts of money from companies that used Ruth\xe2\x80\x99s image.'
b'But in 1984, the Indianapolis-based CMG Worldwide began representing the Ruth estate as its exclusive marketing agent. Mrs. Stevens told Bloomberg News in 1998 that she was receiving more than $80,000 a year in royalties through deals arranged by CMG.'
b'In addition to her son, from her third marriage, Mrs. Stevens is survived by two grandchildren and four great-grandchildren.'
b'On the 50th anniversary of Ruth\xe2\x80\x99s death, she was asked why her father had endured as an American hero.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI think probably because he was so close to the people, to his fans,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Stevens told NBC. \xe2\x80\x9cThey always, naturally, never called him Mr. Ruth. It was always, \xe2\x80\x98Hi ya, Babe.\xe2\x80\x99 It was just like saying hi to a friend.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'An earlier version of this obituary referred incorrectly to Mrs. Stevens\xe2\x80\x99s son, Tom. He is her son from her third marriage, to Brent Stevens \xe2\x80\x94 not her second, to Grant Meloon. And an earlier version of a picture caption with this obituary misidentified the game and championship series at which Mrs. Stevens was shown throwing out the first pitch at Fenway Park in Boston. As the obituary notes, it was the fifth game of the 1999 American League Championship Series \xe2\x80\x94 not the 1998 American Championship League Series.'

b'By George Vecsey'
b'This happened at Shea Stadium a few decades ago: My son and I were in a half-empty section, not far from two older black men.'
b'One of the Mets made a clodhopper move on the bases, and was tagged out.'
b'One of the two fans then turned to the other and said, \xe2\x80\x9cJackie Robinson wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have done that.\xe2\x80\x9d I chortled, and we struck up a conversation, having the burning image of Jackie Robinson very much in common.'
b'Jackie Robinson hovered over that game, and he has hovered over every game that has been played since April 15, 1947, when he made his major-league debut.'
b'He upgraded the game, and he upgraded my Brooklyn Dodgers, and he upgraded life in America in his 10 years in the major leagues and in his short but active career out in the Real World, pushing for opportunities for black people in all businesses.'
b'He raised the consciousness of Americans, whether they wanted it or not, and that is good reason to honor him on the 100th anniversary of his birth.'
b'[On Jackie Robinson\xe2\x80\x99s 100th Birthday, 100 Photos of an Icon]'
b'[Claire Smith: Jackie Robinson Showed Me How to Fight On, Not Fight Back]'
b'He was a hero in my family. I met him once, under the stands in Ebbets Field, in a hot dog line, during a game in 1954 when he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t playing. We asked him how his injury was, and he answered a couple of teenagers with politeness. (I was shocked to see his gray hair and thickening midsection. He seemed old before his time. The stress seemed to have aged him.)'
b'I also interviewed him once on the phone, around 1966, when I was doing a story on the absence of black coaches and managers in baseball.'
b'When I called for the prearranged interview, he turned the theme around, asking how many black journalists were in the sports department where I worked. Umm, none, I said, thereby touching off a monologue on lack of opportunity everywhere. That\xe2\x80\x99s what I\xe2\x80\x99m talking about, he said. I consider the lecture a highlight of my career.'
b'Decades later, I told his widow, Rachel Robinson, about our testy interview.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThat sounds like Jack,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with her Mona Lisa smile.'
b'The world was not paying attention when Jack Roosevelt Robinson was born on Jan. 31, 1919, in Cairo, Ga. Back east we began learning about him in 1945, when Branch Rickey signed him to a contract with the Brooklyn organization.'
b'His presence in Dodger camp in 1946 was a big deal in my family\xe2\x80\x99s home in Queens County, just east of the mother ship \xe2\x80\x94 that is to say, Brooklyn. We were Dodgers fans and my parents were also 1930s lefties who adored Franklin Delano Roosevelt and who regarded two other African-Americans, Marian Anderson and Paul Robeson, as icons, both for their singing talent and for their dignity.'
b'Not all Dodgers fans came from our sociopolitical point of view, of course, but Jackie Robinson was on all Dodgers fans\xe2\x80\x99 minds as he trained with dubious white teammates in 1946 and then succeeded at the top farm club in Montreal that season.'
b'The next year, the phone rang in our house on April 10. My dad was calling from his newspaper office and he had momentous news to relay: The Dodgers had signed Jackie Robinson to a major league contract and he would make his debut on Opening Day, five days later. I was almost 8; I can remember the exhilaration.'
b'And if working-class white folks like us were enthused, it was even more personal for black folks, when there was finally a ballplayer who looked like them.'
b'Ed Charles, the poet-third baseman of the 1969 Mets, who died a year ago, often talked about being a youth in Florida in the late 1940s, watching the touring Dodgers departing by railroad after an exhibition, watching Jackie Robinson play cards with his white teammates.'
b'The Dodgers won the pennant in 1947, Robinson\xe2\x80\x99s first season, and just as important, they gave Brooklyn fans the lifelong certainty that our team was The Good Guys.'
b'And what began for everyone in 1947 was a decade of Jackie Robinson on the field, running the bases pigeon-toed, a big man, a former tailback with adult weight on him, starting to take out infielders with a hard slide, starting to jaw at umpires, learning four new positions to help the Dodgers, bringing his burning audacity to the majors.'
b'For a while, he wore the shackles of docility, to which he had committed, but on the field he was allowed to compete, fair and square. With bat. With glove. With feet. With head.'
b'Black sports fans now had a new hero, excelling blatantly, in mixed company. That was something to sing about.'
b'Sure enough, in 1949, Buddy Johnson (Woodrow Wilson Johnson, for the record) wrote and recorded a song called \xe2\x80\x9cDid You See Jackie Robinson Hit That Ball?\xe2\x80\x9d It was soon covered by the great band of Count Basie.'
b'Did you see Jackie Robinson hit that ball? Did he hit it? Yeah, and that ain\xe2\x80\x99t all. He stole home. Yes, yes, Jackie\xe2\x80\x99s real gone.'
b'Because of Robinson some Americans were lifted up from our stodginess and were able to appreciate a gifted athlete and a driven human being, excelling where no player of color had been allowed since the 19th Century.'
b'Robinson\xe2\x80\x99s style even spread around baseball as black players proliferated: Larry Doby, Roy Campanella, Don Newcombe, Monte Irvin, and in 1951 a wonder-man named Willie Mays, and then Frank Robinson and Henry Aaron and Ernie Banks and Roberto Clemente, all of whom made the National League the powerhouse for the next generation.'
b'Perhaps, too, Jackie Robinson served as an example for a series of begats. James Baldwin. Nina Simone. Malcolm X. Rosa Parks. Martin Luther King Jr. Muhammad Ali. Chuck Berry. John Lewis. Dick Gregory. Harry Belafonte. Harry Edwards. Jim Brown. Bob Gibson. Reggie Jackson. Bill Russell. Shirley Chisholm. Barack Obama. Michelle Robinson Obama.'
b'In 2008, I asked Rachel Robinson what her husband would have thought of the Obama candidacy.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a political question, George,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd we don\xe2\x80\x99t talk about politics.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Her husband had been overcome by diabetes in retirement, and was clearly failing during the 1972 World Series, when he was introduced to Joe Morgan, the thoughtful young star of the Cincinnati Reds. Morgan was thrilled, but also stunned, to see Robinson, the man who danced off third base, now white-haired and shaky and going blind. Jackie Robinson died a few weeks later, at the age of 53.'
b'Thirty-five years later, Morgan, a Hall of Fame member and broadcaster, was at a celebration of the Jackie Robinson Foundation. (Morgan calls Rachel Robinson \xe2\x80\x9cThe Queen Mother.\xe2\x80\x9d)'
b'I stood with Morgan and Willie Randolph, who grew up in Brooklyn, as we watched old game films of a sleek, fleet tailback for U.C.L.A. in 1939 and 1940, going wide, faking tacklers onto the ground.'
b'Did we see Jackie Robinson carry the ball?'
b'We exchanged high-fives at his audacity.'
