b'The Division and the Pennant'
b'The Mets are in. The Cubs, and Durocher, are done. And, believe it or not, the postseason awaits.'
b'By The New York Times'
b'Following is an excerpt from a Times article on Sept. 25, 1969.'
b'By Leonard Koppett'
b'With a flourish worthy of the occasion, the New York Mets officially won their first championship last night by beating the St. Louis Cardinals, 6-0, in the presence of 54,928 paying spectators fully prepared for the ecstasy of the victory.'
b'By scoring their 96th triumph of the baseball season, the Mets clinched first place in the Eastern Division of the National League, a 94-year-old circuit playing in two six-team divisions for the first time. In their previous seven seasons, the Mets had never finished higher than ninth in a 10-team league.'
b'Five runs in the first inning, three on a home run by Donn Clendenon and two on a home run by Ed Charles, set the victory mood early.'
b'Gary Gentry, a rookie, proceeded to pitch a four-hit shutout and Clendenon added another home run in the fifth. That gave the crowd nearly two hours to work itself up for the celebration that erupted at 9:07 p.m., when Joe Torre bounced into a double play that ended the game.'
b'As the players raced for the clubhouse for their own celebration, thousands of spectators leaped railings and quickly filled the field, roaring and chanting \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re No. 1.\xe2\x80\x9d For the next 20 minutes at least, the cheering and milling continued, until the lights were dimmed and a slow dispersal began.'
b'All concerned were fully and joyfully aware of what the victory meant. The Chicago Cubs, who had led the division and the Mets by 10 games in mid-August, had won their afternoon game, precluding the possibility that the Mets would clinch before taking the field.'
b'But to perform the clincher at home, before the fans who have been such an integral part of the club\xe2\x80\x99s peculiar history, the Mets had to win this game, the last scheduled for Shea Stadium. Three games remain to be played in Philadelphia this weekend and two in Chicago next week.'
b'The style with which the Mets won was not restricted to the sudden, staggering attack with which they greeted Steve Carlton, the Cardinal left-hander who had set a major league record by striking out 19 Mets only 10 days ago in St. Louis.'
b'The Mets had won that game, despite the strikeouts. But last Friday they lost a doubleheader to Pittsburgh and on Saturday were held hitless by Bob Moose of the Pirates. If the Cubs, trailing by four games at that point, had a chance for a final counterattack, that was it.'
b'Instead, the Mets beat Pittsburgh twice on Sunday and last night completed a three-game sweep of the Cardinals, the National League champions for the last two years.'
b'This five-game winning streak gave them a record of 34 victories in 44 games since Aug. 16, and 18 in their last 22 games. This constituted a stretch drive equal in quality to any produced by baseball\xe2\x80\x99s most famous champions of the past.'
b'This time, Carlton struck out only one man, Cleon Jones \xe2\x80\x94 and it was the only man he retired. Gentry, who had also started against Carlton in the game in St. Louis, quickly retired the Cardinals in order in the first, and the crowd started chanting and clapping as the first Met, Bud Harrelson, went to bat.'
b'Harrelson looped a single to right field and the cheers increased. Tommie Agee walked and the noise increased again. It merged into a standing ovation for Jones, who was starting a game for the first time since Sept. 11, after sitting out most of the month because of hand and rib injuries. Jones, who still leads the league in hitting, was determined to be part of this occasion and the fans recognized his desire and his season-long contribution.'
b'But Jones struck out, delaying poetic justice only momentarily. The very next pitch was drilled by Clendenon over the center-field wall 410 feet from home plate.'
b'This home run was Clendenon\xe2\x80\x99s 14th of the season, his 10th as a Met, and it put Gentry in control of the game.'
b'The Mets weren\xe2\x80\x99t through, however. Ron Swoboda, whose two two-run homers had beaten Carlton in the game in St. Louis, walked. And Charles, who is 36 and near the end of his career, drove his third home run of the year over the 396-foot sign just to the right of dead center \xe2\x80\x94 a blow, he said later, he would always remember.'
b'Dave Giusti replaced Carlton at that point, and although Jerry Grote greeted him with a double, there was little offensive activity the rest of this brisk game.'
b'Gentry, after three perfect innings, yielded a single to Lou Brock in the fourth, and one to Mike Shannon in the fifth. Meanwhile, the Mets started making sensational fielding plays \xe2\x80\x94 by Harrelson at short, by Al Weis at second, by Agee near the fence in left-center.'
b'Eventually, everyone was just waiting for the formal ending.'
b'When the ninth inning began, the crowd began cheering every pitch. But Brock beat out a single after Harrelson made a fine stop behind second, and Vic Davalillo bounced a single out of Harrelson\xe2\x80\x99s reach to center. Still, the crowd roared encouragement to Gentry.'
b'Vada Pinson struck out, bringing up Joe Torre \xe2\x80\x94 the Brooklynite who established his slugging reputation with the Braves, and who \xe2\x80\x9cmight have brought the Mets the pennant\xe2\x80\x9d if they had been willing to trade for him last winter. Instead, he was traded to the Cardinals.'
b'At any rate, with Torre at bat, the crowd was chanting for a double play. And that\xe2\x80\x99s just what it got. Torre bounced to Harrelson, near second, who threw to Weis, who threw to Clendenon, and pandemonium was officially in session.'
b'With their bats, and a broom, the Mets capture the pennant.'
b'Following is an excerpt from a article on Oct. 17, 1969.'
b'By George Vecsey'
b'Rico Carty hit a long foul ball to left field, then he heard a noise. The manager of the Mets was coming out to remove the starting pitcher. The new pitcher was named \xe2\x80\xa6.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t even know his name,\xe2\x80\x9d the cleanup hitter of the Atlanta Braves said later. \xe2\x80\x9cI never saw him before. It didn\xe2\x80\x99t make too much of a difference to me. A lot of pitchers I don\xe2\x80\x99t know.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The new pitcher \xe2\x80\x94 Nolan Ryan \xe2\x80\x94 went into the game in the third inning with two men on. And while Ryan was warming up, Carty visited the dugout. \xe2\x80\x9cI asked the guys what he threw,\xe2\x80\x9d Carty said later. \xe2\x80\x9cSome said fastball, some said slider.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'There was a reason Carty had never seen the young right-hander with No. 30 on his back. Ryan never pitched against the Braves this season because of military duties and injuries. And last year Carty was in a hospital recovering from tuberculosis. So he stepped up to home plate for his first look at Ryan.'
b'The next thing Carty knew, Ryan had busted a fastball past him. The talk about Ryan\xe2\x80\x99s slider was merely a rumor.'
b'Ryan finished the game and won the pennant for the Mets. Afterward the Braves sat in the clubhouse and talked about fastballs, not sliders.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cHeck, we knew what he threw,\xe2\x80\x9d said Henry Aaron, who had a homer and double off Gentry but two pop-ups off Ryan. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve seen him in the past where he was erratic. He\xe2\x80\x99s nothing but a kid but he did a helluva job out there.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s as fast as anybody in the majors,\xe2\x80\x9d said Manager Luman Harris. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the first time we\xe2\x80\x99ve seen him all year. I wish we hadn\xe2\x80\x99t seen him today.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The Braves seemed calm \xe2\x80\x94 or stunned \xe2\x80\x94 after their third straight loss. '
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re destiny,\xe2\x80\x9d said Pat Jarvis, the losing pitcher.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThey won the pennant,\xe2\x80\x9d Harris said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I just don\xe2\x80\x99t believe they can hit our pitching like they did. I admire Gil Hodges. To me, there\xe2\x80\x99s no question he\xe2\x80\x99s the manager of the year. But I still believe if we played \xe2\x80\x99em three more games, we\xe2\x80\x99d beat \xe2\x80\x99em three straight.\xe2\x80\x9d'

b'Royals 6, Yankees 1'
b'By The Associated Press'
b'Homer Bailey has something to smile about if he goes hunting with Cal Eldred during the off-season. Bailey, whose career had come apart over the last few years, on Thursday won a second consecutive start for the first time since July 2017.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI can be the first person to say wins are precious. Sometimes they can be few and far between, so you enjoy them when they come,\xe2\x80\x9d Bailey said after holding the Yankees to three hits over six innings in the Kansas City Royals\xe2\x80\x99 6-1 victory at Yankee Stadium.'
b'Eldred, the Royals\xe2\x80\x99 pitching coach, had called Bailey in January, after the right-hander was released by the Los Angeles Dodgers. Eldred did some research, knowing they had a common interest.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWe both like to deer hunt,\xe2\x80\x9d Eldred said. \xe2\x80\x9cMy recruiting pitch was, \xe2\x80\x98You know what, Homer, you\xe2\x80\x99ve got good stuff, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen you for a long time, and I think that we can work together.\xe2\x80\x99 We\xe2\x80\x99ve got good people over here. We\xe2\x80\x99ve got a young team that he could be a good example to.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Jorge Soler and Ryan O\xe2\x80\x99Hearn backed Bailey (2-1) with solo home runs off Domingo German (3-1).'
b'Bailey, who turns 33 on May 3, has been among baseball\xe2\x80\x99s biggest busts since signing a $105 million, six-year contract with the Cincinnati Reds before the 2014 season'
b'Unable to overcome injuries that have prevented him from pitching a full season since 2013, he went 18-32 for the Reds after the big deal. Bailey missed more than 14 months after Tommy John surgery in May 2015 and then needed another operation in February 2017 to remove bone spurs from his right elbow.'
b'He was 1-14 last season, and then was traded to the Dodgers in December and released with $28 million still due. Bailey signed a minor league contract with the Royals on Feb. 9, made the team and after opening with a no-decision against Minnesota and a loss to Seattle, allowed two hits over seven scoreless innings to beat Cleveland last Saturday for his first win since May 12.'
b'He gave up a tying run to the Yankees on Gleyber Torres\xe2\x80\x99s first-inning sacrifice fly after singles by Aaron Judge and Luke Voit, and then gave up just one more hit. Bailey struck out six and walked one.'
b'Coming off a two-game sweep against the Boston Red Sox, the Yankees got only four hits altogether on Thursday, two of them from Clint Frazier, who is hitting .347.'
b'Bailey has a 4.30 E.R.A., a figure inflated when gave up seven runs to the Mariners.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cHis split was a problem for us,\xe2\x80\x9d Yankees Manager Aaron Boone said. \xe2\x80\x9cWe just were having a hard time laying off that pitch.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'German gave up three runs and six hits in six innings with nine strikeouts and no walks. He gave up consecutive doubles in the first to Adalberto Mondesi \xe2\x80\x94 who had been 0 for 20 on the road this year \xe2\x80\x94 and Alex Gordon, then fell behind 3-1 on leadoff home runs by Soler in the second and O\xe2\x80\x99Hearn in the fourth.'
b'The Yankees bullpen got five more strikeouts for a total of 14, but Whit Merrifield hit an R.B.I. double in the seventh off Jonathan Holder, and Mondesi added sacrifice flies against Zack Britton and Joseph Harvey.'
b'The Yankees lost for the sixth time in nine games, and they have not been over .500 since a 15-3 romp in Baltimore on April 7 gave them a 5-4 record.'
b'Mike Ford made his major league debut for the Yankees after 561 games over seven minor league seasons and went 0 for 3 with a walk and two strikeouts as his parents watched from the stands. Ford saw 24 pitches and took a called third strike that ended the game. He flew out in his first at-bat.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt was just awesome,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI actually wasn\xe2\x80\x99t very nervous, just kind of ready for the moment and just tried to put a good swing on the ball.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'A TRADITION HALTED The Yankees have suspended the use of Kate Smith\xe2\x80\x99s recording of \xe2\x80\x9cGod Bless America\xe2\x80\x9d during the seventh-inning stretch while they investigate an allegation of racism against the singer.'
b'The Daily News reported that there were conflicting claims about Smith\xe2\x80\x99s 1939 song \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s Why Darkies Were Born.\xe2\x80\x9d The song originated in the 1931 Broadway revue \xe2\x80\x9cGeorge White\xe2\x80\x99s Scandals,\xe2\x80\x9d and was considered satire. It was recorded by Smith and also by Paul Robeson, who was black and a prominent civil rights activist.'
b'The team said in a statement: \xe2\x80\x9cThe Yankees have been made aware of a recording that had been previously unknown to us and decided to immediately and carefully review this new information. The Yankees take social, racial and cultural insensitivities very seriously. And while no final conclusions have been made, we are erring on the side of sensitivity.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Smith died in 1986. The Yankees have used her recording of \xe2\x80\x9cGod Bless America\xe2\x80\x9d and sometimes live singers during the seventh inning since the Sept. 11, 2001, terrorist attacks. They used an organ version on Wednesday and Thursday.'

b'The regular season '
b'The Mets overcame a slow start and a stagnant summer to run away with the division.'
b'The scoreboard at Shea Stadium showed no hits for Chicago as Tom Seaver pitched to Ernie Banks of the Cubs on July 9. Seaver came within two outs of a perfect game.CreditCreditAssociated Press'
b'Supported by'
b'By The New York Times'
b'The season began with a loss to the brand-new Montreal Expos, the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 eighth straight opening-day defeat. And the situation grew only worse. At the end of April, the Mets were 9-11. On May 27, they were 18-23, in fourth place in the National League East. Off in the distance were the first-place Chicago Cubs.'
b'But the next day, the Mets beat San Diego, 1-0, in a game in which Jerry Koosman struck out 15 batters. That began an 11-game winning streak that left the Mets \xe2\x80\x94 yeah, the Mets \xe2\x80\x94 with a record of 29-23.'
b'The revolution was on. In early July, the Mets had their first showdown with the Cubs, and Tom Seaver came within two outs of a perfect game. A week later, the Mets took two out of three at Wrigley Field. The Mets were now in second place, just four games out of first.'
b'At which point, the Mets flattened out. On July 30, Manager Gil Hodges even went out to left field to pull Cleon Jones from the game. Jones, he felt, was not hustling.'
b'Hodges was sending a message, but the Mets kept struggling. By mid-August, they were 10 games out.'
b'And then, with Woodstock being staged in the background, the Mets took off. They swept the Padres at Shea in two weekend doubleheaders and began a run in which they would win 38 of their last 49 games of the season, sometimes in stupefying fashion.'
b'There was, for instance, the delirious Aug. 30 game in San Francisco in which the Mets threw out runners at home in the eighth and ninth innings along with another runner at third and then won in the 10th on a home run.'
b'A week later, Manager Leo Durocher and his swooning Cubs arrived at Shea, and the Mets beat them twice, with fans sarcastically serenading Durocher and with a black cat running in front of the Chicago dugout. The next day, the Mets moved into first. Two days later, they won a 1-0, 1-0 doubleheader in Pittsburgh. The starting pitchers drove in both runs.'
b'Three days later, the Mets struck out a record 19 times against Steve Carlton in St. Louis. But so what? They won, anyway, with Ron Swoboda hitting two two-run homers.'
b'The Mets were now pulling away with 15 games to go. The impossible had become the inevitable.'
b'Following are excerpts from New York Times articles in 1969.'
b'SHEA STADIUM, JUNE 3'
b'Scaling new heights again, the New York Mets climbed past the break-even point and into second place in the National League East by beating the Los Angeles Dodgers, 5-2, at Shea Stadium tonight.'
b'Two home runs by Ed Kranepool, a well-placed misplay by the Dodgers, Tom Seaver\xe2\x80\x99s eighth pitching victory and Tug McGraw\xe2\x80\x99s second save marked the historic occasion, which also stretched the current winning streak to six games.'
b'No Met team had ever been above .500 beyond the fourth game of a season. In 1966, and again this year, the Mets won two of their first three games; but in 1966, they lost the next five and this year they lost the next four. Never again did they have more victories than defeats until now, when their record stands at 24-23. LEONARD KOPPETT'
b'JULY 8'
b'It has taken them 7\xc2\xbd years, 439 victories and 771 defeats, but today the Mets finally begin an important series.'
b'At 2:05 p.m. at Shea Stadium, they start a three-game series with the Chicago Cubs, the only team ahead of them in the National League\xe2\x80\x99s Eastern Division.'
b'The bumbling players who opened at the Polo Grounds in 1962 and their masochistic fans would be dizzied by the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 five-game proximity to first place. And the New Yorkers have lost only three more games than the Cubs, the most important measurement between two winning clubs.'
b'Whether sold out or close to it, Shea should be rocking every minute of the series. The Cubs have lost three straight, the Mets have won five straight and Gil Hodges of the Mets and Leo Durocher of the Cubs have set up their best pitchers for the series. GEORGE VECSEY'
b'SHEA STADIUM, July 9'
b'Tom Seaver of the Mets thrilled the biggest baseball crowd in Shea Stadium history tonight by coming within two outs of pitching a perfect game.'
b'Jim Qualls, a rookie outfielder of the Chicago Cubs, drilled a solid single in the ninth that disappointed Seaver and the 59,083 fans who were quivering with every pitch.'
b'Seaver finished with a one-hitter and a 4-0 victory that narrowed the Cubs\xe2\x80\x99 division lead over the Mets to three games.'
b'The Mets have won seven straight and the Cubs have lost five straight. And after the near-perfect game, who knows where these opposite kinds of momentum might lead?'
b'The Mets were loose and confident going into the game because their best pitcher was going to work. The 24-year-old, right-handed Seaver, a fine athlete, had won 13 games and lost 3.'
b'But two hours later, the Mets and their fans wanted much more than victory No. 14. They wanted perfection and the first no-hitter by a Met, and they cheered madly for every Cub out.'
b'The first batter in the ninth was Randy Hundley, the experienced catcher. He tried to bunt a high pitch, but the ball rolled to Seaver\xe2\x80\x99s right and Seaver threw Hundley out at first. The fans, ever sensitive, booed Hundley for having tried to bunt Seaver out of a perfect game.'
b'The 22-year-old Qualls, a switch-hitter batting left-handed in his 18th major league game, was the next batter. Seaver wanted to keep the ball outside to him, but the pitch strayed a little too close to the plate and Qualls stroked it solidly to left-center, nowhere near Cleon Jones or Tommie Agee. GEORGE VECSEY'
b'July 16'
b'The moon didn\xe2\x80\x99t figure in the conversation \xe2\x80\x94 mostly in Spanish \xe2\x80\x94 at the Full Moon Bar and Grill at 161st and Broadway. The bar was recently renamed La Luna Llena.'
b'It\xe2\x80\x99s owner, Richard Hauseman, was asked why the customers weren\xe2\x80\x99t following the moon shot on television. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s there?\xe2\x80\x9d he retorted. \xe2\x80\x9cWalter Cronkite talking \xe2\x80\x94 that\xe2\x80\x99s all.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The television sets were on at most other bars, but not for Walter Cronkite or the astronauts. In Chicago, the Mets were ahead of the Cubs, suggesting a miracle that even Jules Verne did not foresee. A bartender was asked whether his customers were more interested in the Mets or the astronauts.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe Mets, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAren\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d JOSEPH LELYVELD'
b'Aug. 4'
b'The Mets have seen the moment of their greatness flicker, pressing Chicago for the Eastern Division lead before falling back. The fans overreacted, talking World Series, and the national press and broadcasting, who used to come to Shea Stadium to interview the other teams of the National League, trumpeted the emergence of a great new power. Then the Mets lost seven of 11, their first bona fide slump. They also suffered some of the emotional problems of sudden glory.'
b'Last Wednesday afternoon, Hodges pointedly removed Cleon Jones, his best player, from the outfield in the midst of a game, apparently for playing a ball too casually. And then Ron Swoboda, the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 best off-season banquet player, publicly chided the tra-la-la attitude of the club, the lack of intensity. ROBERT LIPSYTE'
b'SAN DIEGO, Aug. 27'
b'Jerry Koosman pitched the New York Mets to within two games of first place tonight as he overpowered the San Diego Padres, 4-1, with a two-hitter for the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 sixth straight victory.'
b'He allowed the two hits in the first inning but no more the rest of the game and he faced only 31 batters \xe2\x80\x94 four more than the minimum.'
b'The Mets continued past another milestone on their remarkable road several hours after the first-place Chicago Cubs had lost again to the Cincinnati Reds. And by the time the Mets had finished their work tonight, they were all even with Chicago in games lost, and the Cubs\xe2\x80\x99 lead in the Eastern Division rested solely on the fact that they had played \xe2\x80\x94 and won \xe2\x80\x94 four more times.'
b'The victory was Koosman\xe2\x80\x99s third in a row, and the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 12th in their last 13 decisions, while the Cubs were dropping eight of 10. It also was No. 74 for the Mets this season, one more than the record total they achieved all of last season, and it was their 11th in a row over San Diego.'
b'The situation at the top of the Eastern Division was growing so sticky that Ernie Banks even telephoned long-distance to San Diego tonight. JOSEPH DURSO'
b'SAN FRANCISCO, Aug. 30'
b'The New York Mets survived three hours of thrills \xe2\x80\x94 and sometimes terror \xe2\x80\x94 and defeated the San Francisco Giants, 3-2, on a 10th-inning home run by Donn Clendenon.'
b'The victory ended the Giants\xe2\x80\x99 winning streak at nine games and kept the Mets 3 1/2 games behind the first-place Chicago Cubs, who beat the Atlanta Braves, 5-4.'
b'Clendenon put the finishing touches to the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 afternoon by hitting a 2-1 pitch from Gaylord Perry over the right-field fence with two outs. But he did it only after the Mets lived through two wild innings in the eighth and ninth, during which the Giants put five runners on base \xe2\x80\x94 and the Mets threw two of them out at home plate and one at third base. JOSEPH DURSO'
b'SHEA STADIUM, Sept. 9'
b'The New York Mets \xe2\x80\x94 the urchins of baseball for the last seven years \xe2\x80\x94 shook down the thunder from the sky tonight when they overpowered the Chicago Cubs, 7-1, before 58,436 persons in Shea Stadium.'
b'It was the fourth straight victory for the Mets and the sixth straight defeat for the Cubs, and the urchins moved to within a half game of first place in the National League East with three weeks to go.'
b'The Mets, who have never finished higher than ninth, made the tumultuous scene last night on the five-hit pitching of their prodigy, Tom Seaver. The 25-year-old Californian allowed four singles, one double and one walk, and achieved his 21st victory in the gaudiest season of pitching in Mets history.'
b'He was never behind and rarely was in trouble. And the crowd \xe2\x80\x94 which included 51,448 paying customers \xe2\x80\x94 spent much of the game on its feet, waving handkerchiefs and serenading Manager Leo Durocher of Chicago with stanzas of \xe2\x80\x9cGoodbye, eo.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Leo might have felt like going, too. JOSEPH DURSO'
b'SHEA STADIUM, Sept. 10'
b'Stop the season!'
b'The New York Mets reached and held first place tonight by sweeping a doubleheader from the Montreal Expos, 3-2 in 12 innings, and 7-1, and it is rank disregard for poetic justice to make them risk this precious attainment through the 21 games that still remain on the schedule.'
b'Nevertheless, the ultimate height has been scaled, after seven humiliating years only partially soothed by laughter, and never again can it be said that the Mets have never been on top. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re No. 1, we\xe2\x80\x99re No. 1\xe2\x80\x9d chanted the 23,512 in Shea Stadium tonight, and that moment can never be taken away from them or from Met supporters everywhere.'
b'The moment, actually, was 10:13 p.m., Eastern Daylight Time.'
b'This eminence, not even dreamt of for this year when the season began, has been achieved by a four-week hot streak, coinciding with Chicago\xe2\x80\x99s first slump. JOSEPH DURSO'
b'PITTSBURGH, Sept. 12'
b'The galloping New York Mets continued their assault on the establishment of baseball tonight by defeating the Pittsburgh Pirates twice by identical scores of 1-0 and running their winning streak to nine games.'
b'They also added half a game to their lead over the Chicago Cubs in the National League\xe2\x80\x99s Eastern Division. The Cubs ended an eight-game losing streak tonight when they beat the St. Louis Cardinals, and they now trail the Mets by 2\xc2\xbd games with less than three weeks to go.'
b'Three pitchers limited the Pirates to eight hits and no runs in the doubleheader. In the opener, Jerry Koosman pitched a three-hitter and, in the second game, Don Cardwell allowed four hits for eight innings and Tug McGraw gave up one in the ninth.'
b'In both games, the starting pitchers also batted themselves to victory \xe2\x80\x94 Koosman with a single off Bob Moose in the fifth inning, and Cardwell with a single off Dock Ellis in the second inning.'
b'As a result, the Mets added these features to their high-flying caps with only 18 games left: They won their 24th and 25th games of 31 played since Aug. 13, when they trailed the Chicago Cubs by 10 games; they won their 21st and 22nd shutouts of the season, and their third in a row, and they extended their recent string of shutout innings to 34. JOSEPH DURSO'
b'ST. LOUIS, Sept. 15'
b'Steve Carlton of the St. Louis Cardinals set a major league record tonight by striking out 19 New York Mets. But the Mets still won the game, 4-3, on a pair of two-run home runs by Ron Swoboda and extended their lead to 4\xc2\xbd games with 15 to play.'
b'Carlton, a 24-year-old left-hander, struck out the side in four of the nine innings as he surpassed the record of 18 strikeouts set by Sandy Koufax, Bob Feller and Don Wilson. He even fanned Swoboda twice, on his first and third times at bat.'
b'But on his second and fourth trips to the plate, the Maryland muscleman drove home runs into the left-field seats, both times with a man on base, both times with the Mets trailing by one run.'
b'As a result, the Mets swung even higher on their high-flying trapeze with two and a half weeks to play. JOSEPH DURSO'

b'By Tyler Kepner and Kevin Draper'
b'The best player in baseball is closing in on the richest contract in the history of North American sports. Mike Trout, the center fielder for the Los Angeles Angels, is finalizing a contract extension that will be worth about $430 million and keep him with the team through 2030, according to two people who were briefed on the deal but were not authorized to comment publicly.'
b'However the deal is calculated \xe2\x80\x94 either an additional $360 million over 10 years or the Angels\xe2\x80\x99 total commitment of about $430 million for the next 12 years \xe2\x80\x94 Trout has eclipsed the previous record for guaranteed money: the 13-year, $330 million deal Bryce Harper signed this month with the Philadelphia Phillies.'
b'The extension, which was first reported by ESPN, would establish Trout as the highest-paid baseball player on an annual basis, topping the $34.4 million Zack Greinke is earning from the Arizona Diamondbacks on a six-year, $206.5 million contract signed in December 2015.'
b'[From 2017: Mike Trout, Baseball\xe2\x80\x99s Best Without the Brand]'
b'While it is an enormous amount of money, even this record-breaking contract doesn\xe2\x80\x99t necessarily bring Trout into the highest tier of overall income for professional athletes.'
b'While comparing contracts across sports and continents is an inherently difficult undertaking because of varied contractual structures, tax burdens, bonuses and ancillary benefits, it is clear that even with the monster deal Trout would fall well short of becoming the world\xe2\x80\x99s highest paid athlete annually.'
b'The soccer superstar Lionel Messi, for example, is expected to earn more than 106 million euros (about $120 million) per year from Barcelona through 2021, an amount that could rise to 120 million euros if certain incentives are met. The boxer Canelo \xc3\x81lvarez will earn at least $73 million annually from a five-year deal with the streaming service DAZN.'
b'Trout, 27, also won\xe2\x80\x99t have the highest annual salary in North American team sports. In the N.B.A., Stephen Curry of the Golden State Warriors is making $37.5 million this season, and the Houston Rockets\xe2\x80\x99 James Harden will earn $42.3 million annually once his four-year, $169.3 million contract extension kicks in.'
b'The highest-paid N.F.L. player, Green Bay Packers quarterback Aaron Rodgers, earns a bit less than Trout is expected to: an average of $33.5 million from a four-year, $134 million contract (though only $98.7 million of that is guaranteed). Unlike Major League Baseball, the N.F.L. and N.B.A. have salary caps.'
b'Compared with superstars in other sports, Trout has a limited ability to earn money outside the game. Baseball players typically aren\xe2\x80\x99t as well-known nationally or internationally as their counterparts in other major sports, and Trout\xe2\x80\x99s lack of marketability has been lamented by M.L.B. Commissioner Rob Manfred.'
b'Trout does have a number of endorsement deals with companies including Rawlings and Nike, and in 2014 he became of the first baseball player since Ken Griffey Jr. to get a Nike signature cleat. But baseball players\xe2\x80\x99 ability to move merchandise pales in comparison to that of a basketball player or a soccer player, and endorsement contracts reflect that.'
b'Trout earns an estimated $2.5 million from endorsements annually, according to Forbes. Kevin Durant\xe2\x80\x99s endorsement deal with Nike alone is worth up to $300 million over 10 years, while Harden will earn $200 million over 13 years from Adidas. LeBron James of the N.B.A. and Cristiano Ronaldo, the Portuguese soccer star for Juventus, have lifetime deals with Nike that are rumored to be worth $1 billion each.'
b'While a handful of athletes earn more from their contracts annually than Trout, those individuals almost assuredly will not be doing so in 2030, the final year of Trout\xe2\x80\x99s deal. N.B.A. contracts last a maximum of five years, and with a few exceptions, soccer contracts are rarely longer than that, either. Boxers typically earn their money fight to fight, while golf and tennis players simply claim a share of tournament prize money.'
b'But in 2030, no matter how Trout\xe2\x80\x99s baseball career progresses, he will earn at least $36 million \xe2\x80\x94 a priceless long-term assurance.'
b'He has earned the payout by putting together seven astounding seasons to begin his career; the most similar players, production-wise, at his age, according to Baseball Reference, have been the Hall of Famers Frank Robinson, Ken Griffey Jr. and Mickey Mantle. Trout has won two American League Most Valuable Player Awards and been the runner-up four times. His career batting average is .307 and he is the majors\xe2\x80\x99 active leader in on-base plus slugging percentage, at .990.'
b'But, adding to Trout\xe2\x80\x99s lack of recognition and earning power, postseason success has eluded him. He has reached the playoffs just once, in 2014, when the Angels were swept by the Kansas City Royals in a division series. The possibility of Trout departing for a new team loomed after the 2020 season, when his six-year, $144.5 million contract was set to expire; Harper, for one, had said he would love for Trout to join him in Philadelphia.'
b'Trout still lives in his hometown, Millville, N.J., in the off-season, and is a die-hard Philadelphia Eagles fan. But the Angels had tried to impress upon Trout the benefits of keeping his work and home lives separate, and to sell him on the idea of being a one-team icon with a franchise with which he has thrived. And with a promise of an enormous paycheck \xe2\x80\x94 at least for on-field performance \xe2\x80\x94 the Angels put together a winning pitch.'

b'Supported by'
b'By Tyler Kepner'
b'Mariano Rivera, the career saves leader whose elegant efficiency helped the Yankees win five World Series, on Tuesday became the first player ever elected unanimously to the Baseball Hall of Fame.'
b'Two other right-handed pitchers, Roy Halladay and Mike Mussina, also were elected, Halladay on his first try and Mussina on his sixth. Edgar Martinez, the longtime Seattle Mariners designated hitter, gained entry in his 10th and final year on the ballot. Halladay, a former ace of the Toronto Blue Jays and the Philadelphia Phillies, died in a plane crash in 2017.'
b'Rivera was named on all 425 of the ballots cast by members of the Baseball Writers\xe2\x80\x99 Association of America, eclipsing the previous record percentage, 99.3, by Ken Griffey Jr. in 2016. Halladay and Martinez both received 85.4 percent of the votes, and Mussina \xe2\x80\x94 a stalwart for the Baltimore Orioles and the Yankees \xe2\x80\x94 76.7 percent. Candidates need 75 percent for election.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cAfter my career, I was thinking that I had a shot to be a Hall of Famer,\xe2\x80\x9d Rivera said on a conference call with reporters. \xe2\x80\x9cBut this was just beyond my imagination. I was amazed the way all this has been, through my whole career \xe2\x80\x94 and this being the pinnacle of every player that plays the game of baseball, to be unanimous.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The players will be inducted to the Hall of Fame on July 21 in Cooperstown, N.Y., along with designated hitter Harold Baines and reliever Lee Smith, who were passed over by the writers but were elected by a smaller committee last month.'
b'Barry Bonds and Roger Clemens, whose dominant careers stretched from the mid-1980s through 2007, were again denied entry in what was the seventh appearance on the ballot for both players. Both have strong ties to performance-enhancing drugs, which baseball did not test for until 2003. Bonds got 59.1 percent of the vote, and Clemens 59.5 percent \xe2\x80\x94 their highest totals yet, but still shy of 75 percent.'
b'Rivera, 49, signed with the Yankees from Panama in 1990 for a $3,500 bonus. He reached the majors five years later and started 10 games; the last batter he faced as a starter was Martinez, who singled home a run in the fifth inning against him on Sept. 5, 1995, knocking Rivera from the game and sending him to the bullpen forever.'
b'It was a perfect fit. Rivera thrived as a setup man in the Yankees\xe2\x80\x99 1996 championship run and took over as the team\xe2\x80\x99s closer in 1997, the year he discovered his devastating cut fastball, which broke hundreds of bats with its hard, late movement into the hands of left-handers. Rivera remained the closer through his retirement in 2013, compiling 652 saves with a 2.21 earned run average, the lowest in baseball history for anyone born after 1889 (and with a minimum of 1,000 major-league innings).'
b'Rivera was even better across his record 96 postseason games, with a 0.70 E.R.A. and 42 saves \xe2\x80\x94 matching his uniform number. He was the last player to regularly wear No. 42, which is retired across Major League Baseball for Jackie Robinson.'
b'Rivera was so prolific that M.L.B. named an award after him to recognize the American League reliever of the year. Likewise, Martinez is the namesake for the award recognizing the best designated hitter. Martinez, 56, made only 560 career starts in the field (mostly at third base) but nearly 1,400 as the Mariners\xe2\x80\x99 D.H.'
b'Martinez won A.L. batting titles in 1992 and 1995, when he helped lead the Mariners to their first playoff berth, securing the team\xe2\x80\x99s future in Seattle. For his career, he hit .312 with a .418 on-base percentage and a .515 slugging percentage; only two other right-handed hitters in the game\xe2\x80\x99s history \xe2\x80\x94 Jimmie Foxx and Rogers Hornsby \xe2\x80\x94 were better in all three categories with at least 5,000 plate appearances.'
b'Even so, it took a while for Martinez\xe2\x80\x99s case to build momentum. The writers had never elected a player with as many games as Martinez as a D.H., a position created in 1973 to boost offense in the American League. In 2014, only 25.2 percent of the writers voted for Martinez.'
b'But that support has grown steadily, and Martinez became the fifth player elected in his final year of eligibility by the writers, joining Red Ruffing (1967), Ralph Kiner (1975), Jim Rice (2009) and Tim Raines (2017). Martinez made it just in time \xe2\x80\x94 but, he said, the time was right.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe fact that I\xe2\x80\x99m going in with Mariano, that means a lot, especially because not only was he the top reliever in the game, but also he\xe2\x80\x99s a great human being, a great person,\xe2\x80\x9d said Martinez, who went 11 for 19 in his career against Rivera. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s also the fact that he got 100 percent of the votes \xe2\x80\x94 that makes it also extra special.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Mussina, 50, earned 270 victories and finished with a flourish, securing his first 20-win season on the final day of his career in 2008. His strikeout-to-walk rate \xe2\x80\x94 3.58-to-1 \xe2\x80\x94 is the best in history among pitchers with at least 500 starts, and he did it all in the A.L. East, taming its brawny hitters in small ballparks with a wide array of pitches and a knack for improvisation.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI just kept trying to make adjustments,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mussina, whose curveball was perhaps the best of his generation. \xe2\x80\x9cYou keep trying to do things as you get older and certain skills start going away. You have to find other ways to do your job and be good at it. That\xe2\x80\x99s really all I was ever trying to do \xe2\x80\x94 to be one of the five starters every year.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Halladay reached the majors in 1998 but tumbled to Class A in 2001 to rebuild his mechanics and confidence. He re-emerged as baseball\xe2\x80\x99s pre-eminent starter from 2002 through 2011, averaging 17 victories a season and winning the Cy Young Award in both leagues.'
b'He led his league in complete games nine times and finished with 67, a figure unlikely to be matched anytime soon. No active pitcher has more than 38 complete games, and no staff last season collected more than five. Halladay, who threw a perfect game and a playoff no-hitter for the Phillies in 2010, now seems like a relic in a craft that relies more and more on relief pitchers.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s funny, nobody\xe2\x80\x99s getting to 200 innings anymore, and to me 200 was like the minimum,\xe2\x80\x9d Halladay said in March 2017. \xe2\x80\x9cYou watch the minor leagues and young players now, there\xe2\x80\x99s such an emphasis on swing-and-miss and strikeouts, so there\xe2\x80\x99s more pitches, there\xe2\x80\x99s deeper counts. I felt like my best games were when I threw under 100 pitches and threw a complete game. To me, that was a better game.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cSometimes I\xe2\x80\x99d go out and throw seven or eight innings and be at 120 pitches, but if I was efficient I wanted to throw nine in under 100 pitches. But that was: \xe2\x80\x98challenge \xe2\x80\x99em, pitch to contact,\xe2\x80\x99 where now there\xe2\x80\x99s so much put on, \xe2\x80\x98with two strikes, you can\xe2\x80\x99t let him hit it.\xe2\x80\x99 For me, I felt like with two strikes \xe2\x80\x94 0-2, 1-2 \xe2\x80\x94 if they didn\xe2\x80\x99t swing at it, it was going to be strike three.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Halladay, the son of a pilot, took up aviation after back and shoulder problems forced his retirement in 2013. He was only 40 when he crashed his small plane into shallow water near Holiday, Fla., on Nov. 7, 2017. He is the first player elected posthumously by the writers since Roberto Clemente, the Pittsburgh Pirates outfielder who died in a plane crash in 1972.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIf only Roy were here to personally express his gratitude for this honor,\xe2\x80\x9d said Brandy Halladay, his widow, in a statement, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat an even more amazing day this would be.\xe2\x80\x9d'

b'By James Wagner'
b'Aroldis Chapman was nicknamed the Cuban Missile for a reason. The Yankees\xe2\x80\x99 lanky left-handed closer from the Caribbean island has long been the most prominent face of the velocity explosion in modern baseball. Ever since he broke into the major leagues in 2010, few pitchers could match his 100-miles-per-hour fastball.'
b'Until last season. A month on the injured list with left knee tendinitis sapped some power, and his average fastball speed dipped slightly from the previous season, to 98.9 m.p.h. He was still among the hardest throwers in baseball, but for the first time since 2013, Chapman was not tops: St. Louis Cardinals relief pitcher Jordan Hicks, nine years younger, claimed the title with a 100.5-m.p.h. average, according to Fangraphs.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s no problem for me,\xe2\x80\x9d Chapman, 31, said in Spanish recently, adding later: \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not 20 or 22 anymore. The years pile up \xe2\x80\x94 not by choice.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'So some eyebrows, including those of the injured and inconsistent Yankees (8-10), were understandably raised when Chapman began this season \xe2\x80\x94 albeit in the early April chill of the Northeast \xe2\x80\x94 throwing fastballs as slow as 92 m.p.h. It was yet another potentially worrying issue for a Yankees bullpen that has not lived up to lofty expectations, and for the depleted roster in general.'
b'But Chapman himself was not concerned \xe2\x80\x94 and with reason. In keeping with past early-season buildups, his speed has ticked up each outing, reaching 98 to 100 m.p.h. with ease in his two most recent appearances. In the admittedly small sample size of seven games, his average was back up to 97.1 m.p.h. entering Friday night\xe2\x80\x99s game against the Kansas City Royals.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cAs I get more work, I feel much better and my arm\xe2\x80\x99s better, too, as well as my velocity and my pitches,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chapman. \xe2\x80\x9cLittle by little. A lot of people worry about velocity, but I\xe2\x80\x99m not worried about it.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'But as his average picks up with the temperature and innings, where will it ultimately settle?'
b'Chapman, a five-time All-Star, is still an outlier given how long he has held his velocity. Yet as he continues aging, not even Chapman thinks he will average 100 m.p.h. for an entire season anymore. Nor does he want to, especially with the resurgence of his wipeout slider.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cMy first few years, yes, I was trying to throw hard; it\xe2\x80\x99s what I had,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut through the years, your mentality starts changing. My objective now isn\xe2\x80\x99t to throw as hard but to pitch. For example: tossing innings with as few pitches as possible or throwing first-pitch strikes to every batter.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'There were times last season, as Chapman\xe2\x80\x99s velocity hovered around 97 m.p.h., that Larry Rothschild, the Yankees\xe2\x80\x99 longtime pitching coach, thought it might the new norm.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cBut then you\xe2\x80\x99d see the big numbers,\xe2\x80\x9d Rothschild said. \xe2\x80\x9cAs it warms up and he gets out there, you\xe2\x80\x99ll still see them. But he\xe2\x80\x99s thrown a lot of fastballs at a very high velocity, so at some point, you\xe2\x80\x99re going to see a little bit of a dip.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'It feels preposterous to doubt whether 97 m.p.h. is enough: Many professional pitchers are unable to throw that hard, let alone average it for an entire six-month season \xe2\x80\x94 and for many years. The average fastball in baseball last season was 92.8 m.p.h., according to Fangraphs. But Chapman is so freakishly athletic that he set a new norm.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe years have gone by, and back then, \xe2\x80\x98the Cuban Missile\xe2\x80\x99 was good,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut before, how many pitchers were throwing that hard? Maybe two or three pitchers throwing over 100.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'There are more now who can reach it, even starting pitchers. Chapman marveled at Hicks\xe2\x80\x99s arm, which uncorked a 105 m.p.h. fastball in a game last May, matching Chapman for the fastest pitch in recorded baseball history. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd there are some hard throwers who haven\xe2\x80\x99t reached the big leagues yet,\xe2\x80\x9d Chapman said.'
b'Even though Chapman has mitigated his aging process by bulking up his once-gangly 6-foot-4 frame, his arm will continue to lose power over time. His fastball has already induced fewer swings and misses each year (2017 and 2018 were his lowest rates since the start of his career).'
b'Still, as Chapman pointed out, even 96-m.p.h. fastballs would be more than enough to succeed in the majors \xe2\x80\x94 as long as he still gets outs. And in his six outings this season entering Friday, he mostly has, allowing two runs, taking one loss, striking out nine and walking one.'
b'What has helped Chapman already is his slider. Before departing via free agency after last season, reliever David Robertson helped Chapman tweak the grip on his slider, which led to sharper movement, and it proved successful in the second half.'
b'Chapman used his slider a career-high 25 percent of the time last season, partly at the Yankees\xe2\x80\x99 urging. Opponents hit .106 against it, and whiffed 62 percent of the time they swung \xe2\x80\x94 the highest rate for a slider among relief pitchers in baseball last season, according to Baseball Prospectus.'
b'And so far this season, the third of Chapman\xe2\x80\x99s five-year, $86 million deal with the Yankees, he is using the slider even more: about 31 percent of the time entering Friday. He said he had never felt better with it.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s why the slider is not only a pitch here and now while he still has electric stuff, but I think, as long as he wants to do this and he\xe2\x80\x99s healthy, that gives him a pitch that\xe2\x80\x99s going to give him longevity,\xe2\x80\x9d Yankees Manager Aaron Boone said last month, adding later: \xe2\x80\x9cIt really impressed me last year when he started to use it more. There were times he was able to strike that pitch better than his fastball.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'That is not to say that Chapman\xe2\x80\x99s trademark pitch is feeble just yet. After seeing fastballs of 95 and 96 m.p.h. from Chapman in the ninth inning of an April 6 game, Baltimore Orioles outfielder Trey Mancini struck out on a 98 m.p.h. offering down the middle of the plate.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt seemed even harder,\xe2\x80\x9d Mancini said. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought that it was probably 100, just from the eye test. It\xe2\x80\x99s still an electric pitch. He\xe2\x80\x99s a really tall guy and when he releases it, the ball seems like it\xe2\x80\x99s right on top of you.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Two days before that, Mancini saw three sliders from Chapman, lining out on the final one.'

b'WASHINGTON \xe2\x80\x94 A beard on his face, a new catcher in front of him and Robinson Cano at his back: Plenty had changed in Jacob deGrom\xe2\x80\x99s world between the time he won the National League Cy Young Award last fall and when he took the mound for the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 season opener on Thursday.'

b' THE PERSONALITIES'
b'Many people, and a cat, played a part in the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 road to a championship.'
b'By Jay Schreiber'
b'He wore the uniform of the Brooklyn Dodgers, the New York Giants, the Yankees and the Mets. Although he was often depicted as engaging in \xe2\x80\x9cStengelese,\xe2\x80\x9d Robert Lipsyte, who covered the early Mets for The Times, said that was nonsense. \xe2\x80\x9cHis hourslong monologues made perfect sense,\xe2\x80\x9d he wrote. And if you listened, you would hear the always quotable, always clever Stengel advise young players to \xe2\x80\x9cget in shape now, you can drink during the season,\xe2\x80\x9d and proclaim after another loss that \xe2\x80\x9cthe attendance got robbed.\xe2\x80\x9d In every way, Lipsyte said, Stengel was the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 leading man.'
b'His real name was Marvin Eugene Throneberry, which meant his initials spelled MET. Originally a Yankee, he joined the Mets in May 1962 and, in the spirit of things, immediately started tripping over himself. His crowning moment came in a game in June when, playing first base, he was called for interference in the top of the first and then, in the bottom of the inning, failed to touch first and second base after hitting a triple. When a teammate, Charlie Neal, followed with a home run, Stengel came out of the dugout and pointed at each base for Neal\xe2\x80\x99s benefit.'
b'Long before he won the Pulitzer Prize for his newspaper columns, Breslin wrote a slim, engaging account of the 1962 Mets, \xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t Anybody Here Play This Game?\xe2\x80\x9d From Breslin\xe2\x80\x99s gleefully subversive point of view, the Mets were nothing less than great comedy, as good as Bert Lahr in \xe2\x80\x9cThe Wizard of Oz\xe2\x80\x9d or the Marx Brothers in \xe2\x80\x9cRoom Service.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cTake any day, any town, any inning,\xe2\x80\x9d Breslin wrote of the season. \xe2\x80\x9cWith the Mets, nothing changed, only the pages on the calendar. It was all one wonderful mistake.\xe2\x80\x9d Before long, Breslin\xe2\x80\x99s book became a byword for the Mets. They had, with his help, become even more amusing.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe Mets Is Coming,\xe2\x80\x9d declared one of the banners that Mets fans brought to the Bronx \xe2\x80\x94 although not into the stadium \xe2\x80\x94 on the night of June 20, 1963, for the first Mayor\xe2\x80\x99s Trophy game. It was a matchup of the worst team in baseball and the mighty Yankees. The banner may not have made it inside Yankee Stadium but tens of thousands of Mets fans did. Amazingly, the Mets won, 6-2, although, to be fair, the Yankees had played a regular-season game earlier that day. Still, as Robert Lipsyte wrote in The Times afterward: \xe2\x80\x9cA frightened man told his foreman to shut up, a beaten dog bit a cruel master. The long shot came home and beautiful, blue-eyed tomorrow finally arrived.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'She was a wealthy society matron who was at home at the racetrack and at the ballpark, and in 1962 she became the first owner of the Mets. She had been a minority stockholder in the New York Giants, and had even tried to block their move to San Francisco. That didn\xe2\x80\x99t work, but then along came the Mets, a team whose games she would sometimes follow at the track with a transistor radio stuck to her ear. Despite all her money, she was, said the writer Frank Sullivan, \xe2\x80\x9ca simple, generous woman with no swank.\xe2\x80\x9d And all her considerable patience was ultimately rewarded with a championship.'
b'The Mets had begun their existence with a lot of players who were near the end of their careers. So it was hardly surprising that Snider, a Brooklyn hero who had gone to Los Angeles with the rest of the Dodgers, was back in 1963 as a Met. In Brooklyn, he had been a graceful center fielder, famous for sharing that position in New York with Willie Mays and Mickey Mantle. In 1963, at age 36, he usually played right field or left. And as a Met, he hit 14 homers, batted .243 and then went to San Francisco for the 1964 season, where he ended his career amid a lot less losing.'
b'The original bedsheet banners that popped up at the Polo Grounds in 1962 and 1963 essentially came out of nowhere. There was no internet to organize the effort; no publicity campaign by the Mets to get things going. Instead, there were just banners, some of them pretty clever. But soon enough, the Mets tried to put a more respectable gloss on the phenomenon by creating an annual Banner Day. And for years at Shea Stadium, it proved to be a popular event, even if the banners tended to be a little more cute (see above, 1965) and a little less irreverent than the originals.'
b' As a Yankee, he won 10 championships and three Most Valuable Player awards before retiring in 1963. A year later, the Yankees fired him after just one season as their manager. And by the following May, Berra, about to turn 40, was back in uniform, for the Mets, but as a catcher and pinch-hitter. The Berra experiment did not last long. Four games, including two behind the plate. Nine at-bats. Two singles. At that point, Berra proclaimed himself \xe2\x80\x9can old man\xe2\x80\x9d and retired again. But 1969 would bring him his 11th championship, as the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 first-base coach.'
b'He was a fixture at first base for the Brooklyn Dodgers in the 1950s, and the ball landed in his glove for the last out of the 1955 World Series, the only time those Dodgers won it all. He finished his playing career with the Mets, in 1962 and 1963, and by 1968 he was managing them, his powerful physical presence allowing him to convey a quiet, unbreakable authority. George Vecsey, a longtime sports columnist for The New York Times, said he came to think of Hodges \xe2\x80\x9cas one of the most decent, straight, strong-minded managers I ever met.\xe2\x80\x9d It was a strength that \xe2\x80\x9cmade you listen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom Seaver, and it would propel the Mets in 1969.'
b'At 36, Charles was the elder statesman of the 1969 Mets, a team with many key players in their mid-20s. The team\xe2\x80\x99s part-time third baseman and sometime poet, Charles grew up in the segregated South of the 1930s and \xe2\x80\x9940s and as a teenager he drew inspiration from spotting Jackie Robinson in spring training. He once even ran after a train on which Robinson was riding with his Dodger teammates. Charles hit only .207 in 1969, but he started the winning rally in Game 2 of the World Series. And at the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 victory parade, he read a poem he had written while stuck in the minor leagues.'
b'He joined the Mets in June 1969 in a trade with the Montreal Expos and represented the only significant pickup the Mets would make during the season. He turned 34 a month later, making him the second-oldest player on the team. Like Charles, a fellow African-American, he became a veteran leader on a team that was mostly white. Tall and powerful, Clendenon became part of the four platoons that Manager Gil Hodges used daily. He played first base against left-handed pitchers; Ed Kranepool played against righties. And it was Clendenon, who later earned a law degree, who became a huge factor in the World Series.'
b'He was born in 1931, played for the same Negro league team that Jackie Robinson did and in 1953 became the first African-American player on the Chicago Cubs. In the years that followed, Banks became one of the best and most popular players in the game, first as the Cubs\xe2\x80\x99 power-hitting shortstop and later as the team\xe2\x80\x99s first baseman. He also became known as Mr. Cub, the optimist who endured one losing season after another and still hoped for better. And 1969 seemed to be that season, the one that would get him to his first World Series. It didn\xe2\x80\x99t happen.'
b'In 1948, Durocher was the colorful, controversial manager of the Brooklyn Dodgers and Gil Hodges was his rookie first baseman. But by July of that year, Durocher had left Brooklyn to become the manager of the Giants. After that, it was in the caldron of Dodgers vs. Giants that Hodges and Durocher sized each other up. In 1969 they were squaring off again, Durocher managing the Cubs, Hodges running the Mets; Durocher still fiery and quotable, Hodges just the opposite. But Hodges got the last word when the Mets beat out the Cubs.'
b'And what were the Yankees up to in 1969? What were they up to while the Mets pulled off one of the great reversals of all time? The answer is nothing much. The Yankees went 80-81, continuing a decline that came out of nowhere in 1965. The manager was Ralph Houk, in his second stint in that role. In his first, he had led the Yankees to World Series titles in 1961 and 1962. But in \xe2\x80\x9969 the Yankees weren\xe2\x80\x99t winning. The best player on the team was Mel Stottlemyre, who went 20-14. Notable teammates included Gene Michael, Joe Pepitone, Horace Clarke and Bobby Murcer. All of them were overshadowed by the events in Queens.'
b'The cat showed up uninvited, strolling right into the middle of a crucial Mets-Cubs game at Shea Stadium on Sept. 9, 1969. More precisely, it chose the top of the fourth to make its appearance, walking behind Cubs third baseman Ron Santo, who was in the on-deck circle, and then parading past the Cubs\xe2\x80\x99 dugout. At the time, the Cubs were losing, 2-0, and the Mets had Tom Seaver on the mound. A black cat was not what the Cubs needed. In the end, the Cubs did get a run that inning, but that was it. The Mets won, 7-1, and moved to half a game behind the Cubs in the standings. The cat disappeared, its work done.'
b'He was the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 Sign Man, the guy with the degree in design art from Pratt Institute who, from his seat at Shea Stadium, would hold up preprinted placards throughout the game to comment on the action. He was, in effect, picking up where all the fans\xe2\x80\x99 bedsheet banners left off. A banner hung from a railing and stayed there. But Ehrhardt, with his bag of 20-by-26-inch placards by his side, could go with the flow as the game proceeded. He said he liked to anticipate what might happen in a game by studying beforehand. He began holding up his signs when Shea opened. Five years, and a lot of bad baseball later, he, too, made it to the postseason.'
b'The Mets confronted one future Hall of Famer after another on their way to the championship in 1969, but not one was better than Atlanta\xe2\x80\x99s Hank Aaron, the Braves\xe2\x80\x99 slugger and right fielder. And in the three-game National League Championship Series against the Mets, the 35-year-old Aaron was superb: three home runs and two doubles in 14 at-bats. But all of his heroics at the plate couldn\xe2\x80\x99t bring the Braves a single victory against a Mets team that normally relied on pitching, but simply outhit Atlanta in the N.L.C.S. Afterward, Aaron called the Mets \xe2\x80\x9camazing.\xe2\x80\x9d Casey Stengel had often called the Mets that, too, but with a big wink. Aaron was serious.'
b'The ebullient left-hander was the busiest member of the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 bullpen in 1969, throwing 100\xe2\x85\x93 innings while compiling a 9-3 record and a 2.24 E.R.A. while picking up 12 saves. And yet he never got to pitch in the World Series, when the Mets used only six pitchers from start to finish. McGraw did, however, appear in Game 2 of the N.L.C.S., throwing three scoreless innings to close out an 11-6 victory for the Mets. And in the bottom of the ninth of that game, after the first two Braves hitters reached base, McGraw struck out Hank Aaron looking.'

b'Memorial Stadium, Baltimore | Saturday, Oct. 11, 1969 '
b'First pitch: Bowie Kuhn, baseball commissioner National anthem: Joseph Eubanks, opera singer'
b'Leonard Koppett covered the World Series for The New York Times. Following are excerpts from his articles about the first four games.'
b'BALTIMORE, Oct. 11 \xe2\x80\x94 The euphoric suspicion that the New York Mets might never lose another game was dispelled conclusively today as the Baltimore Orioles defeated them, 4-1, in the opening game of the World Series.'
b'Mike Cuellar, the 32-year-old left-handed Cuban, pitched a strong six-hitter, striking out eight men. Tom Seaver, the most glamorous Met, also gave up six hits, but one was a leadoff home run by Don Buford in the first inning and four more hits came with two out in the fourth, when the Orioles scored their three other runs.'
b'So Seaver, who left after five innings, suffered his first defeat since early August after 11 consecutive victories. And the Mets, for the first time in three weeks, lost a game that counted.'
b'The Orioles, however, were not concerned with Met statistics. Having run away with the American League\xe2\x80\x99s Eastern Division title (by 19 games) and polished off Minnesota in three straight in the championship series, they extended some streaks of their own by taking a 1-0 lead in the four-of-seven Series.'
b'The only other time a Baltimore team had played in the Series was in 1966, when the Orioles swept the Los Angeles Dodgers, winning the last two games by 1-0 scores here in Memorial Stadium.'
b'Thus, when the Mets finally scored on a sacrifice fly by Al Weis in the seventh inning, the run ended a string of 39 consecutive scoreless innings posted by Baltimore pitchers in Series competition. The Orioles also stand at this moment as the only team that has won every Series game it has played.'
b'The Mets could have had that distinction had they won today, but the only real threat they mounted against Cuellar came too late because Seaver did not approach the form that had brought him 25 victories during the regular National League season.'
b'In his last start, against Atlanta in the opening game of the pennant playoff there, he was also hit hard, but he emerged as the winning pitcher. This time, he didn\xe2\x80\x99t get away with it.'
b'Memorial Stadium, Baltimore | Sunday, Oct. 12, 1969 '
b'First pitch: Clara Merritt-Ruth, second wife of Babe Ruth National anthem: York Suburban High School band'
b'BALTIMORE, Oct. 12 \xe2\x80\x94 Amid the sort of ninth-inning melodrama that makes baseball legends, the New York Mets evened the World Series at one game apiece today by beating the Baltimore Orioles, 2-1.'
b'Three straight singles with two out, by Ed Charles, Jerry Grote and Al Weis, gave the Mets the go-ahead run in the top half of the ninth inning. Then Jerry Koosman, who had held Baltimore hitless for six innings before yielding the tying run in the seventh, needed Ron Taylor\xe2\x80\x99s help to nail down the final out with the potential tying and winning runs on base.'
b'For the Mets and their followers, the entire final inning was a moment of truth.'
b'If they lost this game, especially after Koosman\xe2\x80\x99s fine two-hit effort, their position would approach hopelessness.'
b'Dave McNally had been tagged for a home run by Donn Clendenon in the fourth. The Orioles then made it 1-1.'
b'Now, with two outs in the top of the ninth, Charles, the 36-year-old third baseman to whom this World Series is an unexpected and immeasurable experience, bounced a single through the left side, his second hit in a row.'
b'Grote also singled on a hit-and-run play and Charles reached third. Then Weis, the unappreciated infielder, lined McNally\xe2\x80\x99s first pitch to left for a single, scoring Charles.'
b'Now it was up to Koosman, who needed three more outs. He retired Don Buford and Paul Blair but that brought up Frank Robinson, as feared a batter as baseball can provide.'
b'Robinson walked. So did huge Boog Powell. Out of the dugout came Manager Gil Hodges, out of the game came Koosman, and out of the bullpen came Taylor to face Brooks Robinson.'
b'On a full count, Robinson then swung and hit a sharp grounder to Charles, wide of third. Charles started to run the few steps to third base for a forceout, saw he didn\xe2\x80\x99t have a play, and threw to first. Clendenon dug up the low throw and Robinson was out.'
b'The Series was even at a game apiece.'
b'Shea Stadium, Queens | Tuesday, Oct. 14, 1969 '
b'First pitch: Roy Campanella, Hall of Fame catcher National anthem: Steve Lawrence, actor/singer'
b'The victory scene in the New York Met dressing room, once a subject for jokes or dreams, is becoming routine pandemonium. The players are getting used to it and more polished in handling the invading army of interviewers.'
b'However, Tommie Agee, the superhero of Game 3 of the World Series, wasn\xe2\x80\x99t in the dressing room. He was off in a special conference room, where superheroes are subjected to mass questioning, and later was on television. He did come in for a while to stand on a table and answer more massed questions and then went out again. An hour and a half after the game was over he was still in his uniform, finally seated before his locker in an emptying room, answering questions again.'
b'Most dealt with the two great catches, the one that ended the fourth inning with two men racing for home plate and the one that ended the seventh with three base runners and the batter, Paul Blair, all intent on scoring (Elrod Hendricks had hit the first one).'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI was shading him a little bit toward right field,\xe2\x80\x9d said Agee of Hendricks, who bats left-handed. Hendricks hit a high drive toward the left-center-field fence, and Agee and Cleon Jones convened on it. \xe2\x80\x9cI saw the ball well because the sky was cloudy, not bright, but I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t sure I could reach it. I figure, if I can touch a ball I can hold it.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'He held it, in the webbing between the thumb and body of the glove, with which he had reached across his body.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI knew it would be tough because it was away from my glove side,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.'
b'Blair\xe2\x80\x99s ball, Agee felt, was less difficult.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s an easier play because it was on my glove side,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cand I didn\xe2\x80\x99t have as far to go, but the wind kept taking it away and I had to dive.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Most observers, however, voted for the second catch as more remarkable \xe2\x80\x94 among them, Manager Gil Hodges.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThey were both great, but the second one was the greatest,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hodges, who has seen a lot of World Series in the past 22 years as a player or spectator. He was pressed to issue a rating.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d have to say the second one was No. 1 of any World Series catch I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied.'
b'Shea Stadium, Queens | Wednesday, Oct. 15, 1969 '
b'First pitch: Casey Stengel, Hall of Fame manager National anthem: Gordon MacRae, actor/singer'
b'Following is an excerpt from an article published Oct. 16, 1969.'
b'Neither the umpires nor the Baltimore Orioles noted that the New York Mets scored the winning run in Game 4 of the World Series on an illegal play.'
b'The run scored because the throw by Orioles pitcher Pete Richert to first, on J. C. Martin\xe2\x80\x99s bunt in the bottom of the 10th, was deflected by Martin\xe2\x80\x99s left wrist a few strides before he reached the base. The ball bounced away from second baseman Dave Johnson, who was waiting for the throw, and only after that did pinch-runner Rod Gaspar, who had gone from second to third, continue home with the run that ended the game.'
b'But Martin, running to first base, was not in the lane that exists specifically to prevent this sort of interference with a throw. Pictures show that he ran on the left side of the foul line instead of the right side, where he is supposed to run when there is a possibility of a throw being made to first base from behind him.'
b'There is a special marking for this lane: a white line, three feet to the foul side and parallel to the foul line, extending the last 45 feet of the distance between home plate and first base.'
b'No umpire called this infraction, for which Martin should have been declared out \xe2\x80\x94 and no Baltimore player or coach protested the oversight.'
b'If Martin had been called out, the game would have continued with one out, Gaspar on third, Al Weis on second and Tommie Agee coming to bat, with Bud Harrelson due to bat.'
b'The 3-foot lane has been the source of controversy in several American League games during the last two years. On 99 plays out of a hundred, the lane doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter, since it takes effect only if there is a throw from behind the runner. Which is what happened in Game 4.'
b'Unlike photographs of some controversial plays, pictures of this play leave no room for ambiguity. Martin had both feet on the wrong side of the foul line. The throw hit his left wrist. It could not have done that if he had been in the proper lane.'
b'The home-plate umpire in Game 4 was a National Leaguer, Shag Crawford, but the play could also have been called by the first-base umpire, Lou DiMuro, of the American League.'
b'A complete game from Koosman and complete lunacy in New York as the Mets dot the \xe2\x80\x98i\xe2\x80\x99 in impossible.'
b'Shea Stadium, Queens | Thursday, Oct. 16, 1969 '
b'First pitch: Joe DiMaggio, Hall of Fame outfielder National anthem: Pearl Bailey, actress/singer'
b'This article was originally published in The Times on Oct. 17, 1969.'
b'By Leonard Koppett'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWe did it all.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Ron Swoboda said it, and in saying it, he said it all. The New York Mets had their biggest, their longest, their noisiest, their sweetest and their third victory celebration in 22 days at Shea Stadium yesterday.'
b'On Wednesday night, Sept. 24, they clinched first place in the National League East. Champagne splashed like bath water, and everyone got splashed by it.'
b'On Monday afternoon, Oct. 6, they completed a three-game sweep of Atlanta in the first \xe2\x80\x9cchampionship series\xe2\x80\x9d to determine the National League pennant winner. The activity was similar, but the intensity was less, and the World Series loomed ahead.'
b'And now, on a mid-October Thursday a good two weeks before Halloween, the Mets had won the World Series and this was positively the last \xe2\x80\x94 and wildest \xe2\x80\x94 champagne shower.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThis is the summit,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Ed Charles, 36 years old and, until a couple of months ago, beyond hoping for this particular thrill. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re No. 1 in the world and you just can\xe2\x80\x99t get any bigger than this.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cSome people still might not believe in us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Cleon Jones, \xe2\x80\x9cbut then, some people still think the world is flat.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt boiled down to this,\xe2\x80\x9d declared Bud Harrelson. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen it had to be done, we did it and they didn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The tumult was augmented by the crowded condition of an otherwise ample dressing room. This time, the crush of interviewers, cameramen and others carrying various equipment was as dense as the subway at peak rush hour, far denser than for the other two celebrations. No one could move a full step in any direction without pushing someone else aside.'
b'That was the main dressing room. But the office of the manager, which is to the left of the entrance door (while the corridor leading into the rest of the clubhouse leads off to the right) was an oasis of order.'
b'Manager Gil Hodges at first was in the mob outside. The first one into the empty office was Gil Jr., the C.W. Post College student, who hastily climbed out of his civilian clothes and into his Met uniform for the sweaty hour to follow.'
b'Then came his mother, and the older two of his three sisters.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re champions,\xe2\x80\x9d cried out Joan Hodges, who has lived through her husband\xe2\x80\x99s baseball triumphs and setbacks with such fervor all these years. \xe2\x80\x9cImagine, we did it!\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'A moment later, the head of the family entered, and Joan hugged him and squealed:'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cYou know what we are? You know? Champions. We\xe2\x80\x99re champions of the world.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Hodges hugged her back, and his daughters. And they kissed him, and he grinned, and Gil Jr. put his arms around them from behind while the Mets photographer snapped a family portrait.'
b'And then the stream of interviewers began to trickle in, filling the office with bodies but not with any of the hysteria outside. Hodges, as he has always been, was calm, speaking softly but distinctly, mopping his face, listening attentively, answering carefully, smiling quickly \xe2\x80\x94 working, in a way, harder than during the game.'
b'Mrs. Joan Payson, the team\xe2\x80\x99s owner, arrived, too.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cOh my, oh my,\xe2\x80\x9d she kept repeating. \xe2\x80\x9cOh my.\xe2\x80\x9d Donald Grant, the chairman of the board, was there, and then Johnny Murphy, who actually ran the club as general manager, and Georgie Weiss who had started it, and other members of the Mets family.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it\xe2\x80\x99s the same and it\xe2\x80\x99s different,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hodges, trying to answer questions about how this victory felt alongside other World Series triumphs he experienced with the Dodgers, in 1955 and 1959. \xe2\x80\x9cAs a player, and as a manager, it\xe2\x80\x99s different \xe2\x80\x94 it\xe2\x80\x99s hard to explain. In a way this means more. I\xe2\x80\x99m so proud of them all.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Did he think Donn Clendenon was the most valuable player, worthy of the car given for that distinction?'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s not my job,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gil. \xe2\x80\x9cI think of all 25 of them \xe2\x80\x94 and four coaches. Let\xe2\x80\x99s see, that\xe2\x80\x99s 29.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the manager,\xe2\x80\x9d someone suggested.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, not the manager,\xe2\x80\x9d demurred Hodges.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe manager already has a car,\xe2\x80\x9d a voice interrupted, and everyone laughed, including Hodges.'
b'Later it became celebrity time \xe2\x80\x94 Pearl Bailey, who kissed Hodges, called him a \xe2\x80\x9ccool cat,\xe2\x80\x9d and wound up dancing in the main room with Charles while a record player blared the singalong record the Met squad had made two weeks ago; Robert Merrill, the opera star, who sat with the Met party all three days at Shea and was still shaking. Bill Shea, who set the whole thing in motion and for whom the stadium is named, saying \xe2\x80\x9cin my lifetime \xe2\x80\x94 they did it in my lifetime!\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'After an hour or more, Joan Hodges was casting anxious glances at her husband \xe2\x80\x94 who, after all, had suffered a heart attack only 13 months before and whose marvelous discipline and recovery can\xe2\x80\x99t eliminate worry. Only two days ago, however, a doctor\xe2\x80\x99s examination had shown Gil in fine shape.'
b'But sporadic yelling and singing were still going on in the clubhouse, the floor slippery, the room less crowded, but few players dressed. In another hour or two, they would go upstairs to the Diamond Club for a real party, with their wives and the whole Met official family.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the first one,\xe2\x80\x9d Swoboda had shouted at the beginning, \xe2\x80\x9cand the sweetest, and because it\xe2\x80\x99s the first, nothing can ever be that sweet again.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'In other words, all they have to look forward to is repetition.'
b'The Final Out: The announcer Curt Gowdy on NBC: \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a fly ball out to left. Waiting is Jones \xe2\x80\xa6 the Mets are the world champions! Jerry Koosman is being mobbed! Look at this scene!\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'In both years, the World Series lasted five games, but the similarities pretty much ended there. Daytime games then, nighttime now. Complete games then, an army of relievers now. And so on. However, each Series did have a first baseman who excelled and did so with virtually the same statistics. What was very different was the vehicle they drove away with as the Series M.V.P.'
