b'President Trump killed a deal that would have allowed Cuban baseball players to enter the United States legally and safely.'
b'By The Editorial Board'
b'The editorial board represents the opinions of the board, its editor and the publisher. It is separate from the newsroom and the Op-Ed section.'
b'When President Barack Obama began thawing long-frozen relations with Cuba \xe2\x80\x94 a drive that included attending a baseball game in Havana alongside President Ra\xc3\xbal Castro \xe2\x80\x94 Major League Baseball began negotiating with the Cuban Baseball Federation to start legally bringing Cuban stars to play in the United States. The deal, heralded as a way to combat the illegal cross-border smuggling of ballplayers, was finally clinched in December, and the Cuban federation sent over its first list of 34 candidates on April 3.'
b'They won\xe2\x80\x99t be coming.'
b'At least not legally, after the Trump administration abruptly ended the deal, announcing last Monday that the Cuban federation was not independent of the Cuban government, as the Obama administration had ruled, and so paying it the fees mandated in the agreement would be a violation of American trade rules.'
b'Officials in the Trump administration also linked the reversal to Cuba\xe2\x80\x99s support for the Venezuelan dictator Nicol\xc3\xa1s Maduro, whom Washington is trying to oust. \xe2\x80\x9cAmerica\xe2\x80\x99s national pastime should not enable the Cuban regime\xe2\x80\x99s support for Maduro in Venezuela,\xe2\x80\x9d tweeted John Bolton, the national security adviser, on the day before the baseball deal was canceled.'
b'The administration\xe2\x80\x99s arguments are not without merit. No organization as prominent as the Cuban Baseball Federation can be fully independent of the Havana government. And Cuba has remained a firm ally of Mr. Maduro, receiving Venezuelan oil in exchange for doctors and other specialists and intelligence, which has helped him remain in power despite demands from Washington and more than 50 other governments that he end his terrible rule.'
b'But that is not entirely what this is about. Mr. Obama\xe2\x80\x99s effort to end more than five decades of hostility toward Cuba was approved by a large majority of Americans as an opening that was long overdue. Allowing some players from baseball-mad Cuba to play legally in the major leagues was a win-win proposition: Players who might have risked dangerous flight could legally reach for stardom and wealth; Cuban baseball would make some money; and their presence would be tangible evidence of a crack in the ice. Accepting the myth of an independent Cuban Baseball Federation was deemed a necessary wink.'
b'The thaw was bitterly opposed from the outset by anti-Castro Republicans, including Senator Marco Rubio of Florida, a Cuban-American, and President Trump soon set about reversing some of what Mr. Obama had done. The baseball deal was in the opponents\xe2\x80\x99 cross hairs even before the drive to oust Mr. Maduro began. Mr. Rubio assailed it repeatedly, and in December, before Elliott Abrams became Mr. Trump\xe2\x80\x99s special representative for Venezuela, he wrote a scathing attack in National Review.'
b'Yet canceling the deal was a bad move done for the wrong reasons. Cuba\xe2\x80\x99s decision to let its athletes earn their living outside Cuba was a step in the right direction, even if some dollars might have spilled into government coffers. And while Cuba should be dissuaded from propping up the Maduro regime, that should not be a pretext for indulging the right-wing obsession with maintaining a permanent freeze on relations with Cuba.'
b'In the end, what Mr. Rubio and the administration largely achieved was to deny Cuban baseball players their right to play at the highest level without having to sneak circuitously and dangerously into the United States and to forgo the right to ever return to their homeland.'
b'The Times is committed to publishing a diversity of letters to the editor. We\xe2\x80\x99d like to hear what you think about this or any of our articles. Here are some tips. And here\xe2\x80\x99s our email: letters@nytimes.com.'
b'Follow The New York Times Opinion section on Facebook, Twitter (@NYTopinion) and Instagram.'

b'By Victor Mather'
b'A machine helping with ball-and-strike calls. An end to the hallowed 60 feet 6 inches between the pitcher\xe2\x80\x99s rubber and the plate. Bigger bases.'
b'At the behest of Major League Baseball, the Atlantic League, an independent minor league based mostly in the Northeast, will try out several rule changes in the coming season. M.L.B. said its goal was \xe2\x80\x9cto create more balls in play, defensive action, base running, and improve player safety.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Baseball made no promises about eventually including the changes in the majors, but presumably the experiment is not merely for academic interest.'
b'The changes are:'
b'\xe2\x96\xa0 The umpire will be assisted by a radar system that tracks where balls land in the strike zone. Baseball did not elaborate on how the system \xe2\x80\x94 called TrackMan \xe2\x80\x94 would be used, whether umpires could consult it if they chose, or whether it would overrule calls made by the umpires.'
b'\xe2\x96\xa0 No players or coaches will be allowed to visit the mound unless a pitching change is being made. This move would seem designed to speed games, which can be slowed by frequent conferences at the mound.'
b'\xe2\x96\xa0 Pitchers will be required to face a minimum of three batters. This move, which has also been proposed for the major leagues, is designed to speed the game by reducing pitching changes, which have proliferated in the modern game.'
b'\xe2\x96\xa0 Defensive shifts will be limited, as teams will be required to have two players on each side of second base. Many fans dislike the elaborate shifting that has come into play in recent years, feeling that infielders\xe2\x80\x99 positioning has become too different from the traditional placements they are used to.'
b'\xe2\x96\xa0 Time between innings will be reduced to 1 minute 45 seconds from 2:05, another timesaving move.'
b'\xe2\x96\xa0 The distance from the pitcher\xe2\x80\x99s rubber to home plate will be increased to 62 feet 6 inches, a two-foot increase. Such a move would be an aid to batters, and would presumably cut down on strikeouts, which have drastically increased in recent years.'
b'\xe2\x96\xa0 All the infield bases \xe2\x80\x94 first, second and third \xe2\x80\x94 will be increased in size, to 18 inches from 15 inches. A larger base would provide more room for both a fielder and a runner to have a part of the bag.'
b'M.L.B. said it would consider changing or dropping some of the rules at the halfway point in the season, depending on their effectiveness.'
b'M.L.B. has a three-year deal with the Atlantic League, covering selling of players, scouting, statistics and other matters.'

b'By Kevin Armstrong'
b'PORT ST. LUCIE, Fla. \xe2\x80\x94 Jeff McNeil, the Met with the most mitts in his clubhouse locker, was encouraged to reorganize his stall during spring training. He was rotating between third base and left field, depending on the day, and batting anywhere from leadoff to eighth, so the third-base coach, Gary DiSarcina, sat down and explained to him that he needed to eliminate some clutter.'
b'McNeil proceeded to pull eight gloves from his locker and pile them on the floor. The last one he retrieved was the most worn.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the gamer!\xe2\x80\x9d he said.'
b'McNeil, 27, is the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 most valuable grinder. His blond goatee whiskers and notable nimbleness earned him the nickname Squirrel during his days diving for line drives as an outfielder at Long Beach State, and he has continued to pounce on any opportunity.'
b'In his first major league at-bat last summer, he swung on the first pitch he saw for a base hit to center field, and he has kept connecting with his knobless bat ever since, pulling doubles into right field and smacking the ball to left against shifts with a stroke he considers a product of his golfing past. On Saturday night against the Atlanta Braves, he set a new Mets record with 90 hits in his first 76 games in the majors.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cBefore he goes to bat, I love telling him, \xe2\x80\x98Go get squirrelly for us real quick!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d first baseman Dominic Smith said. \xe2\x80\x9cSquirrel hits are the best hits.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Squirrel fielding, however, can be more of an adventure. One afternoon early in spring training, he was manning left field when he tried to track a line drive from Miami\xe2\x80\x99s Isaac Galloway. McNeil, wearing shades and still reorienting himself to the position after playing second and third last season, backpedaled before the wind blew the ball over his right shoulder. He turned all the way around in time for the ball to hit the palm of his glove. It then popped out, and McNeil crashed into the warning-track dirt.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cDid the most athletic thing that I could,\xe2\x80\x9d said McNeil, who continues to take extra repetitions in the outfield before games to work on his route running, with the quality-control coach Luis Rojas serving as his tutor. \xe2\x80\x9cI make that catch almost every time.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'McNeil makes up for any missteps in the field with his extraordinary resourcefulness, which has proved indispensable for the Mets thus far. For all their talk about their depth, the Mets have found themselves relying heavily on McNeil since infielders Todd Frazier and Jed Lowrie went down with injuries in spring training. Neither has played this season.'
b'And McNeil has managed to consistently be in the middle of the action in the early part of this season, whether it was stroking a 400-foot triple, collecting four hits in a game, being hit by pitches three times in two days, being caught in rundowns twice or the time he made two of the three outs in an inning. In essence, McNeil can be thrilling and a little maddening.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI think baseball is kind of coming back around to those guys,\xe2\x80\x9d right fielder Michael Conforto said of his teammate. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not so much about the guys who just launch balls. I think you are seeing teams value guys who play a lot of positions and do all the little things right: Steal bases, play defense and spray the ball all over the field.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'With his uncommon hand-eye coordination at the plate, McNeil\xe2\x80\x99s offense is ultimately the reason Manager Mickey Callaway must find places for him to play. Though he can appear off-balance in the batter\xe2\x80\x99s box at times, McNeil patiently keeps his hands back to wait on breaking balls. To Callaway, McNeil\xe2\x80\x99s approach ranks somewhere on the swing spectrum between Wade Boggs and Ichiro Suzuki.'
b'And no matter what happens at the plate, teammates and coaches eagerly await McNeil\xe2\x80\x99s reviews of his own at-bats.'
b'As Conforto said: \xe2\x80\x9cIf the pitcher gets him out with something, he comes back and he says, \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m going to get this guy! If he throws me that pitch again, I\xe2\x80\x99m going to get him!\xe2\x80\x99 Just constantly overflowing with confidence.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'It was his batting that allowed him to finally break through. Selected in the 12th round of the 2013 draft, McNeil languished in the minors until ripping through Class AA and AAA last season with a combined .342 average, 19 home runs, 36 walks and 71 R.B.I. He continued to do the same after the Mets called him up, stitching together an 11-game hitting streak during one stretch and batting .329 over all in 225 at-bats with three homers, six triples and 19 R.B.I. In a lost season for the Mets, McNeil, at the advanced rookie age of 26, was something of a revelation.'
b'The Mets appreciated McNeil\xe2\x80\x99s ability enough to keep him out of the winter trade that brought closer Edwin Diaz and second baseman Robinson Cano from Seattle to Queens. But the acquisitions of Cano in December and Lowrie in January also meant that McNeil needed to expand his versatility.'
b'In spring training, McNeil, who also bulked up to 195 pounds, was slated to take 80 percent of his assignments in left field, but then Lowrie went down with a knee injury the first week. Frazier, slated for third base, followed with an oblique strain. McNeil moved to third, picking grounders and talking positioning with Frazier, who has played every spot in the majors except pitcher, catcher and center field.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cHe is going to hit his way into the lineup,\xe2\x80\x9d Frazier said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have to get your legs under you at each position because it is a lot of different movements everywhere you go.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'McNeil will figure it out. His unconventional path to the Mets included playing baseball as a youth but then giving it up for golf when he got to high school. He eventually competed against Jordan Spieth (now a three-time winner of major golf tournaments) at the United States Junior Amateur Championship in July 2009. Spieth won the tournament, but McNeil still wonders where he might have ended up if he had putted better.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWho knows where I\xe2\x80\x99d be,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.'
b'Troy Buckley, the baseball coach at Long Beach State, was the ultimate beneficiary of McNeil\xe2\x80\x99s transition back to baseball. Unable to secure a golf scholarship, McNeil played baseball for the California Wahoos in a scout league after his junior year of high school and committed to California State University, Northridge, even before suiting up for Nipomo High as a senior. But the Northridge coaching staff was fired before McNeil ever made it to campus. In turn, Northridge recommended McNeil to Buckley, who watched him play for one weekend and offered him a scholarship. McNeil was now a Long Beach State Dirtbag.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cHe had a pencil-thin neck, those buck teeth, and his diet was composed of Mountain Dew and Cheez-Its,\xe2\x80\x9d Buckley said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\xe2\x80\x99d ask him to play third, and he would say yes before ever taking a grounder there.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'If Buckley had a hole, McNeil, who weighed just 160 pounds back then, filled it. He started his college career splitting time between second and left. Then he added center field, right field and shortstop. After he was drafted by the Mets, injuries slowed his progress. There was a double sports hernia and a torn hip labrum, followed by a torn quadriceps.'
b'The Mets finally took the measure of him last spring. He was a skinny minor leaguer taking repetitions as an infielder.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI was like, who is this \xe2\x80\x98Squirrel\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d Callaway said. \xe2\x80\x9cHe just raked. He\xe2\x80\x99s going to ambush you if you\xe2\x80\x99re not careful.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Once he broke through, McNeil settled in quickly in Queens. He stayed in a hotel last summer and fall but now lives in an apartment with his wife, Tatiana. His cleats were all organized at the bottom of his locker for the team\xe2\x80\x99s home opener in April, and his gloves were in place, as well.'
b'Still, he continues to hoard leather of all kinds. Though he has yet to play first base in the majors, he carries a first baseman\xe2\x80\x99s glove just in case. He is also the team\xe2\x80\x99s emergency catcher but does not carry a catcher\xe2\x80\x99s mitt.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIs he going to catch?\xe2\x80\x9d Frazier said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou never know.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'McNeil recently had two words sewn into a new outfield glove. It is a sign that he is finally embracing comparisons to the restless rodent. The words read \xe2\x80\x9cFlying Squirrel.\xe2\x80\x9d'

b'Supported by'
b'By Ken Belson'
b'Tom Seaver, the most dominant and prominent player in Mets history, is stepping back from public life because of advancing dementia, his family said Thursday.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cTom will continue to work in his beloved vineyard at his California home, but has chosen to completely retire from public life,\xe2\x80\x9d the Seavers said in the statement, which was released by the Baseball Hall of Fame. \xe2\x80\x9cThe family is deeply appreciative of those who have supported Tom throughout his career, on and off the field, and who do so now by honoring his request for privacy.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Seaver\xe2\x80\x99s family said he recently learned he has dementia. But for many years, Seaver, 74, has suffered from the effects of Lyme disease, which can cause cognitive problems similar to dementia.'
b'Seaver was known as The Franchise during his career with the Mets, which extended from 1967 to 1977 and included a one-year stint in 1983. He holds numerous team records and is the only former Mets player besides Mike Piazza to have his number retired. He retired from baseball in 1986.'
b'Seaver won 311 games and struck out 3,640 batters during his 20-year career, which included time with the Cincinnati Reds, Chicago White Sox and Boston Red Sox. He was selected for the All-Star team 12 times. Seaver was elected into the Hall of Fame in 1992 by near-unanimous decision.'
b'The announcement on Thursday came just months ahead of a celebration of the 50th anniversary of the Mets team that won the 1969 World Series. Seaver had appeared less frequently in public in recent years, and even before the announcement he had informed the Mets that he would not be at the ceremony at Citi Field at the end of June.'
b'The Mets released a statement from Jeff Wilpon, the chief operating officer, that said the team\xe2\x80\x99s thoughts were with Seaver and his family.'
b'Seaver will not be the only member of that team to miss the ceremony. Several memorable players from that team have died, including Tommie Agee, Donn Clendenon and Tug McGraw. A few others who are not in good health may also decline to attend.'
b'Still, Seaver was without a doubt the star of the 1969 team; he went 25-7 and won the first of his three Cy Young Awards. The Mets beat the heavily favored Baltimore Orioles, which shocked the baseball world because they had finished last or second to last every year since they joined the National League as an expansion franchise in 1962.'
b'The conversion from lovable losers to Miracle Mets was the culmination of a three-year climb that started when Seaver arrived in New York as a rookie in 1967. He won rookie of the year honors that season, and went 16-13 with a 2.76 E.R.A. The next year, Gil Hodges became the manager of the team, and Seaver blossomed under his wing.'
b'Seaver credited Hodges, who died in 1972, for helping him develop a better work ethic and become a smarter pitcher. But it was Seaver\xe2\x80\x99s confidence, enthusiasm and ease in front of the camera that made him a favorite with fans, who dubbed him Tom Terrific. For years, crowds at Shea Stadium were noticeably larger when Seaver pitched.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cTo have such a special baseball player, such a special pitcher, a legend of the game of baseball be a Met is a tremendous honor for our organization,\xe2\x80\x9d Mets Manager Mickey Callaway said Thursday in West Palm Beach, Fla. \xe2\x80\x9cOur thoughts and prayers go out to him and his family.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Seaver helped the Mets return to the World Series one more time, in 1973, as they surged from last place in late August to become division champions. The Mets went on to defeat the Reds in a wild, five-game playoff, and then pushed the Oakland Athletics to seven games in the World Series before losing.'
b'Seaver\xe2\x80\x99s departure on June 15, 1977, is considered one of the darkest days in Mets history. After a long dispute with management over his salary, Seaver was traded to the Reds for Pat Zachry, Doug Flynn, Steve Henderson and Dan Norman. Seaver energized an already-powerful Reds team, while the Mets finished in last place four out of the next six years. Almost a year after he was traded to the Reds, Seaver pitched his only no-hitter, against the St. Louis Cardinals in Cincinnati.'
b'Seaver returned in 1983 for one more year in New York, making him the last member of the 1969 team to play with the Mets. He was welcomed warmly on opening day that year and pitched six shutout innings. But he ended the season with an 9-14 record. He was picked up by the Chicago White Sox in the off-season and won his 300th game in Yankee Stadium in 1985.'
b'Seaver worked as a Mets broadcaster after he retired and was a regular fixture at the annual induction weekend at the Hall of Fame in Cooperstown, N.Y. Seaver also appeared at memorabilia shows in the New York area.'
b'Seaver\xe2\x80\x99s arrival in New York almost did not happen. He was drafted by the Atlanta Braves in 1966, but the pick was voided on a technicality. Seaver\xe2\x80\x99s name was put into a lottery, and the Mets won.'
b'After he left broadcasting, Seaver focused much of his energy on making wine at a vineyard on his 116-acre estate in Calistoga, Calif. At a wine tasting in New York several years ago, he talked about his new passion.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cYou end up with the job you have and you end up in the Hall of Fame, and when you get there, you realize it\xe2\x80\x99s the journey, not the destination,\xe2\x80\x9d Seaver said, by way of explaining what excited him about making wine.'
b'Seaver also appreciated how his success in baseball enabled him to pursue a second life working outdoors producing something he, his family and many others could enjoy.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cEvery night, when you close the gate and look back at the house,\xe2\x80\x9d Seaver said at the time, his voice cracking and his eyes welling up with tears, \xe2\x80\x9cand you realize, how cotton-picking lucky am I?\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Kevin Armstrong contributed reporting.'
b''
b'Advertisement'
b'Advertisement'
b'Supported by'
b'On Baseball'
b'By Tyler Kepner'
b'SURPRISE, Ariz. \xe2\x80\x94 After the Kansas City Royals departed for a spring training road game last month, their general manager, Dayton Moore, stayed behind at the team\xe2\x80\x99s training complex to watch prospects on the back fields. It was from there, more than a decade ago, that a longtime coach, Bill Fischer, had called him excitedly to share a vision.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cFish said, \xe2\x80\x98I just saw the Latin version of Johnny Bench,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Moore said, sitting in a dugout and smiling at the memory. \xe2\x80\x9cI said, \xe2\x80\x98Who\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x99 He said, \xe2\x80\x98Salvador Perez.\xe2\x80\x99 Fish recognized it right away.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Bench may have been the greatest catcher ever, but Perez is the only other catcher to make at least six All-Star teams while winning five Gold Gloves and a World Series Most Valuable Player Award.'
b'He is a rare modern species: a high-impact offensive catcher who plays a lot of games.'
b'As recently as 2007, 28 players appeared in at least 95 games as a catcher. Last season that number had dwindled to 15, the fewest since the majors expanded to 30 teams in 1998. Perez did it for the sixth season in a row, but his streak will end in 2019: At the moment Moore spoke, Perez was in Los Angeles to undergo Tommy John surgery, which will cost him the season.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a big blow to the morale of the organization, because Salvy\xe2\x80\x99s our energy-giver out there,\xe2\x80\x9d Moore said. \xe2\x80\x9cThe fans adore him, and they show up to watch him play regardless of where we are in the standings.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Without Perez \xe2\x80\x94 who hit 27 homers in each of the last two seasons \xe2\x80\x94 catchers\xe2\x80\x99 offensive production could slip again this season. In 2018, major league catchers produced a .233 batting average and a .678 on-base plus slugging percentage, both the lowest figures of the 30-team era.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a very physically demanding position, but that\xe2\x80\x99s been true for years,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matt Klentak, the general manager of the Philadelphia Phillies. \xe2\x80\x9cOn top of that, there has been a shift throughout the league in recent years in the way catcher defense is evaluated, prioritizing catcher framing. The impact of framing on a game is real, and in some cases teams are willing to trade out some offense in exchange for better receiving skills.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Catchers who can hit are still valued, as evidenced by the Phillies\xe2\x80\x99 trade with the Miami Marlins for J. T. Realmuto, the game\xe2\x80\x99s best offensive catcher. But the Boston Red Sox showed last season that a slugger behind the plate is not always necessary: They won the World Series despite a .194 average and .533 O.P.S. from their catchers, the worst marks for any champion in the live-ball era.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWe look at catcher as a defensive position,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dave Dombrowski, Boston\xe2\x80\x99s president of baseball operations. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd so the defensive efficiencies they bring to us, not only the things you can measure with framing, throwing guys out and time to second base, but handling the staff, following the game plan that we want \xe2\x80\x94 we put a high priority on that.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'And the job is only getting more challenging. Teams use more pitchers per season than ever, and while fastball velocity has risen in recent years, pitchers are also throwing a greater percentage of breaking balls. That is one factor explaining why wild pitches and passed balls are on the rise.'
b'More important, though, is the emphasis on framing \xe2\x80\x94 that is, getting borderline pitches called for strikes. When a catcher tries to present a pitch on the corner as a strike, he will try to keep most of his glove in the strike zone. Catching such pitches in the tip of the webbing, and not the pocket, naturally leads to more pitches that clip the edge of the mitt and roll away.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt may be the most demanding position in all of sports,\xe2\x80\x9d Moore said. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are more good N.F.L. quarterbacks than there are complete major league catchers with the standards we were raised with.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Those standards are changing, as reflected by the Red Sox, who were so satisfied with catchers Blake Swihart and Christian Vasquez that they brought them both back despite their weak bats. Boston catchers excelled on defense, ranking third in the majors at framing, according to Baseball Prospectus. The two teams above them were the National League champion Los Angeles Dodgers and, in first place, the Arizona Diamondbacks.'
b'It is no coincidence that one of Arizona\xe2\x80\x99s catchers last season, Jeff Mathis, attracted the attention of the team that ranked worst at framing, the Texas Rangers. Mathis hit .200 and has a .198 average across 14 seasons, but his exceptional defensive skills earned him a two-year, $6.25 million deal from the Rangers.'
b'That is a modest deal by industry standards, but it is the richest one Mathis, who turns 36 this month, has ever signed. He might have had little market appeal anymore if not for today\xe2\x80\x99s methods of measuring defense.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve always taken a lot of pride in the defensive side of my game,\xe2\x80\x9d Mathis said. \xe2\x80\x9cObviously I\xe2\x80\x99d love to contribute a lot more offensively, and I think I can. But for them to acknowledge defense the way they have the last few years is something that I\xe2\x80\x99ve definitely benefited from and definitely thankful for.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Jon Daniels, the Rangers\xe2\x80\x99 president of baseball operations, said the team determined at the start of the off-season to target a defensive-oriented catcher. The Rangers are entering their final season at outdoor Globe Life Park \xe2\x80\x94 a hitter\xe2\x80\x99s paradise \xe2\x80\x94 and plan to integrate young pitchers onto their roster as they move to an indoor ballpark in 2020.'
b'Besides Mathis, the Rangers considered Martin Maldonado, who lingered on the market and eventually signed with the Royals as Perez\xe2\x80\x99s replacement, for $2.5 million. Maldonado had a meager .616 O.P.S. for the Los Angeles Angels last season but still appealed to the Houston Astros, who traded for him in July and started him in six of their eight playoff games.'
b'The previous October, Brian McCann caught every inning for the Astros through their victory in Game 7 of the World Series. The 35-year-old McCann, who is second on the active list in games caught behind the St. Louis Cardinals\xe2\x80\x99 Yadier Molina, slumped to a .212 average last season and signed for one year and $2 million with his original team, the Atlanta Braves.'
b'Jeff Luhnow, the Astros\xe2\x80\x99 general manager and a former Cardinals executive, spoke wistfully recently about \xe2\x80\x9cthe days of having a Yadi Molina.\xe2\x80\x9d He could not bring himself to say that those days are over, but he does not see a successor.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure there will be some guys like that in the future, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t know why we\xe2\x80\x99re not developing catchers as much,\xe2\x80\x9d Luhnow said. \xe2\x80\x9cOf all the positions in baseball, that\xe2\x80\x99s probably the thinnest.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'This may simply be a down cycle; Baseball America ranked seven catchers among its top 100 prospects this season, the most since 2012. Then again, that crop from seven years ago \xe2\x80\x94 Travis d\xe2\x80\x99Arnaud, Yasmani Grandal, Devin Mesoraco, Jesus Montero, Wilin Rosario, Gary Sanchez and Swihart \xe2\x80\x94 has not yielded many stars.'
b'Neal Huntington, the Pittsburgh Pirates\xe2\x80\x99 general manager, said the rise of year-round amateur baseball could be burning out catching prospects; the relentless physical demands, he said, do not help developing hitters. If a catching prospect shows a promising bat, he added, talent evaluators may want him to change positions. (Bryce Harper, the Phillies\xe2\x80\x99 new $330 million outfielder, was a catcher as an amateur.)'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe guy who may be a midrange defender but can swing the bat a little bit, we may be thinking about shifting him out from behind home plate because we want the bat to play \xe2\x80\x94 and we\xe2\x80\x99ve become so strict on receiving analytics that we\xe2\x80\x99re almost unforgiving in some cases,\xe2\x80\x9d Huntington said.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cSo we\xe2\x80\x99ve shifted the pendulum way to the defensive side because we recognize the value it brings. There\xe2\x80\x99s also value in that guy who can receive and hit, but that guy who\xe2\x80\x99s an exceptional receiver, he gets the benefit of the doubt every single time, and we\xe2\x80\x99ve been able to quantify that. So the guys that used to kind of fade away are now hanging around longer.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Mathis was not the only veteran catcher to receive a multiyear deal in free agency. Kurt Suzuki, 35, got a two-year, $10 million contract in November from the Washington Nationals, who did not stop there. Ten days later, they traded for Yan Gomes, an All-Star catcher for Cleveland last season.'
b'Suzuki \xe2\x80\x94 who caught 141 games for Oakland in 2008, his first full season in the majors \xe2\x80\x94 said he was happy to share the role. The same analytics wave that identifies and values catchers\xe2\x80\x99 defense also makes the job more demanding because of the volume of information to process before games.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt used to be a DVD,\xe2\x80\x9d Suzuki said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d watch video and go, \xe2\x80\x98This is how we\xe2\x80\x99re going to get him out.\xe2\x80\x99 Now you\xe2\x80\x99ve got all these heat maps, velocities, and you\xe2\x80\x99re like, \xe2\x80\x98Wow, this is a brain cram.\xe2\x80\x99 It\xe2\x80\x99s a lot of studying, a lot of stress and strain on the brain. To have that mental rest is awesome.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'The Nationals\xe2\x80\x99 time-sharing plan is becoming more of the rule than the exception. The productive hitter who catches nearly every game is harder and harder to find \xe2\x80\x94 even, at least for this season, in Kansas City.'

b'By Michael Powell'
b'A few weeks back I drove across central Florida, that flatland of farms and cattle and sweet-smelling orange groves, in the company of a childhood friend, Peter Kurz, and we talked, as we have been prone to do over many years, about our lifelong obsession with the New York Mets.'
b'We recalled our preadolescent battery at the Soldiers and Sailors Monument on Manhattan\xe2\x80\x99s Upper West Side, my imaginary Tom Seaver tossing fastballs to his imaginary Jerry Grote. We got everyone out of course, Willie Stargell and Roberto Clemente, Frank and Brooks Robinson, too, because that was just the way Tom Terrific rolled.'
b'The 1969 Mets inhabited our consciousness, as happens when the hypnotic beauty of baseball sinks its hook in kids. Those Mets, those world champion guys, have been stepping into mortality\xe2\x80\x99s shadow for years now, Tommie Agee and Donn Clendenon, Ed Charles and Tug McGraw, all gone, so we can not lay claim to shock that another star will now be moving off the stage.'
b'Except that Seaver was the immortal, the forever young, so composed and so fiercely competitive and so analytical about his efforts. Now his family says that at age 74 he suffers from dementia and we will see no more of him in public life. He will tend to his vineyards in Napa as long as he can.'
b'[Read About the Seaver family\xe2\x80\x99s announcement that the baseball legend has dementia and will retire from public life.] '
b'That is not how I will remember him and maybe that\xe2\x80\x99s the point of his withdrawal. He was a powerful, stocky pitcher from California and he dominated his mound like a tenor astride his stage. He would pull off his cap and run his hand through that thick shock of hair and then ready himself and rear and toss and rear and toss. No need to put a 20-second clock on him; out there on his mound he was pure business and if a batter fiddled around too much he just might sneak a hard fastball under the batter\xe2\x80\x99s chin by way of a reminder to buckle down.'
b'He perfected the drop-and-thrust delivery, dropping so far down as he threw that his measure of a good night was whether his right knee had dirt on it from dragging on the mound. No weight work and iron sleds for him; he ran and he worked out with light weights. And still that fastball crackled.'
b'Magic could happen any night he pitched and so you tried to wheedle your parents into letting you stay up and watch with them on the black-and-white WOR Channel 9 broadcast, or you\xe2\x80\x99d curl up in bed with a transistor radio and listen to Bob Murphy paint the picture for you, of Seaver tossing and tossing.'
b'Data can be a sportswriting crutch although it\xe2\x80\x99s also true that baseball without statistics is like the Bible without words. So in 1969 Seaver went 25-7 with a 2.21 earned run average. He also threw five complete-game shutouts.'
b'I recently took a look at the prevailing, and too little questioned, orthodoxy that starting pitchers should not go more than six or seven innings into a game. By way of making a point, Seaver became my insane outlier. If hitters wanted to get to him, they were well-advised to swing early before Seaver tuned up his curve and that live snake of a fastball. His career E.R.A. in the first inning of a game was 3.75. In the last three innings, his lifetime E.R.A. was 2.75.'
b'And forgive me my statistical drunkenness but peak-performance Tom tilted into absurdity. In 1969, he pitched in the ninth inning 17 times and surrendered not a single run.'
b'Oh yes, in September of that year, he went 6-0 with an 0.83 E.R.A. All this courtesy of Baseball-Reference.com.'
b'Seaver like his peers Bob Gibson and Juan Marichal and Jim Palmer grooved on competition. That was never more true than in 1973, when the Mets became the most unlikely team in baseball history to reach the World Series, stumbling into the postseason with a motley record of 82-79. Many Mets had been injured that season, they fell into one ditch after another and yet somehow they dusted themselves off and made it to the Series against the vastly more powerful Oakland As.'
b'That series went a taut seven games and Seaver facing off against Reggie Jackson was alone worth the price of admission. Reggie had led the American League in home runs and runs batted in that year, and Seaver was Seaver, 19-10 with a 2.08 E.R.A. and 250 strikeouts. Reggie strutted to the plate, wiggle-wagging his bat, ready for the struggle. Seaver looked in to Grote for the signs but there really was not much doubt what the call would be.'
b'My mind\xe2\x80\x99s eye recalls Seaver throwing more or less nothing but fastballs, mano a mano. He got Jackson and Jackson got him. Seaver was perhaps not at his best in that Series as he actually lost a game and had a 2.40 E.R.A. He also struck out 18 men in 15 innings.'
b'This fairy tale ended badly. Seaver wanted more money and the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 board chairman, M. Donald Grant, who was imperious to the point of self-satire, bridled at the help acting up. Goaded by Dick Young, a reactionary columnist for The Daily News, Grant sent Seaver into exile in Cincinnati. If that city by the Ohio River was to be his Elba, he made do well enough, going 14-3 in his first year there.'
b'None of it felt right though. He was the essential Met. He returned in the midst of the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 Dark Ages, 1983, and the team lost 94 games. He was an old man in baseball terms, the vigor had left his fastball, but he still fashioned a reasonable season, with a 3.55 E.R.A. He gave up up just 201 hits in 231 innings.'
b'He moved on, an itinerant starter, and won 16 games at age 40 for the White Sox. A year later he called it quits. He had a Hall of Fame career, one of the best in the history of the game, and his cumulative career earnings in 20 years totaled a hair under $6 million, or roughly what an average relief pitcher makes in today\xe2\x80\x99s game.'
b'Me, I remember trying to mimic that drop-and-thrust motion of his. And I recall riding the No. 7 train to Shea Stadium with my friend Peter. We bought $1.30 tickets and took our seats for a doubleheader: Jerry Koosman in the opener, Tom Terrific in the nightcap.'
b'If I could, I\xe2\x80\x99d give him a standing O still.'

b'Pitchers have used all sorts of tricks to adulterate balls and make their jobs easier. These days, though, it\xe2\x80\x99s harder to create the necessary mess.'
b'Gaylord Perry pitching for the Indians at Yankee Stadium in 1973. Perry is one of several Hall of Fame pitchers with a reputation for doctoring baseballs.CreditCreditAssociated Press'
b'Supported by'
b'By Tyler Kepner'
b'The Brooklyn Dodgers met an old friend on a trip to St. Louis early in the 1955 season. Preacher Roe, a sly and slender lefty who had just retired to West Plains, Mo., entertained his former teammates at the Chase Hotel. He needed their advice.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWe sat around a big table, all talking to Preacher,\xe2\x80\x9d recalls Carl Erskine, a top Dodgers starter then. \xe2\x80\x9cHe said, \xe2\x80\x98Fellas, I want to ask you a question: Sports Illustrated has offered me $2,000 to tell them how I threw the spitter. You think I should do that or not? With that $2,000 I can blacktop my driveway and I can fix up my house. I could really use that $2,000. What do you think?\xe2\x80\x99'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe guys all said, \xe2\x80\x98Yeah, go ahead, Preach, sure, why not?\xe2\x80\x99 I did not. I admired Preacher. He was a great study to watch pitch. He was very clever and won a lot of games; he was really a pitcher\xe2\x80\x99s pitcher. So I didn\xe2\x80\x99t say yes to that. As we were leaving the dining room, Preacher said, \xe2\x80\x98I didn\xe2\x80\x99t hear you speak up.\xe2\x80\x99 I said, \xe2\x80\x98No, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t, Preacher. You were such an outstanding pitcher without the spitter, I\xe2\x80\x99d hate to see you taint your whole career by talking about throwing it.\xe2\x80\x99'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cSo anyway, Preacher did the article, he got the $2,000, and sometime later when I talked to Preach he said, \xe2\x80\x98Carl, you know what? I just ruined my chances for the Hall of Fame by admitting I threw the spitter, and you\xe2\x80\x99re the only one that advised me not to do it.\xe2\x80\x99 And I said, \xe2\x80\x98Well, Preacher, I saw you pitch, you were a pitcher\xe2\x80\x99s pitcher. You could have won without the spitter.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Maybe, maybe not. While Erskine believed in Roe, the man himself didn\xe2\x80\x99t: In his confession, Roe said he turned to the pitch after slumping to 4\xe2\x80\x9315 with the Pirates in 1947. Reviving the spitter, which he had practiced with Harry Brecheen as a Cardinals farmhand, was his last chance. He made the most of it, becoming a regular All-Star, though not quite a Cooperstown candidate, for the Dodgers. Cheating only made sense.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWhy shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I have?\xe2\x80\x9d Roe told Dick Young in the Sports Illustrated article. \xe2\x80\x9cI was about through when I decided to get me the pitch. \xe2\x80\x98If I get caught,\xe2\x80\x99 I told myself, \xe2\x80\x98they\xe2\x80\x99ll kick me out. If I don\xe2\x80\x99t, I\xe2\x80\x99m through anyway, so how can I lose?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'He didn\xe2\x80\x99t lose very often. Roe won so much \xe2\x80\x94 he had a .715 winning percentage over seven seasons with Brooklyn \xe2\x80\x94 that he guessed the spitter had earned him $100,000. On the bench between innings, Roe would pop a stick of Beech-Nut gum into his mouth and announce, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to get me a new batch of curveballs.\xe2\x80\x9d In the game, he\xe2\x80\x99d spit on the meaty part of his thumb while pretending to wipe his brow. Then, while hitching his belt, he\xe2\x80\x99d subtly wipe his index and middle fingers on the saliva. Two wet fingers on top, a dry thumb underneath, and Roe was ready. He compared it to squeezing a peach pit or a watermelon seed with your fingertips.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe idea is to get part of your grip wet, and the other dry,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen the ball leaves your hand, it slips off your wet fingers and clings, just tiny-like, to the dry part of your thumb. The ball jumps on account of it. If it\xe2\x80\x99s a good \xe2\x80\x99un, it drops like a dead duck just when it crosses the plate.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Roe\xe2\x80\x99s confession only confirmed his reputation. As Stan Musial wrote decades later, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d always be first-pitch hitting against Roe, because when he had two strikes on you, he\xe2\x80\x99d usually load up, and I hated to get a shower.\xe2\x80\x9d Anticipation of the spitter helped Roe, and he knew how to destroy evidence. He was never caught in the act.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe umpire would call for the ball, and Preacher would stomp around and look at his hand like, \xe2\x80\x98Uh-oh, you probably got me this time,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Erskine says, \xe2\x80\x9cand then instead of tossing the ball in, he\xe2\x80\x99d roll it to the umpire on the ground!\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Like steroid users decades later, Roe stayed ahead of the cops and thrived. But his pang of regret to Erskine \xe2\x80\x94 at least for going public and bursting his cloud of mystery \xe2\x80\x94 showed that he knew what he had done. The specter of the dark arts would indeed follow Roe to his grave; when he died in 2008, the word \xe2\x80\x9cspitball\xe2\x80\x9d appeared in the headline of his New York Times obituary.'
b'After two chaotic decades or so, the spitball was banned for 1920, the same year the country went dry under Prohibition. The rule simply turned the mound into a speakeasy, with many pitchers going undercover to get the same slippery edge as their predecessors.'
b'The physics behind the spitball is simple enough: When the ball slides off wet fingers, it loses backspin and therefore rotates less \xe2\x80\x94 something like a knuckleball, which should not rotate at all, or a forkball tumbling in its final plunge.'
b'Defacing the surface of a ball produces the same kind of effect \xe2\x80\x94 added movement, to the opposite side of the scuff. This also dates to the game\xe2\x80\x99s early days. Imagine what the baseballs looked like in the early 20th century:'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99d play a whole game with one ball, if it stayed in the park,\xe2\x80\x9d Wahoo Sam Crawford, a Hall of Famer who played from 1899 to 1917, says in \xe2\x80\x9cThe Glory of Their Times,\xe2\x80\x9d a book by Lawrence S. Ritter.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cLopsided, and black, and full of tobacco and licorice stains,\xe2\x80\x9d Crawford continues. \xe2\x80\x9cThe pitchers used to have it all their way back then.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Even the possibility of scuffing gives pitchers an edge. These words come from Chet Brewer, a star for the Kansas City Monarchs of the Negro National League in the late 1920s, and they sound timeless:'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cA cut ball? I got credit for that,\xe2\x80\x9d he told John Holway in the book \xe2\x80\x9cBlack Diamonds.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cIf I picked up a rough one, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t throw it out of the game. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t exactly put the cuts on it myself, but I could pitch it.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Scuffing charges never dogged Mike Mussina, a Hall of Fame right-hander for the Orioles and the Yankees from 1991 to 2008. But like any smart pitcher, he would never reject a ball that came to him with a mark. This was Mussina\xe2\x80\x99s order for every catcher: If a ball scrapes the ground, never volunteer it to the umpire.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cSay a guy hits a ground ball to short and they throw it back to me from first base \xe2\x80\x94 as soon as I grab the ball, I\xe2\x80\x99m running my thumb over these,\xe2\x80\x9d Mussina says. He is holding a ball and referring to the white area within the horseshoe. \xe2\x80\x9cDo I have a sinker out of this ball? And if I find one I think\xe2\x80\x99s pretty good, I flip it to the other side to see if it\xe2\x80\x99s scuffed up on the wrong side.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'By that he means the opposite side; if both horseshoe areas are scuffed, they\xe2\x80\x99ll counteract each other, to no advantage. If there\xe2\x80\x99s a scuff where the seams narrow \xe2\x80\x94 the sweet spot \xe2\x80\x94 it creates a four-seam sinker, a pitch that spins like a straight fastball but veers away, in the direction opposite the scuff.'
b'A common lament among retired pitchers is that too many balls are now thrown out of play. To them, it underscores a lack of craftsmanship on the mound.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve literally seen teammates, when there\xe2\x80\x99s a scuff on the ball they get rid of it,\xe2\x80\x9d says Jamie Moyer, who won 269 games in a career that ended in 2012, the year he turned 50. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ll say, \xe2\x80\x98Oh, it does funny stuff,\xe2\x80\x99 and you go, \xe2\x80\x98Wait a minute, that funny stuff can be a benefit to you!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Hitters are conditioned now to ask umpires to discard any ball that skips. Umpires and catchers know this, so catchers tend to have an almost reflexive response: They will spear a ball from the dirt, transfer it quickly to their throwing hand, and hold it up for the ump to keep or reject.'
b'Of course, some fielders still try to create a scuff: Catchers may deliberately short-hop their throws before innings, and outfielders may do the same on routine throws to the infield. But times have changed.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I played, a ball hit the dirt, it was still in play,\xe2\x80\x9d says Chili Davis, the Mets hitting coach, who played from 1981 to 1999. \xe2\x80\x9cToday\xe2\x80\x99s game, if a catcher throws a ball down to second on a short hop, or a ball gets blocked in the dirt, that ball\xe2\x80\x99s out of the game. It\xe2\x80\x99s just automatic now.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Pitchers also seek ways to look natural while applying moisture or tackiness to their fingers. One former player, now a broadcaster, reached into his team\xe2\x80\x99s ball bag for me and pulled out a canister of colorless Tuf-Skin, a spray that helps secure athletic tape. With one spritz of Tuf-Skin on my arm, I had an invisible island of instant tackiness for my fingers.'
b'That\xe2\x80\x99s also why pitchers like putting clear BullFrog sunscreen on their arms. Grab the rosin bag, aimlessly touch the BullFrog spot, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll have just enough stickiness to help guide your pitches. One catcher says he has seen pitchers leave the dugout between innings and wrap their fingers around a ball coated with pine tar, leaving just enough residue on their hands to get a better grip when they return to the mound.'
b'Next time you go to a game, notice all the surfaces a pitcher touches with his hand. Pitchers are fidgety creatures, constantly tugging and swiping and scratching their caps, their sleeves, their skin, something. Corey Kluber, the two-time Cy Young Award winner for Cleveland, grabs his tongue on the mound before every pitch \xe2\x80\x94 which became legal again years ago \xe2\x80\x94 then wipes his hand on the side of his pants.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe way they rub the balls up now, they rub them up all in advance,\xe2\x80\x9d Kluber says. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re not rubbed up all day, and they sit in the bucket with all the dust and stuff, and they get so slippery. Just get a little bit of moisture on your hand, at least. You\xe2\x80\x99ve still got to wipe it off, obviously, but you get a little bit of moisture on your hand so the ball\xe2\x80\x99s not as dry.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Kluber does this no matter the weather, but such tactics are especially handy in the cold and at high altitude, where it\xe2\x80\x99s harder to generate moisture.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI tell you what,\xe2\x80\x9d says one 10-year veteran, \xe2\x80\x9cif they could get a camera behind the dugout in Colorado, it would be like a mad scientist\xe2\x80\x99s laboratory down there, people doing anything they can to find a grip.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Most players, even hitters, tend to accept this practice. A pitch can be an instrument of destruction, after all, and a hitter would rather the pitcher know where it\xe2\x80\x99s going than accidentally fire a cue ball at his head. It is a fine distinction, to be sure, but this is the logic: Tackiness helps command and finish on a pitch, and that\xe2\x80\x99s O.K. Sandpaper or K-Y Jelly helps enhance movement, and that\xe2\x80\x99s not.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIn the cold weather in Minnesota, you had to have something to grip the curveball,\xe2\x80\x9d says Jim Kaat, who starred for the Twins in the 1960s. \xe2\x80\x9cPitchers for years have lobbied: If hitters can use pine tar to grip the bat better \xe2\x80\x94 it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t help \xe2\x80\x99em hit it any farther \xe2\x80\x94 we should be able to use pine tar.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Then again, it comes back to discretion: In 2014, when the Yankees\xe2\x80\x99 Michael Pineda brazenly smeared pine tar on his neck on a chilly night at Fenway Park, it was so overt that the Red Sox felt compelled to object. Pineda got a 10-game suspension and widespread ridicule. But generally, as long as a pitcher is discreet, the other side has no problem with pine tar.'
b'Pine tar is one thing \xe2\x80\x94 cheating by the letter of the law, yes, but not by the spirit. Whatever happened to the spitball, the Hall of Fame pitch of Jack Chesbro and Ed Walsh, Stan Coveleski and Red Faber, Burleigh Grimes and Gaylord Perry? Richie Ashburn, who had the most hits of the 1950s, always thought of pitchers as shifty characters, never to be trusted.'
b'Where have all the scoundrels gone?'
b'When I asked Perry in 2018 why more pitchers were not as, shall we say, crafty as they were in his day, he had a short but telling answer: \xe2\x80\x9cWell, maybe they don\xe2\x80\x99t need it.\xe2\x80\x9d Dan Plesac, who pitched in more than 1,000 games, agreed.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re not looking for a Don Sutton now, a guy that can sink it and cut it and make the ball move,\xe2\x80\x9d Plesac says. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you don\xe2\x80\x99t have velocity, you can\xe2\x80\x99t pitch anymore. How many guys, how many real power pitchers, need to scuff the ball to be successful? Not many. You don\xe2\x80\x99t need to, if your stuff is that good.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Plesac works for MLB Network, founded in 2009, the hub of baseball\xe2\x80\x99s vast and ever-expanding visual empire. Every fan can watch games on the At Bat app. Every team has a bank of video screens, usually just off the dugout, showing every conceivable angle for replays and analysis. Good luck evading the most sophisticated alarm system in baseball history.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWhen there\xe2\x80\x99s so many TV cameras, it\xe2\x80\x99s so hard to get away with it,\xe2\x80\x9d says Jason Giambi, who played 20 seasons in the majors. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have so many sets of eyes on these guys, and especially if they see a pitch that looks really abnormal, the (team video analyst) rewinds it 5,000 times: \xe2\x80\x98O.K., what did he do different? Oh, he went to the side of his pants, he went to his belt, he went to the top of his hat.\xe2\x80\x99 Then they start to put together the timeline of every time he\xe2\x80\x99s pitched, does that ball do the same thing? What about his last start, his start before that? And before you know it, they\xe2\x80\x99ve got it down \xe2\x80\x94 all right, go tell the umpire to check his hat, or check the side of his pants, or check inside his glove. You can\xe2\x80\x99t hide anymore.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'But what if you could? What if the next Preacher Roe or Gaylord Perry was hiding in plain sight on a diamond near you, fiendishly fooling all the viewers and video technicians at the ballpark and beyond?'
b'He could blacktop a lot of driveways by selling those secrets \xe2\x80\x94 and he\xe2\x80\x99d probably keep the supplies after finishing the job. The sticky sealant just might come in handy.'
b'The article is an excerpt from \xe2\x80\x9cK: A History of Baseball In Ten Pitches,\xe2\x80\x9d by Tyler Kepner, a New York Times sportswriter. The book will be released on April 2.'

b'By Jay Schreiber'
b'Ask Rod Gaspar what moment he remembers most from the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 1969 season, and the backup outfielder with the powerful arm points to a head-spinning game in San Francisco at the end of August. And why not? It was a game in which he and Donn Clendenon combined to throw out three Giants runners at third base and home plate in the eighth and ninth innings before the Mets won it in the 10th.'
b'Or, as Gaspar put it: \xe2\x80\x9cTypical Mets.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Ask Ron Swoboda the same question and he graciously overlooks his famous catch in the World Series and instead cites a September game against the Chicago Cubs, with first place on the line.'
b'In the bottom of the first inning, the Cubs knocked down the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 invaluable leadoff hitter, Tommie Agee. Swoboda takes it from there:'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIn the top of the second, Jerry Koosman throws a rocket right at Ron Santo\xe2\x80\x99s coconut and he put an arm up. You want to play this game? I don\xe2\x80\x99t think so. b'\xe2\x80\x9cAfter they knocked down Agee, I remember Tom Seaver yelling in the dugout, \xe2\x80\x98You don\xe2\x80\x99t want to do that.\xe2\x80\x99 Koosman took care of the rest.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Ask Koosman who or what stands out most 50 years later and he is quick to cite Gil Hodges, the manager behind the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 miracle.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cGil was the man \xe2\x80\x94 there was a lot of quiet management that went on,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cGil talked a little bit in code, but you knew what he meant. You knew where he stood and what he expected of you. And it worked great. We won, didn\xe2\x80\x99t we? At 100-to-1 odds in Vegas before the season, and we end up winning.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Ed Kranepool, who played 18 seasons for the Mets, remembers what it was like to be a platoon player in the World Series. In the five games it took for the Mets to upend the favored Baltimore Orioles, Kranepool and three other left-handed hitters \xe2\x80\x94 Art Shamsky, Ken Boswell and Wayne Garrett \xe2\x80\x94 were in the starting lineup just once, for Game 3.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cDid the left-handers want to play in the World Series?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYeah, but you had to respect Gil and what he was doing. We did get to face Jim Palmer in Game 3 and we knocked him out. The next day, we sat down again. So we sat there, but we were winning.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Jim McAndrew, who was in the back end of the 1969 starting rotation, was on the postseason roster and didn\xe2\x80\x99t get to play a single moment. But he almost did.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI was scared to death because Seaver gave up that leadoff homer in the first inning of the World Series, and Hodges immediately had me up in the bullpen,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. Still, he didn\xe2\x80\x99t get into that game or any other in the Series.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cBut you can\xe2\x80\x99t gripe,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.'
b'McAndrew, Gaspar, Swoboda, Koosman and Kranepool spoke to The New York Times early this month in connection with the 50th anniversary of one of the most remarkable moments in baseball history \xe2\x80\x94 the sudden ascent of the Mets to a championship after the team\xe2\x80\x99s previous life as a laughingstock.'
b'The five spoke with pride of what they accomplished, but with some sadness, too. Fifty years is a long time, and a half-dozen players from that team have died. Others have serious medical issues.'
b'It is a somber scorecard that also includes Hodges, who died of a heart attack before the 1972 season, and the death of three of his four coaches. And Seaver, 74, the best player on that 1969 team, has advancing dementia.'
b'The disclosure of Seaver\xe2\x80\x99s condition was made shortly after the former Mets had spoken with The Times. But they were all aware that Seaver had been struggling with the effects of Lyme disease for years. More than anything, they were aware of how relentless time is.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cCasey was right when he said you get old very quick,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kranepool, referring to Casey Stengel, who was the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 manager when Kranepool joined the team as a teenager. Now Kranepool is 74 and in need of a kidney transplant, although he remains intent on being part of the 1969 reunion the Mets will stage at Citi Field in June.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s frustrating,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou would like to see everybody, but it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t work that way. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I\xe2\x80\x99m going to be around for the 100th, so I\xe2\x80\x99m glad we\xe2\x80\x99re doing this.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Swoboda, who is also 74, said: \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know where the 50 years went, but we are a band of brothers. Very few of us were involved in anything else of that magnitude.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s no fun seeing our guys go down,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gaspar, who is 72. \xe2\x80\x9cI hope we can have 15 of us at the reunion.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Koosman said he planned to be there. He is 76, the oldest of the five, but still doing back-and-forth bantering with Swoboda. Except now it\xe2\x80\x99s via email instead of in the clubhouse. And what do they jab each other about?'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cPolitics,\xe2\x80\x9d said Koosman. Swoboda, he said, is too liberal. \xe2\x80\x9cTell him to get a life,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, joking. \xe2\x80\x9cTell him to quit watching MSNBC and reading The New York Times. Tell him to read the sports section and stop there.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Told what Koosman had said about him, Swoboda laughed. \xe2\x80\x98I love the guy,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut he sends me all this stuff from the blogosphere.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x99'
b'McAndrew, amused by all of this, was willing to volunteer that he views himself as \xe2\x80\x9csocially compassionate, fiscally responsible.\xe2\x80\x9d Swoboda, he added, \xe2\x80\x9cis a non-backing-off lefty.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'A lefty who, in 1969, played right field, and not always that well. Hodges would take him out of games for defensive purposes and insert Gaspar, \xe2\x80\x9cwhich drove me crazy,\xe2\x80\x9d Swoboda said.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI wanted to be good enough for Hodges not to have to do that,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x99 he added. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd eventually, I was. Eventually, he left me alone.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Right through the top of the ninth inning of Game 4 of the Series, when Swoboda\xe2\x80\x99s do-or-die catch of Brooks Robinson\xe2\x80\x99s line drive kept the Orioles from taking control of the game.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cClendenon,\xe2\x80\x9d Swoboda noted sardonically, \xe2\x80\x9ccalled it the stupidest catch he ever saw because I went for it.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Clendenon. Alert, tough, college-educated. A veteran voice in the clubhouse. And a central figure in that wild August game in San Francisco that Gaspar so vividly remembers. Here\xe2\x80\x99s how he tells it:'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the bottom of the ninth, tie score, one out, Bob Burda is on first, Willie McCovey is up and we have the McCovey shift on. Everyone is pulled over to the right. I\xe2\x80\x99m playing left, but I\xe2\x80\x99m almost in center. Tug McGraw is pitching and McCovey sticks out his bat and hits a high, nine-iron shot down the left-field line. I chase after the ball, pick it up and throw blindly toward home plate.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI knew it was the game right there. I figured Burda was going to score easy. But you know what? I threw a rocket to Jerry Grote, all the way in the air, and Burda was out by 15 feet. And Grote was so stunned he rolled the ball back to the mound even though there only two outs. And McCovey, who had reached second, sees this and takes off for third. And Clendenon, who was always paying attention, picks the ball up and throws to third. Double play.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe next inning, Clendenon hits a home run and we win the game.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'McAndrew said: \xe2\x80\x9cClendenon could strap you on his back and get those big hits to drive in runs for a week or 10 days. All the low-scoring games, we won them.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'And all the crazy games \xe2\x80\x94 the Mets won them, too. Some mystical combination of talent, toughness, luck and magic conquered everything in its path. For one season.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe next year,\xe2\x80\x9d Swoboda said, \xe2\x80\x9cwe tried to make it happen, rather than let it happen. Something was missing. All it does it add more mystery to 1969 in my book.\xe2\x80\x9d'

b'By George Vecsey'
b'With the Vietnam War dragging on, 1969 was not a good year for discussing the character-building value of the military.'
b'From a distance of half a century, however, it seems rather clear \xe2\x80\x94 at least to me \xe2\x80\x94 that the loopy young Mets franchise had its spine stiffened by two men trained by the United States Marines.'
b'Two of the steeliest people in the Mets clubhouse were Gil Hodges, the manager, and Tom Seaver, the pitcher, later known as The Franchise. Both could turn downright taut \xe2\x80\x94 eyes narrowing, jaws tightening, speech clipped \xe2\x80\x94 when reporters or broadcasters or fans or even Mets ballplayers acted as if these were the Same Old Mets.'
b'We reporters were the worst. I know I was conditioned to believe that the Mets were fun, but terminally hapless.'
b'Wipe that smile off your face, Mister. This is no laughing matter.'
b'Hodges had seen combat on Okinawa, while Seaver was a Marine reservist. Semper Fi. They staked out territory and they did not give it up.'
b'Hodges had been part of the Mets in the first goofy days, in spring training 1962, when Casey Stengel was filibustering to catch the public\xe2\x80\x99s attention. Hodges was happy to be back from Los Angeles, in his wife\xe2\x80\x99s home borough of Brooklyn, where he was beloved for his grand decade-plus with the Dodgers. His knee was shot, and Marvin Eugene Throneberry (check out the initials) became the regular first baseman and an immortal Met blunderer.'
b'The Mets were not adequate enough to benefit from Hodges\xe2\x80\x99s Boys of Summer way of doing things. He became the manager of the Washington Senators for what some felt was an apprenticeship for the Mets\xe2\x80\x99 job, which he was offered in 1968.'
b'Seaver arrived from the farm system in 1967, after growing up fast in the Marines and later learning the game under Rod Dedeaux at the University of Southern California. In some ways, Seaver was a normal young man in his early 20s \xe2\x80\x94 full of energy, a whooping laugh, pranks on teammates. He could also do The New York Times crossword puzzle and converse with writers. When he was playing, he did not bring up his time in the Marine Corps Reserve in 1962-63, but later he talked proudly about it.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI was a mediocre student,\xe2\x80\x9d Seaver recalled on Veterans Day, Nov. 11, 2011, at a ceremony in Manhattan honoring military veterans. \xe2\x80\x9cIf it didn\xe2\x80\x99t have a ball involved, I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t much interested.\xe2\x80\x9d But the Marines sharpened his focus.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cThe principles that I learned in boot camp were the principles that I took to the mound,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cFocus, dedication. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have made it without the Marine Corps.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Seaver mentioned a picture taken of him on the mound, locked into attention, during the playing of the national anthem. \xe2\x80\x9cYou look at my feet, they\xe2\x80\x99re in military position, absolutely,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the discipline that was instilled in me. It\xe2\x80\x99s a very important reason why I\xe2\x80\x99m in Cooperstown.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'It is not clear how much Seaver and Hodges discussed their Marine roots with each other. They had served under vastly different circumstances \xe2\x80\x94 Seaver stateside, Hodges facing incoming kamikaze pilots in the Pacific Theater.'
b'Hodges was a college boy from southern Indiana, noticeable because of his muscles. Stengel, on one of his franchise-building conversational rambles, once said that Hodges was so strong \xe2\x80\x9che could squeeze your earbrows off.\xe2\x80\x9d Nobody knew what an earbrow was, and nobody wanted to ask.'
b'I had a mentor in the newspaper business who had been a young Columbia University dropout and baseball freak, sent to the island of Okinawa. There were rumors that a large young Marine on the island was a major league ballplayer. (The Dodgers had let Hodges play one game at third base in 1943 before he shipped out.)'
b'On Okinawa, my friend heard rumors that Hodges had been in hand-to-hand combat, but that rumor has been deflated by Mort Zachter, the author of \xe2\x80\x9cGil Hodges: A Hall of Fame Life,\xe2\x80\x9d published in 2015. Hodges was awarded a Bronze Star, with the citation mentioning his duties supporting his commanding officer, in a dangerous zone, but there is no mention of his being in direct combat. He surely saw death all around him.'
b'When Hodges became a star with the Dodgers, he often went to the movies in his free time. A friend who accompanied him said Hodges would grow solemn if there was a scene of combat, and he would pray, \xe2\x80\x9cAmen,\xe2\x80\x9d at every fallen military person.'
b'Hodges carried his Marine steel in the manager\xe2\x80\x99s uniform. When reporters made jokes he smiled weakly and politely, but he did not encourage levity or cynicism.'
b'Few players challenged Hodges, except for the earnest and curious young outfielder Ron Swoboda, otherwise known as Rocky, who had grown used to the garrulous orations of Stengel.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI would sit and listen to him,\xe2\x80\x9d Swoboda said of the Old Man in 2009. \xe2\x80\x9cIt would sound as if he was speaking in Stengelese, but all of a sudden I\xe2\x80\x99d say, \xe2\x80\x98Hey, man, this guy is telling a parable \xe2\x80\x94 and it\xe2\x80\x99s aimed at me!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'By 1969, Casey was three full seasons into retirement and Gil Hodges was the manager. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen you\xe2\x80\x99re young and full of yourself, you do things you regret,\xe2\x80\x9d Swoboda said. \xe2\x80\x9cGil sometimes made me feel he was rubbing our noses in his authority, but that was me, that was my fault.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cI remember one time early in 1969 he held a meeting and he said, \xe2\x80\x98You guys are professionals, you set your own curfew,\xe2\x80\x99 and somebody suggested two and a half hours after the game, and Gil said it was too long, and I popped up, \xe2\x80\x98Gil, why don\xe2\x80\x99t you just tell us?\xe2\x80\x99 I would do my thing like that. Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t I just shut up?\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'One player who was definitely in tune with Hodges was the third-year pitcher Tom Seaver. On May 21, 1969, Seaver beat the Braves in Atlanta to bring the Mets to an 18-18 record \xe2\x80\x94 the latest in their first eight seasons that they had been even.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cTom, Tom,\xe2\x80\x9d babbled one of the regular reporters (not me, but it easily could have been). \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re a .500 ball club. Aren\xe2\x80\x99t you going to celebrate?\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Seaver, who could be garrulous when he wanted to be, gave us the Hodges stare.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s so good about .500?\xe2\x80\x9d Seaver snapped. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s only mediocre. We didn\xe2\x80\x99t come into the season to play .500 ball. I\xe2\x80\x99m tired of the jokes about the old Mets.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'And he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t done. Seaver then invoked two fan-and-writer favorites from the first few years of the Mets.'
b'\xe2\x80\x9cLet Rod Kanehl and Marvelous Marv laugh about the Mets. We\xe2\x80\x99re out here to win. You know when we\xe2\x80\x99ll have Champagne? When we win the pennant.\xe2\x80\x9d'
b'Seaver and Hodges seemed to have similar drill instructors in the Marines. Hodges was changing the work habits of the team, backed up by having talented young players at his command. Nobody was exempt from protocol. One day he trudged slowly out to left field and inquired about the health of Cleon Jones, his best hitter, who had pursued a ball a little too slowly for Hodges\xe2\x80\x99s taste. The manager then escorted Jones to the dugout, and fans and writers wondered if he might have lost Jones. Instead, Jones played well down the stretch.'
b'Those of us who were around in 1962 \xe2\x80\x94 The Year of Marvelous Marv \xe2\x80\x94 never thought the Mets, even with those young arms, could ever win anything. But they did win the pennant, and they did spray Champagne.'
b'Just before the World Series, Seaver was asked about the national antiwar protest planned for Oct. 15. He was quoted as asking, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is so unpatriotic about being against such a terrible war?\xe2\x80\x9d He touched off some debates, and stopped commenting on the war, but he had made his point.'
b'The Mets won the World Series in five games, and they sprayed everybody all over again \xe2\x80\x94 Seaver being one of the chief celebrants.'
b'In that stunning autumn, we did not think of Seaver and Hodges as ex-Marines, but now it seems that some of their discipline was part of those Amazing Mets.'
b'George Vecsey wrote about the Mets in the 1960s for Newsday and The New York Times.'

b'Keeping Score'
b'By Benjamin Hoffman'
b'Ronald Acuna Jr. is 21 years, 106 days old. He has played just 115 games at the major league level. He has not hit .300 in a season, nor has he reached 30 career home runs. Yet on Tuesday he and the Atlanta Braves agreed to a contract that locks him up for eight years at a record price for a player his age: $100 million.'
b'And the Braves, as ridiculous as it may sound, almost assuredly got a bargain.'
b'The contract for Acuna, which will carry him through his age-28 season \xe2\x80\x94 and includes team options that could keep him in Atlanta until he\xe2\x80\x99s 30 \xe2\x80\x94 continued a trend this spring in which players, seemingly wary of a slowing free agent market, are signing extensions with their teams earlier than usual. For the players, it can lock in a large payday at a relatively young age but postpone their entry into free agency, a process that for decades had been considered the best way to maximize career earnings.'
b'Mike Trout was the most prominent player to sign a huge extension this off-season, agreeing to a $360 million extension with the Angels two years before he was set to become a free agent. Nolan Arenado, who was supposed to be the jewel of next year\xe2\x80\x99s free-agent class, signed a $234 million extension with Colorado instead. Chris Sale got $145 million from Boston; Jacob deGrom got $137.5 million from the Mets; Paul Goldschmidt got $130 million from St. Louis; and Alex Bregman got $100 million from Houston.'
b'And those were just the nine-figure deals.'
b'The extension spree may seem like baseball lavishing its players with cash, but for clubs wary of enormous free-agent deals like the ones signed by Bryce Harper and Manny Machado before this season, it is a way to save money in the long run: lock players up early and they won\xe2\x80\x99t reach free agency at an age in which they can maximize their payday. And those players, unwilling to endure years of lower pay and the process that Harper and Machado went through, are agreeing to take less overall money in exchange for a dose of security.'
b'That seems especially relevant in the case of Acuna, an outfielder who has already done so much at such a young age that two of the players he can most easily be compared to are Harper and Machado.'
b'Last year in his Rookie of the Year Award-winning campaign, Acuna accounted for 4.1 wins above replacement as a 20-year-old despite playing in just 111 games. Going all the way back to John McGraw in 1893, only 24 position players age 20 or younger have recorded a season with a WAR figure of at least 4.0, according to Baseball Reference. That group includes 12 Hall of Famers, three players (Trout, Harper and Machado) on a Hall of Fame track, and Alex Rodriguez, a player who will have a complicated Hall of Fame case when he becomes eligible, but who certainly put up Hall of Fame numbers.'
b'Of the members of that group, only Claudell Washington, an outfielder for several teams in the 1970s and 1980s, and Dick Hoblitzell, a first baseman for Cincinnati and Boston in the early 20th century, failed to live up to their early promise. And there\xe2\x80\x99s Jason Heyward, who despite having been sensational from age 20 to 25, fell off in the latter half of his 20s.'
b'But even accounting for the limitations of Washington, Hoblitzell and the mixed results of Heyward, it remains a relatively solid decision to gamble on players who are stars at age 20 \xe2\x80\x94 especially considering the potential savings for the Braves.'
b'Had Acuna eschewed an extension for a more traditional path, he most likely would have spent this season and the next one earning at or near the major league minimum of $555,000. In 2021, he would have entered baseball\xe2\x80\x99s arbitration process, where he most likely could have commanded more than $10 million in his first year, $20 million or more in his second year, and $25 million or more in his third year. He would have been a free agent at 26, presumably setting himself up for a deal worth $30 million or more per year.'
b'So, realistically, Acuna might have cost himself more than $15 million over the lifetime of this deal, and another $25 million or more considering the two $17 million team options that the team can exercise at the end of the eight years. He is also unlikely to hit the open market before turning 30, an age at which players become much less likely to receive lengthy, high-dollar contracts.'
b'There are plenty of reasons to celebrate this deal for both Acuna and the Braves: A player who has been with the organization since he was 16 gets to be paid now instead of waiting for a payday down the road, and Atlanta has secured the face of its franchise through much of that player\xe2\x80\x99s prime. But for the Braves, the upshot includes a healthy bit of savings in the long run.'
